Makkot



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>How are witnesses rendered conspiring</b> witnesses? This applies in a case where two witnesses came before the court and said: <b>We testify with regard to so-and-so,</b> who is a priest, <b>that he is the son of a divorced woman or the son of a <i>ḥalutza</i>,</b> a <i>yevama</i> who performed the rite of <i>ḥalitza</i> to free herself from the levirate bond. Those testimonies render him a <i>ḥalal</i> (see Leviticus 21:6–7), one disqualified from the priesthood due to flawed lineage. If a second set of witnesses testifies in court and renders the first set conspiring witnesses, <b>one does not say</b> with regard to each of the conspiring witnesses: <b>This</b> witness <b>shall be rendered the son of a divorced woman or the son of a <i>ḥalutza</i> in his stead. Rather, he receives forty lashes</b> as punishment for his false testimony.
Likewise, in a case where two witnesses came before the court and said: <b>We testify with regard to so-and-so that he is liable to be exiled</b> to a city of refuge for unwittingly killing another (see Numbers 35:11), and a second set of witnesses testifies in court and renders the first set conspiring witnesses, <b>one does not say</b> with regard to each of the conspiring witnesses: <b>This</b> witness <b>shall be exiled in his stead. Rather, he receives forty lashes.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara analyzes the opening question of the mishna: <b>But</b> based on the cases discussed in the mishna, the <i>tanna</i> <b>should have</b> asked: <b>How are witnesses not rendered conspiring</b> witnesses? The standard punishment for conspiring witnesses is the punishment that they conspired to have inflicted upon the subject of their testimony. The mishna cites anomalous cases where their punishment does not correspond to the punishment they sought to have inflicted. The Gemara asks: <b>And furthermore, from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> in a mishna cited <b>later</b> (5a): <b>But</b> if the second set of witnesses attempting to render the first set conspiring witnesses <b>said to them: How</b> can <b>you testify</b> to that incident when <b>on that day you were with us in such and such place, these</b> first witnesses <b>are conspiring</b> witnesses. One learns <b>by inference</b> from the final phrase in the cited passage: These are conspiring witnesses, that <b>those</b> enumerated in the mishna here <b>are not conspiring</b> witnesses.
The Gemara answers both questions: <b>The <i>tanna</i> is standing there</b> in his studies, at the end of tractate <i>Sanhedrin</i>, which immediately precedes <i>Makkot</i>, and <i>Makkot</i> is often appended to the end of <i>Sanhedrin</i>. The mishna there teaches (89a): <b>All</b> those <b>who are</b> rendered <b>conspiring witnesses are led to</b> be executed with <b>the same</b> mode of <b>execution</b> with which they conspired to have their victim executed, <b>except for conspiring</b> witnesses who testified that <b>the daughter of a priest and her paramour</b> committed adultery, where the daughter of the priest would be executed by burning (see Leviticus 21:9) and her paramour would be executed by strangulation. In <b>that</b> case, they are <b>not taken directly to</b> be executed with <b>the same</b> mode of <b>execution</b> that they sought to have inflicted on the woman; <b>rather,</b> they are executed with <b>a different</b> mode of <b>execution,</b> the one they sought to have inflicted on the paramour.
Therefore, the <i>tanna</i> continues in this first mishna in <i>Makkot</i>: <b>And there are other conspiring witnesses with regard to whom</b> the court <b>does not apply the <i>halakhot</i></b> governing the punishment in standard cases of <b>conspiring</b> testimony <b>at all,</b> and they do not receive the punishment they sought to have inflicted. <b>Rather,</b> they receive <b>forty lashes. How,</b> and in what cases, is this applied? This is applied in a case where two witnesses came before the court and said: <b>We testify with regard to so-and-so that he is the son of a divorced woman or the son of a <i>ḥalutza</i>, one does not say</b> with regard to each of the conspiring witnesses: <b>This</b> witness <b>shall be rendered the son of a divorced woman or the son of a <i>ḥalutza</i> in his stead. Rather, he receives forty lashes.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>From where is this matter</b> derived that the court does not punish the witnesses with the punishment they sought to have inflicted and disqualify them from the priesthood? <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi says</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish says:</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as the verse states: “And you shall do to him as he conspired”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:19), from which the Gemara infers: It is done <b>“to him,” but not to his offspring.</b> Rendering the witness a <i>ḥalal</i> would disqualify his offspring as well. The Gemara challenges: <b>Let</b> the court <b>invalidate</b> the witness <b>and not invalidate his offspring.</b> The Gemara explains: That too would not accord with the directive in the verse, as based on the verse <b>we require</b> that the punishment be <b>“as he conspired to do”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:19), <b>and</b> that is <b>not</b> the case here, as the witness conspired to disqualify the subject of his testimony and his offspring.
<b>Bar Padda says</b> that this alternative form of punishment is derived through <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>If one who</b> actually <b>disqualifies</b> another from the priesthood, i.e., a priest who fathers a son with a divorcée disqualifies their son from the priesthood, <b>is not</b> himself <b>disqualified</b> from the priesthood, so too with regard to this witness <b>who came to disqualify</b> another from the priesthood but was unsuccessful <b>and did not disqualify</b> him because he was rendered a conspiring witness, <b>isn’t it logical that he should not be disqualified? Ravina objects to this</b> reasoning: <b>If so,</b> that the failure of the conspiring witnesses to achieve their objective is the consideration at the basis of the <i>a fortiori</i> inference, <b>you have</b> thereby <b>rendered the <i>halakha</i> of conspiring witnesses obsolete,</b> as one could claim:

Daf 2b

<b>If one who stones</b> another, i.e., who testified that another is liable to be executed via the death penalty of stoning and was rendered a conspiring witness after that person was executed, <b>is not stoned,</b> as the <i>halakha</i> is that conspiring witnesses receive the punishment that they conspired to have inflicted and not the punishment that they actually had inflicted, then with regard to a conspiring witness <b>who came to stone</b> another <b>and</b> was unsuccessful <b>and did not stone</b> him, as he was rendered a conspiring witness before that person was executed, <b>isn’t it logical that he should not be stoned?</b> The Gemara concludes: <b>Rather, it is clear as we answered initially:</b> “And you shall do to him as he conspired”; this should be done to him, but not to his offspring.
§ The mishna teaches that in a case where two witnesses came before the court and said: <b>We testify with regard to so-and-so that he is liable to</b> be punished with <b>exile,</b> one does not say that these witnesses shall be exiled in his stead; rather, they receive forty lashes. The Gemara asks: <b>From where is this matter</b> derived? <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as the verse states</b> with regard to an unwitting killer: <b>“And he shall flee to one of the cities”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:5), and the Gemara infers: <b>He</b> shall flee, <b>but conspiring</b> witnesses shall <b>not.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> It is derived through <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>If</b> the killer, <b>who performed an action</b> that, had he done so <b>intentionally, he would not be exiled</b> for it even if he were not sentenced to death, e.g., because there was no forewarning, then in the case of the conspiring witnesses, <b>who did not perform an action,</b> as their conspiracy was exposed and their testimony rejected, even if they testified <b>intentionally, isn’t it logical that they should not be exiled?</b>
The Gemara challenges: <b>But that</b> distinction <b>provides</b> support to the contrary. <b>Could this not</b> be derived through <b>logical inference?</b> With regard to <b>one who performed an action,</b> i.e., killed a person, if he did so <b>intentionally, let</b> him <b>not be exiled so that he will not have atonement</b> for his action, and he will instead receive harsh punishment at the hand of Heaven. With regard to the conspiring witnesses, <b>who did not perform an action,</b> even if they testified <b>intentionally, let them also be exiled so that they will have atonement</b> for their misdeed. The Gemara concludes: <b>Rather, it is clear in accordance with</b> the explanation <b>of Reish Lakish:</b> “And he shall flee to one of the cities”; he, but not conspiring witnesses.
§ <b>Ulla says: From where</b> is <b>an allusion in the Torah to conspiring witnesses</b> derived? The Gemara asks: Is <b>an allusion to conspiring witnesses</b> required? <b>But isn’t it written</b> explicitly: <b>“And you shall do to him as he conspired”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:19)? <b>Rather,</b> Ulla’s question is: <b>From where</b> is <b>an allusion in the Torah to</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>that conspiring witnesses are flogged</b> in certain cases derived? It is derived from that <b>which is written:</b> “If there is a quarrel between people and they come to judgment, and the judges judge them, <b>and they vindicated the righteous and condemned the wicked, and it shall be if the wicked is deserving of lashes”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:1–2). Ostensibly, this verse is difficult: Is it <b>due to</b> the fact that <b>“they vindicated the righteous”</b> that they <b>“condemned the wicked, and it shall be if the wicked is deserving of lashes”?</b> In most disputes, the fact that one party is vindicated does not necessarily lead to lashes for the other party.
<b>Rather,</b> the verse is addressing the case of <b>witnesses who,</b> through their testimony, <b>condemned the righteous, and other witnesses came and vindicated the original righteous</b> person <b>and rendered these</b> first set of witnesses <b>wicked</b> conspiring witnesses. In that case, the verse states: <b>“And it shall be if the wicked is deserving of lashes,”</b> indicating that lashes are an appropriate punishment for conspiring witnesses.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> why not <b>derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> that conspiring witnesses are liable to receive lashes <b>from</b> the prohibition: <b>“You shall not bear</b> false witness against your neighbor” (Exodus 20:13)? The Gemara answers: One is not flogged for violating that prohibition, <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that it is a prohibition that does not involve an action,</b> as one violates it through speech, not action, <b>and</b> the principle is: For <b>every prohibition that does not involve an action, one is not flogged for its</b> violation. Therefore, it was necessary to derive the <i>halakha</i> from the verse: “And they vindicated the righteous.”
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Four matters were stated with regard to conspiring witnesses,</b> i.e., there are four cases in which their punishment deviates from the norm. <b>They are not rendered the son of a divorced woman or the son of a <i>ḥalutza</i>; they are not exiled to a city of refuge; they do not pay the ransom</b> if they testified that the forewarned ox of someone killed another; <b>and they are not sold as a Hebrew slave</b> in a case where they testified that one stole property and he would be sold into slavery if he lacked the means to repay the owner. The Sages <b>said in the name of Rabbi Akiva: They also do not pay based on their own</b> admission. If they were rendered conspiring witnesses in one court, and before that court managed to collect the payment that they owed, they appeared in a different court and admitted that they had been rendered conspiring witnesses, they are exempt from payment.
The Gemara elaborates: <b>They are not rendered the son of a divorced woman or the son of a <i>ḥalutza</i>, as we stated</b> and explained earlier. <b>And they are not exiled to a city of refuge, as we stated</b> and explained earlier. <b>And they do not pay the ransom,</b> as these Sages <b>hold</b> that <b>the ransom</b> paid by one whose ox killed another is <b>atonement</b> for him, as he is liable for the actions of his animal, and it is not payment of damages. <b>And these</b> conspiring witnesses <b>are not subject to</b> a need for <b>atonement,</b> as their ox did not kill anyone.
The Gemara asks: <b>Who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> that <b>the ransom</b> is <b>atonement? Rav Ḥisda says: It is Rabbi Yishmael, son of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Beroka, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse concerning a case where the ox of one person gored another: “If ransom is imposed upon him, <b>and he shall give the redemption of his soul”</b> (Exodus 21:30); the term “his soul” means <b>the value of the victim,</b> i.e., the owner of the ox must pay the heirs of the deceased his value as it would be assessed were he sold as a slave in the market. <b>Rabbi Yishmael, son of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Beroka, says:</b> The term “his soul” means <b>the value of the one liable for the damage. What, is it not</b> that <b>they disagree with regard to this</b> principle: <b>That</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds</b> that <b>the ransom</b> is <b>monetary restitution</b> for the damage that his ox caused the victim; <b>and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yishmael, <b>holds</b> that <b>the ransom</b> is <b>atonement,</b> as he thereby redeems his own soul from death at the hand of Heaven?
<b>Rav Pappa says: No,</b> perhaps <b>everyone agrees</b> that <b>the ransom</b> is <b>atonement, and here,</b> it is <b>with regard to this</b> matter that <b>they disagree:</b> One <b>Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds</b> that <b>we assess</b> the payment <b>in</b> terms of the value <b>of</b> the <b>victim; and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yishmael, <b>holds</b> that <b>we assess</b> the payment <b>in</b> terms of the value <b>of</b> the <b>one liable for the damage.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of the Rabbis,</b> who abandon the straightforward meaning of the verse? The Rabbis derive it in the following manner: <b>Imposition is stated below</b> with regard to one whose ox killed another: “If ransom is imposed upon him” (Exodus 21:30), <b>and imposition is stated above,</b> in the case of one who struck a pregnant woman, causing her to miscarry: “He shall be punished, as the husband of the woman imposes upon him” (Exodus 21:22). <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to the miscarriage, it is <b>in</b> terms of the value <b>of</b> the <b>victim,</b> the fetus, that we assess the payment, <b>here too,</b> in the case of the ransom, it is <b>in</b> terms of the value <b>of</b> the <b>victim</b> that we assess the payment.
<b>And Rabbi Yishmael,</b> son of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Beroka, says: <b>“And he shall give the redemption of his soul” is written,</b> indicating that the sum is based on the value of the one seeking atonement. <b>And the Rabbis</b> explain: <b>Yes,</b> the phrase <b>“redemption of his soul” is written,</b> and the payment functions to redeem his soul. <b>Nevertheless, when we assess</b> the sum of the redemption payment, it is <b>in</b> terms of the value <b>of</b> the <b>victim</b> that <b>we assess</b> the payment.
§ The <i>baraita</i> teaches: Conspiring witnesses <b>are not sold as a Hebrew slave</b> in a case where they testified that one stole property. <b>Rav Hamnuna thought to say: This statement</b> applies only in a case where the falsely accused <b>has</b> means <b>of his own</b> to repay the sum of the alleged theft, <b>as since,</b> had the testimony been true, <b>he would not have been sold</b> for his transgression, the witnesses too are <b>not sold</b> when they are rendered conspiring witnesses. <b>But</b> in a case <b>where</b> the falsely accused <b>does not have</b> means <b>of his own,</b> and would have been sold into slavery had their testimony been accepted, <b>even if</b> the witnesses <b>have</b> means <b>of their own, they are sold,</b> as they sought to have slavery inflicted upon him.
<b>Rava said to him: And let</b> the witnesses <b>say to</b> the person against whom they testified: <b>If you had</b> money, <b>would you have been sold? We too will not be sold.</b> Since they have the means, they can pay the sum that they owe and not be sold as slaves. <b>Rather,</b> the Gemara proposes an alternative formulation of the statement of Rav Hamnuna. <b>Rav Hamnuna thought to say: This statement</b> applies only in a case <b>where either he,</b> the falsely accused, <b>or they,</b> the witnesses, <b>have</b> the means to pay the sum; <b>but</b> in a case <b>where neither he nor they have</b> the means, the witnesses <b>are sold.</b> In that case, had their testimony stood, the alleged thief would have been sold into slavery, and they too lack the means to pay the sum that they are liable to pay as conspiring witnesses. Therefore, they are sold. <b>Rava said to him:</b> That is not so, as <b>the Merciful One states: “And he shall be sold for his theft”</b> (Exodus 21:30), from which it is inferred: <b>For his theft, but not for his conspiring</b> testimony.
§ It is taught in the <i>baraita</i>: The Sages <b>said in the name of Rabbi Akiva:</b> They also do not pay based on their own admission. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva?</b> He <b>holds</b> that money paid by a convicted conspiring witnesses <b>is a fine [<i>kenasa</i>], and</b> the principle is: <b>One does not pay a fine based on his own</b> admission; one pays a fine only on the basis of testimony. <b>Rabba says: Know</b> that it is a fine, <b>as</b> these witnesses <b>did not perform an action</b> and caused no actual damage, <b>and</b> yet <b>they are executed or pay</b> depending on the nature of their testimony, indicating that it is a fine rather than a monetary restitution. <b>Rav Naḥman says: Know</b> that it is a fine rather than a monetary restitution for damages, <b>as the money</b> they sought to compel him to pay ultimately remains <b>in the possession of the owner</b> against whom they testified, <b>and</b> yet <b>they pay</b> him.

Daf 3a

The Gemara is puzzled by this: <b>What is</b> the meaning of the statement: The money remains in the possession of the owner? It means <b>that they did not perform an action. That is</b> identical to the reasoning <b>of Rabba,</b> and it is not an additional explanation. The Gemara emends the formulation of the presentation of the statement. <b>Say: And likewise Rav Naḥman says:</b> Know that it is so, as the money remains in the possession of the owner. Rav Naḥman is not disagreeing with Rabba; he merely formulates the statement differently.
§ Apropos the statement of Rabbi Akiva in the <i>baraita</i>, <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says: A conspiring witness pays according to his share.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of: <b>Pays according to his share? If we say</b> that it means <b>that this</b> witness <b>pays half</b> of the sum that the set conspired to render another liable to pay with their testimony, <b>and that</b> witness <b>pays</b> the other <b>half, we</b> already <b>learned</b> this in a mishna (5a): When punishing conspiring witnesses, <b>one divides</b> the punishment of <b>money</b> among them, <b>but one does not divide</b> the punishment of <b>lashes</b> among them; each receives the full thirty-nine lashes.
<b>Rather,</b> the Gemara explains that the expression: Pays according to his share, means that in a case <b>where</b> only <b>one of</b> the set <b>was rendered a conspiring</b> witness, the <i>halakha</i> is <b>that he pays his half</b> of the sum. The Gemara asks: <b>And does</b> he <b>pay</b> at all in that case? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>A conspiring witness pays money only when both were rendered conspiring</b> witnesses? If only one was rendered a conspiring witness, he does not pay.
<b>Rava says:</b> The statement is not referring to a case of conspiring witnesses; rather, Rav’s statement applies <b>to</b> the case of one who <b>says:</b> It was <b>false testimony</b> that <b>I testified.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Is it in his</b> power to have this statement accepted by the court? That runs counter to the principle: <b>Once</b> a witness <b>stated</b> his testimony <b>he may not then state</b> a revision of that testimony.
The Gemara answers: <b>Rather,</b> Rav’s statement applies <b>to</b> the case of one who <b>says: We testified and were rendered conspiring</b> witnesses <b>in such and such a court.</b> Since he was already convicted as a conspiring witness, it is as though he is admitting an obligation to give monetary restitution, and he is therefore liable to pay his share. The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this <i>halakha</i>? It is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, since if</b> it were <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, doesn’t he say:</b> A conspiring witness <b>also does not pay based on his own</b> admission?
The Gemara answers: <b>Rather,</b> Rav’s statement applies <b>to</b> the case of one who <b>says: We testified and were rendered conspiring</b> witnesses <b>in such and such a court and we were rendered liable</b> to pay a specific sum of <b>money.</b> In that scenario, Rabbi Akiva would concede that one pays on the basis of his own admission. Once the court rendered him liable to pay a specific sum, his admission is not that he is liable to pay a fine, in which case he would be exempt. Once the court actually instructed them to pay, the debt has the status of any other monetary restitution, and one does pay monetary restitution based on his own admission.
The Gemara explains the novel element in Rav’s statement: It might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>since he is unable to render his fellow</b> witness <b>liable</b> to pay based on his admission, as only the testimony of two witnesses is capable of doing so, <b>he too is not liable</b> to pay based on that admission. To counter this, Rav <b>teaches us</b> that his admission that he owes money renders him liable him to pay.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of witnesses who said: <b>We testify</b> with regard to <b>a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that he divorced his wife and did not give her</b> payment of <b>her marriage contract,</b> and they were then rendered conspiring witnesses, the question arises with regard to the manner in which the sum of their payment is calculated. It is not possible to render the witnesses liable to pay the entire sum of the marriage contract, as they can claim: <b>But isn’t</b> it so that <b>either today or tomorrow,</b> i.e., at some point in the future, he may divorce his wife or die and <b>ultimately he</b> will be liable <b>to give her</b> payment of <b>her marriage contract?</b> That being the case, the witnesses did not conspire to render him liable to pay a sum that he would otherwise not be liable to pay.
The sum of their payment is calculated as follows: The court <b>assesses how much</b> money another <b>person</b> would be <b>willing to give in</b> order to purchase the rights to <b>this</b> woman’s <b>marriage contract,</b> cognizant of the uncertainty <b>that if she was widowed or divorced</b> the purchaser will receive payment of the marriage contract <b>but if she dies, her husband will inherit</b> from <b>her,</b> and the one who purchased her marriage contract will receive nothing.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna establishes that the payment of the conspiring witnesses is calculated based on the sum that one would be willing to pay for rights to payment of the marriage contract, taking into consideration the uncertainty whether he will ultimately receive that payment. The Gemara asks: <b>How does</b> the court <b>assess</b> that sum? <b>Rav Ḥisda says:</b> One calculates the sum <b>in</b> terms of <b>the husband:</b> How much would one be willing to pay for the husband’s rights to the marriage contract based on the likelihood that his wife will die first and he will inherit from her?
<b>Rav Natan bar Oshaya says:</b> One calculates the sum <b>in</b> terms of <b>the wife:</b> How much would one be willing to pay for the wife’s rights to the marriage contract based on the likelihood that the husband will die first or divorce her?
<b>Rav Pappa says:</b> One calculates the sum <b>in</b> terms of <b>the wife,</b> as Rav Natan bar Oshaya said, <b>and</b> the assessment is done <b>in</b> terms of the sum of <b>her marriage contract.</b> One does not include in the calculation any usufruct property that the wife brought with her into the marriage, the profits of which belong to the husband, despite the fact that as a result of the testimony of the conspiring witnesses the husband would lose his rights to those profits. One calculates the sum only in terms of her marriage contract.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of witnesses who said: <b>We testify with regard to a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that he owes another</b> person <b>one thousand dinars</b> that he borrowed <b>on the condition</b> that he is <b>to give</b> the money back <b>to him from now until thirty days</b> have passed, <b>and</b> the borrower <b>says</b> that he borrowed that sum but it was on the condition that he is to give the money back to him <b>from now until ten years</b> have passed, and they were rendered conspiring witnesses, here too, it is not possible to render the witnesses liable to pay the entire sum.
Rather, the court <b>estimates how much</b> money <b>a person</b> would be <b>willing to give</b> so that he <b>would</b> keep a loan of <b>one thousand dinars in his possession,</b> and one calculates the difference <b>between</b> that sum in a situation where he would be required to <b>give</b> the money back <b>from now until thirty days</b> have passed, <b>and</b> that same sum in a situation where he would be required to <b>give</b> the money back <b>from now until ten years</b> have passed. That difference is the sum that the testimony of the conspiring witnesses sought to have the borrower lose; therefore, it is the sum that they must pay.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Shmuel says:</b> In the case of <b>one who lends</b> money to <b>another for</b> a period of <b>ten years, the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>abrogates</b> the debt (see Deuteronomy 15:1–11) and absolves the borrower of the obligation to repay it.

Daf 3b

<b>And even though now,</b> during the Sabbatical Year, <b>we do not</b> yet <b>read concerning his</b> loan the prohibition: <b>“He shall not exact it</b> of his neighbor and his brother, because the Lord’s release has been proclaimed” (Deuteronomy 15:2), as the time for repayment has not yet arrived, <b>ultimately,</b> when the time for repayment arrives, he will <b>come to</b> a situation where the prohibition <b>“He shall not exact it”</b> applies. Therefore, like all other debts it is abrogated by the Sabbatical Year.
<b>Rav Kahana raises an objection</b> from the mishna: Rather, the court <b>estimates how much</b> money <b>a person</b> would be <b>willing to give</b> so that he <b>would</b> keep a loan of <b>one thousand dinars in his possession,</b> and one calculates the difference <b>between</b> that sum in a situation where he would be required <b>to give</b> the money back <b>from now until thirty days</b> have passed, <b>and</b> that same sum in a situation where he would be required to <b>give</b> the money back <b>from now until ten years</b> have passed. <b>And if you say</b> that <b>the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>abrogates</b> a ten-year loan, the conspiring witnesses <b>need</b> to <b>pay</b> the borrower <b>the entire</b> sum of the loan <b>as well,</b> as their testimony sought to render the borrower liable to pay a debt that by right should be entirely forgiven.
<b>Rava said: What are we dealing with here</b> in our mishna? We are dealing with debts that are not abrogated with the passing of the Sabbatical Year, e.g., <b>in</b> the case of one who <b>lends</b> money <b>on</b> the basis of <b>collateral, or in</b> the case of one who <b>transfers his</b> promissory <b>notes to the court</b> for collection. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 10:2): In the cases of <b>one who lends</b> money <b>on</b> the basis of <b>collateral and one who transfers his</b> promissory <b>notes to the court</b> for collection, the Sabbatical Year <b>does not abrogate</b> those loans. In those cases, the debt would not have been forgiven during the Sabbatical Year. Therefore, the conspiring witnesses are not liable to pay the entire sum.
<b>There are</b> those <b>who state</b> another version of this discussion: <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Shmuel says:</b> In the case of <b>one who lends</b> money to <b>another for</b> a period of <b>ten years, the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>does not abrogate</b> the debt. <b>And even though</b> when the time for repayment arrives he will <b>come to</b> a situation where the prohibition <b>“He shall not exact it”</b> applies, <b>now, in any event,</b> during the Sabbatical Year <b>we do not</b> yet <b>read</b> the prohibition of <b>“He shall not exact it” concerning his</b> loan.
<b>Rav Kahana says: We learn</b> this ruling in the mishna <b>as well:</b> The court <b>estimates how much</b> money <b>a person</b> would be <b>willing to give</b> so that a loan of <b>one thousand dinars will be in his possession,</b> and one calculates the difference <b>between</b> that sum in a situation where he would be required <b>to give</b> the money back <b>from now until thirty days</b> have passed, <b>and</b> that sum in a situation where he would be required <b>to give</b> the money back <b>from now until ten years</b> have passed. <b>And if you say</b> that <b>the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>abrogates</b> a ten-year loan, then the conspiring witnesses <b>should pay</b> the borrower <b>the entire</b> sum of the loan <b>as well,</b> as their testimony sought to render the borrower liable to pay a debt that by right should be entirely forgiven. The fact that they are not required to pay the entire sum proves that a ten-year loan is not abrogated by the Sabbatical Year.
<b>Rava said:</b> No proof may be cited from there, as <b>what are we dealing with here</b> in our mishna? We are dealing with debts that are not abrogated with the passing of the Sabbatical Year, e.g., <b>in</b> the case of one who <b>lends</b> money <b>on</b> the basis of <b>collateral, or in</b> the case of one who <b>transfers his</b> promissory <b>notes to the court</b> for collection. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Shevi’it</i> 10:2): In the cases of <b>one who lends</b> money <b>on</b> the basis of <b>collateral and one who transfers his</b> promissory <b>notes to the court</b> for collection, the Sabbatical Year <b>does not abrogate</b> those loans. In those cases, the debt would not have been forgiven during the Sabbatical Year. Therefore, the conspiring witnesses are not liable to pay the entire sum.
§ <b>And Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Shmuel says</b> with regard to abrogation of debts: In the case of <b>one who says to another</b> the stipulation: I am lending you money <b>on the condition that the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>will not abrogate my</b> debt, even if the borrower agrees to that stipulation, <b>the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>abrogates</b> the debt. The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that <b>Shmuel holds</b> that the lender who proposed that stipulation <b>is</b> one who <b>stipulates counter to that which is written in the Torah, and</b> in the case of <b>anyone who stipulates counter to that which is written in the Torah, his stipulation is voided.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But wasn’t it stated</b> that there is a dispute between Rav and Shmuel concerning this matter? In the case of <b>one who says to another</b> the stipulation: I am selling you this item <b>on the condition that you have no</b> claim of <b>exploitation against me</b> if I charge you more than the item is worth, <b>Rav says:</b> The buyer <b>has</b> a claim of <b>exploitation against him,</b> as one cannot stipulate to waive the <i>halakha</i> of exploitation. <b>And Shmuel says: He has no</b> claim of <b>exploitation against him.</b> Apparently, according to Shmuel, one may stipulate to waive a Torah law in monetary matters, as it is tantamount to waiving his rights to money due him and it is not in effect counter to that which is written in the Torah.
The Gemara answers: <b>Wasn’t it stated with regard to that</b> <i>halakha</i> that <b>Rav Anan said: It was explained to me</b> personally <b>by Shmuel</b> himself that the matter depends on the formulation of the stipulation. If the seller stipulates: <b>On the condition that you have no</b> claim of <b>exploitation against me,</b> then the buyer <b>has no</b> claim of <b>exploitation against him,</b> as it is as though he is waiving his right to money due him. But if the seller stipulates: <b>On the condition that there is no</b> prohibition of <b>exploitation in</b> this transaction, <b>there is</b> a prohibition of <b>exploitation in</b> that transaction.
<b>Here too,</b> if the lender stipulated to the borrower: I am lending you money <b>on the condition that you will not abrogate</b> the debt <b>during the Sabbatical</b> Year, <b>the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>does not abrogate</b> his debt, as the borrower is merely waiving money due him. But if he stipulated: <b>On the condition that the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>will not abrogate my</b> debt, <b>the Sabbatical</b> Year <b>abrogates</b> the debt, as that is a stipulation to nullify the <i>halakha</i> of the Sabbatical Year concerning that loan, and one may not stipulate counter to that which is written in the Torah.
§ Apropos the loan discussed in the mishna, the Gemara cites a Sage who <b>taught: One who lends</b> money to <b>another</b> for <b>an unspecified</b> period <b>is not permitted to demand</b> repayment <b>from him less than thirty days</b> after the loan took place. The Gemara comments: <b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana,</b> who was seated <b>before Rav, thought to say: This statement</b> applies only <b>with regard to a loan with</b> a promissory <b>note, as a person does not typically exert</b> himself <b>to write</b> a promissory <b>note</b> for a loan whose duration is <b>less than thirty days. But</b> with regard to <b>a loan by oral</b> agreement, <b>no,</b> it may be for less than thirty days. <b>Rav said to him: This</b> is what <b>my uncle,</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya, <b>said:</b> This <i>halakha</i> applies to <b>both one who lends with</b> a promissory <b>note and one who lends by oral</b> agreement. If one fails to establish a time for repayment of the loan, he may not demand repayment until thirty days have passed since the loan was given.
The Gemara notes: <b>That is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who lends</b> money to <b>another</b> for <b>an unspecified</b> period <b>is not permitted to demand</b> repayment <b>from him less than thirty days</b> after the loan took place, and that is the case with regard to <b>both one who lends with</b> a promissory <b>note and one who lends by oral</b> agreement.
The Gemara relates: <b>Shmuel said to Rav Mattana: You shall not sit on your feet until you have explained this <i>halakha</i>;</b> explain it immediately. <b>From where is this matter that the Sages stated</b> derived: <b>One who lends</b> money to <b>another</b> for <b>an unspecified</b> period <b>is not permitted to demand</b> repayment <b>from him less than thirty days</b> after the loan took place, and that is the case with regard to <b>both one who lends with</b> a promissory <b>note and one who lends by oral</b> agreement?
Rav Mattana <b>said to him</b> that it is derived from a verse, <b>as it is written: “The seventh year, the Sabbatical Year, approaches”</b> (Deuteronomy 15:9). The question arises: <b>From</b> the fact <b>that it is stated: “The seventh year…approaches,” don’t I know that it is the Sabbatical Year? Rather, why</b> must <b>the verse state: “The Sabbatical Year”?</b> It is <b>to tell you</b> that <b>there is another</b> period <b>that is like the Sabbatical</b> Year; <b>and which</b> period <b>is it? It is</b> the period in the case of <b>one who lends</b> money to <b>another</b> for <b>an unspecified</b> period, <b>who is not permitted to demand</b> repayment <b>from him less than thirty days</b> after the loan took place. During that period, as during the Sabbatical Year, one may not exact payment for his loan. In what sense can that period be characterized as a year? It is <b>as the Master said: Thirty days in a year are considered a year.</b>
§ <b>And Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says: One who</b> unwittingly <b>opens</b> a new <b>neck opening</b> in a shirt <b>on Shabbat</b> by cutting through the fabric and threads that kept it closed is <b>liable</b> to bring <b>a sin-offering.</b> By creating the opening he renders the shirt fit to wear, thereby fashioning a utensil on Shabbat. <b>Rav Kahana objects to this: And what</b> is the difference <b>between this and the stopper of</b> a wine <b>barrel,</b> which the Sages permitted piercing on Shabbat in order to serve wine to guests? There too, by piercing the stopper he fashions a utensil. Rava <b>said to him:</b> The cases are not comparable: In <b>this</b> case, that of the neck opening of a shirt, the material closing the neck hole is considered <b>a connecting</b> element, i.e., it is an organic part of the weave of the fabric; <b>and</b> in <b>that</b> case, that of the stopper of the barrel, the stopper is <b>not</b> considered <b>a connecting</b> element, as its purpose is to be removed from the barrel.
§ <b>And Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> In the case of <b>three <i>log</i></b> of drawn <b>water into which one sixty-fourth of a <i>log</i> [<i>kortov</i>]</b> of <b>wine fell, and the color of</b> the water <b>is like the color of wine, and</b> that liquid <b>fell into a ritual bath</b> containing less than forty <i>se’a</i>, although the Sages ruled that three <i>log</i> of drawn water invalidate a ritual bath, in this case the liquid <b>does not invalidate it,</b> because the halakhic status of the liquid that fell into the ritual bath is that of wine, and wine does not invalidate a ritual bath. <b>Rav Kahana objects to this: And what</b> is the difference <b>between this</b> case of water into which wine fell <b>and</b> the case of <b>dye water, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Mikvaot</i> 7:3): <b>Rabbi Yosei says: Dye water in</b> the amount of <b>three <i>log</i> invalidates a ritual bath?</b> The wine, like the dye, colors the water. <b>Rava said to him:</b> The difference is that <b>there</b> the mixture <b>is called dye water,</b> where the water maintains the status of water; <b>here it is called diluted wine.</b>
The Gemara asks with regard to Rav’s statement: <b>But didn’t Rabbi Ḥiyya teach</b> that three <i>log</i> of water into which a <i>kortov</i> of wine fell <b>reduce the ritual bath</b> into a state where it is no longer valid? <b>Rava said</b> that this <i>baraita</i> is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> statement of Rav is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri,</b> and <b>that</b> <i>baraita</i> taught by Rabbi Ḥiyya is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Mikvaot</i> 7:5): In a case where there are <b>three <i>log</i></b> of drawn <b>water</b>

Daf 4a

<b>less one <i>kortov</i></b> or any small measure of water, <b>into which a <i>kortov</i> of wine fell,</b> increasing the measure of liquid to a total of three <i>log</i>, <b>and</b> the <b>appearance</b> of those three <i>log</i> is <b>like the appearance of wine; and</b> then those three <i>log</i> <b>fell into a ritual bath,</b> completing its requisite forty <i>se’a</i>, <b>it did not invalidate</b> the ritual bath, because three <i>log</i> of drawn water invalidate the ritual bath, and less than that measure of water fell into the ritual bath. <b>And likewise,</b> in a case where there are <b>three <i>log</i></b> of drawn <b>water less one <i>kortov</i> into which a <i>kortov</i> of milk fell and</b> the <b>appearance</b> of those three <i>log</i> is <b>like the appearance of water, and</b> those three <i>log</i> <b>fell into a ritual bath,</b> completing its requisite forty <i>se’a</i>, <b>it did not invalidate</b> the ritual bath, because in this case too, less than three <i>log</i> of drawn water fell into the ritual bath.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri says: Everything follows the appearance</b> of those three <i>log</i>. Therefore, if it has the appearance of wine it does not invalidate the ritual bath; if it has the appearance of water it invalidates the ritual bath. Perhaps Rav Yehuda says that Rav says that the mixture of water and wine does not invalidate the ritual bath because he holds in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri that everything follows the appearance. Rabbi Ḥiyya holds in accordance with the opinion of the first <i>tanna</i> that the amount of drawn water is the decisive factor, and therefore, regardless of its appearance, three <i>log</i> of water invalidates a ritual bath.
The Gemara asks: <b>Wasn’t</b> this matter already <b>raised as a dilemma by Rav Pappa? As Rav Pappa raised a dilemma:</b> There are two ways to explain the mishna. One is that <b>Rav teaches:</b> In a case where there are three <i>log</i> of drawn water <b>less one <i>kortov</i>, in the first clause. But</b> in a case where there are <b>three</b> complete <b><i>log</i></b> of water, <b>according to the first <i>tanna</i>, it invalidates</b> the ritual bath even if its appearance is that of wine. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> ben Nuri <b>comes to say: Everything follows the appearance,</b> meaning that even in that case, if its appearance is that of wine it does not invalidate the ritual bath. <b>And</b> if that is the dispute, <b>Rav states</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri.</b>
<b>Or perhaps Rav did not teach:</b> In a case where there are three <i>log</i> of drawn water <b>less one <i>kortov</i>, in the first clause</b> of the mishna; rather, he taught: In a case where there are three complete <i>log</i> of water into which a <i>kortov</i> of wine fell. According to this version of the mishna, even the first <i>tanna</i> agrees that if its appearance is that of wine it does not invalidate the ritual bath. <b>And when Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri disagrees, it is</b> only <b>with regard to the latter clause that he disagrees,</b> in the case of three <i>log</i> of drawn water less one <i>kortov</i> into which a <i>kortov</i> of milk fell and the appearance of those three <i>log</i> is like the appearance of water. The first <i>tanna</i> holds that in order to invalidate a ritual bath two criteria must be fulfilled: There must be three <i>log</i> of water and its appearance must be that of water. In this case there is less than three <i>log</i> of water. Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri holds that there is only one criterion, appearance. Therefore, in the case of milk, the ritual bath is invalidated.
<b>And</b> according to this latter rendering of the dispute, <b>Rav states</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>everyone.</b> Why then does Rava state: This statement of Rav is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri?
The Gemara answers: This is not difficult, as although <b>for Rav Pappa</b> there <b>is a dilemma</b> how to interpret the mishna, <b>for Rava</b> it is <b>obvious.</b>
Apropos this discussion, the Gemara relates that <b>Rav Yosef said: I did not hear this <i>halakha</i></b> with regard to the dilemma whether the first clause of the mishna includes the phrase: Less one <i>kortov</i>. <b>Abaye said to him: You said it to us,</b> but you forgot due to your illness. <b>And this is</b> what <b>you said to us: Rav did not teach: Less one <i>kortov</i>, in the first</b> clause of the mishna, <b>and Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> ben Nuri <b>disagrees</b> only <b>with regard to the latter clause,</b> and it is <b>Rav who states</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>everyone.</b>
§ <b>And Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> In the case of <b>a barrel full of</b> drawn <b>water that fell into the Mediterranean Sea,</b> concerning <b>one who immerses there</b> in the spot where the water fell, the <b>immersion did not effect his</b> purification, because <b>we are concerned that three <i>log</i></b> of drawn water <b>should not be</b> collected <b>in one place,</b> in the place where he immerses. The Gemara qualifies this <i>halakha</i>: <b>And</b> this applies <b>specifically to the Mediterranean Sea, whose</b> waters <b>are</b> largely <b>stagnant. But</b> in the case of <b>a standard river,</b> whose waters flow, <b>no,</b> this <i>halakha</i> does not apply, as the water from the barrel will immediately intermingle with the river water.
The Gemara notes: <b>This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a barrel full of wine that fell into the Mediterranean Sea,</b> concerning <b>one who immerses there,</b> the <b>immersion did not effect his</b> purification, because <b>we are concerned that three <i>log</i> of drawn</b> wine that is unfit for immersion <b>should not be</b> collected <b>in one place. And likewise, a loaf of <i>teruma</i> that fell there,</b> where the wine fell, <b>is impure</b> through contact with the wine.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is added by the clause introduced with the term: <b>And likewise?</b> If the concern is that the wine remained in one place and did not intermingle with the seawater, ostensibly that concern applies to <i>halakhot</i> of ritual impurity. The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say</b> that <b>there,</b> with regard to immersion, the reason he is impure is that since there is uncertainty whether his immersion effected his purification, the principle is: <b>Establish the person on his presumptive status</b> of impurity. But <b>here,</b> in the case of a loaf of <i>teruma</i>, since there is uncertainty whether it was rendered impure, the principle is: <b>Establish the <i>teruma</i> on its presumptive status</b> of purity. Therefore, the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches us</b> that the concern that the loaf touched the wine is substantial to the extent that it prevails even over the loaf’s presumptive status of purity.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If witnesses said: <b>We testify with regard to a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that he is liable to</b> pay <b>another</b> person <b>two hundred dinars, and they were found</b> to be <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>they are flogged, and they pay</b> the money they sought to render him liable to pay. Why do they receive two punishments? It is due to the fact <b>that the source that brings them to</b> liability to receive <b>lashes</b> is <b>not</b> the source that <b>brings them to</b> liability for <b>payment;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say: Anyone who pays</b> as punishment for a transgression <b>is not flogged</b> for that same transgression.
Likewise, if witnesses said: <b>We testify with regard to a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that he is liable</b> to receive <b>forty lashes, and they were discovered</b> to be <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>they are flogged</b> with <b>eighty</b> lashes; one set of lashes <b>due to</b> violation of the prohibition: <b>“You shall not bear false witness against your neighbor”</b> (Exodus 20:13), <b>and</b> one set of lashes <b>due to</b> the verse: <b>“And you shall do to him as he conspired”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:19), which is the punishment for conspiring witnesses; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say: They are flogged</b> with <b>only forty</b> lashes, due to the verse “And you shall do to him as he conspired.”

Daf 4b

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the initial dispute between Rabbi Meir and the Rabbis in the mishna whether conspiring witnesses pay and are flogged, the Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> according <b>to the Rabbis,</b> the verse that states: “The judge shall cause him to lie down, and to be beaten before him, <b>according to the measure of his wickedness”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), <b>is written</b> concerning one who was liable to receive lashes. From “according to the measure of his wickedness” it is inferred with regard to an individual who commits one transgression: <b>For one evildoing you can render him liable, but you cannot render him liable for two evildoings,</b> i.e., one cannot receive two punishments for the same act. <b>But</b> according to <b>Rabbi Meir, what is the reason</b> that he is punished twice for committing a single transgression?
<b>Ulla said:</b> Rabbi Meir <b>derived</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> concerning <b>one who defames</b> his wife, claiming that when he consummated the marriage he discovered that she was not a virgin: <b>Just as the defamer is flogged and pays,</b> as it is written: “And they shall chastise him and fine him one hundred silver coins” (Deuteronomy 22:18–19), <b>so too, anyone</b> who commits a transgression punishable with lashes and a monetary payment <b>is flogged and pays.</b> The Gemara questions this derivation: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>a defamer?</b> It is notable <b>in that</b> the payment of the defamer is <b>a fine,</b> which is a fixed sum that the Torah deems him liable to pay. How can the <i>halakha</i> of conspiring witnesses, whose payment is monetary restitution, be derived from there? The Gemara answers: Rabbi Meir <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who says:</b> The payment that <b>conspiring witnesses</b> are liable to pay <b>is a fine.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>There are those who teach this</b> statement <b>of Ulla with regard to that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: It is stated with regard to the Paschal offering: <b>“And you shall let nothing of it remain until morning, but that which remains of it until morning</b> you shall burn with fire” (Exodus 12:10). <b>The verse comes to provide a positive mitzva</b> to burn the remains <b>after</b> it has taught <b>a prohibition,</b> which states: “You shall let nothing of it remain,” <b>to say that one is not flogged for its</b> violation; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b> This is a prohibition whose transgression entails the fulfillment of a positive mitzva, in which the mitzva serves to rectify the violation of the prohibition, and no lashes are administered.
<b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> The fact that one is not flogged <b>is not for</b> that <b>reason; rather,</b> it is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> this prohibition: “And you shall let nothing of it remain,” <b>is a prohibition that does not involve an action,</b> as one violates the prohibition through failure to take action, <b>and</b> concerning <b>any prohibition that does not involve an action, one is not flogged for its</b> violation. The Gemara learns <b>by inference that Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> in principle with regard to <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action,</b> that <b>one is flogged for its</b> violation. <b>From where does</b> Rabbi Yehuda derive that one is flogged in that case?
<b>Ulla said:</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>derived</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>from the defamer; just as the defamer</b> violates <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action,</b> as it involves only speech, and <b>one is flogged for its</b> violation, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>any prohibition that does not involve an action, one is flogged for its</b> violation. The Gemara questions this derivation: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of the <b>defamer?</b> It is notable <b>in that he is flogged and pays</b> for violation of a single prohibition. Due to that stringency, other less stringent prohibitions cannot be derived from the case of the defamer.
<b>Rather, Reish Lakish says:</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>derives</b> this principle <b>from</b> the case of <b>conspiring witnesses. Just as the conspiring witnesses</b> violate <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action</b> and an individual <b>is flogged for its</b> violation, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>any prohibition that does not involve an action, one is flogged for its</b> violation. The Gemara questions this derivation: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>conspiring witnesses?</b> It is notable <b>in that</b> the witnesses <b>do not require forewarning</b> in order to administer their punishment, which is an exception to the principle that corporal punishment may be administered only after forewarning. Due to that stringency, other less strin-gent prohibitions cannot be derived from the case of conspiring witnesses.
The Gemara answers: <b>The</b> case of the <b>defamer will prove</b> that the absence of forewarning is not a significant factor, as the defamer requires forewarning and nevertheless is flogged for a prohibition that does not involve an action. <b>And the inference has reverted</b> to its starting point. The defining <b>characteristic of this</b> case <b>is not like</b> the defining <b>characteristic of that</b> case, and the defining <b>characteristic of that</b> case is not like the defining <b>characteristic of this</b> case. <b>Their common denominator</b> is that in both cases there is <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action and one is flogged for its</b> violation. <b>So too,</b> with regard to <b>any prohibition that does not involve an action, one is flogged for its</b> violation.
The Gemara questions this derivation: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about their common denominator?</b> They are notable <b>in that</b> payment in both cases is <b>a fine,</b> and therefore other, less stringent prohibitions cannot be derived from them. The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> is <b>not difficult; Rabbi Yehuda does not hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> that the payment of conspiring witnesses is a fine. In his opinion, therefore, that is not a common denominator.
<b>Rather,</b> the Gemara proposes an alternative refutation: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about their common denominator?</b> They are notable <b>in that</b> the cases of the defamer and of conspiring witnesses <b>both contain a stringent aspect;</b> therefore, other, less stringent prohibitions cannot be derived from them. The stringency in the case of the defamer is that he both is flogged and pays, and the stringency in the case of conspiring witnesses is that they do not require forewarning. The Gemara answers: <b>And Rabbi Yehuda does not refute</b> a derivation from a common denominator based on the fact that both cases contain a different <b>stringent aspect.</b> He holds that the mere fact that there is a stringency in each does not serve as a common denominator.
§ The Gemara resumes its analysis of the dispute between Rabbi Meir and the Rabbis in the case of conspiring witnesses who testify that another is liable to receive lashes. Rabbi Meir holds that they are flogged with eighty lashes, one set of lashes due to violation of the prohibition: “You shall not bear false witness against your neighbor” (Exodus 20:13), and one set of lashes due to the verse: “And you shall do to him as he conspired” (Deuteronomy 19:19). The Rabbis say: They are flogged with only forty lashes, due to the verse “And you shall do to him as he conspired.” The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>the Rabbis,</b> concerning <b>this</b> verse: <b>“You shall not bear false witness against your neighbor,” what do they derive from it,</b> if one is not flogged for its violation?
The Gemara answers: <b>They require that</b> verse <b>as a prohibition against conspiring witnesses.</b> Every punishment enumerated in the Torah, including that of conspiring witnesses, is accompanied by an explicit verse prohibiting the action that results in the punishment. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>Rabbi Meir,</b> who holds that conspiring witnesses are flogged for violating this prohibition, <b>from where does he</b> derive <b>a prohibition for conspiring witnesses? Rabbi Yirmeya said: He derives it from</b> the verse written in the context of conspiring witnesses: <b>“And those who remain shall hear and fear, and shall not continue</b> to perform <b>any more</b> evil of this kind in your midst” (Deuteronomy 19:20). The verse warns that conspiring witnesses should not continue with their sinful conduct.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> concerning <b>the Rabbis,</b> what do they derive from that verse? The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> verse <b>is necessary</b> according to the Rabbis

Daf 5a

<b>for</b> the requirement of <b>proclamation.</b> When the witnesses are taken to their execution, there is a mitzva for the court to publicly proclaim the transgression for which they are being punished, in order to deter others from committing the same transgression. <b>And Rabbi Meir derives</b> the requirement of <b>proclamation from</b> the phrase in that verse: <b>“Shall hear and fear.”</b> The prohibition is derived from the phrase “and shall not continue to perform any more evil.”
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> When punishing conspiring witnesses based on the verse: “As he conspired to do to his brother” (Deuteronomy 19:19), <b>one divides</b> the punishment <b>of money</b> among them, <b>but one does not divide</b> the punishment <b>of lashes</b> among them; each receives thirty-nine lashes. The mishna elaborates: <b>How so?</b> If the witnesses <b>testified about</b> someone <b>that he owes another</b> person <b>two hundred dinars and they were</b> then <b>found</b> to be <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, the witnesses <b>divide</b> the sum <b>among themselves</b> and pay a total of two hundred dinars. <b>But if they testified about</b> someone <b>that he was liable</b> to receive <b>forty lashes and they were</b> then <b>found</b> to be <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>each and every one</b> of the witnesses <b>receives forty lashes.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters,</b> that the witnesses do not divide the punishment of lashes among themselves, derived? <b>Abaye said: “Wicked”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), <b>is stated with regard to those liable</b> to receive <b>lashes, and: “Wicked”</b> (Numbers 35:31), <b>is stated with regard to those liable</b> to receive <b>a court</b>-imposed <b>death</b> penalty. <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to those sentenced to be executed, <b>there is no partial death</b> penalty, <b>so too here,</b> with regard to those liable to receive lashes, <b>there is no partial</b> administering of <b>lashes.</b>
<b>Rava said:</b> The reason the punishment of lashes is not divided is that <b>we require</b> fulfillment of the verse: <b>“As he conspired to do to his brother”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:19), <b>and</b> were the conspiring witness to receive fewer than thirty-nine lashes, the verse would <b>not</b> be fulfilled. The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> in the case of <b>money too,</b> one should not divide the sum between them, as each sought to cause the defendant loss of the entire sum. The Gemara answers: Sums of <b>money</b> paid by the witnesses can <b>combine,</b> as the person against whom they testified receives the entire sum that they sought to cause him to lose, but <b>lashes</b> administered to the witnesses <b>cannot combine.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Witnesses are not rendered conspiring</b> witnesses <b>until</b> the witnesses who come to render them conspiring <b>impeach</b> the witnesses <b>themselves</b> and not merely their testimony.
<b>How so?</b> A set of witnesses <b>said: We testify with regard to a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that he killed a person,</b> and they attested to the precise time and place that the murder took place. Then, a second set of witnesses came to court and <b>said to them: How can you testify</b> about this event? <b>This</b> person who <b>was killed, or this</b> person <b>who killed, was with us,</b> i.e., with the second set of witnesses, on <b>that day in such and such place,</b> which is not the location identified by the first set of witnesses. In that case, although the second set of witnesses contradicted the testimony of the first set, <b>these</b> first witnesses are <b>not</b> rendered <b>conspiring</b> witnesses. <b>But</b> if the second set of witnesses came to court and <b>said to them: How can you testify</b> about that event? <b>You were with us</b> on <b>that day in such and such place.</b> In this case, <b>these</b> first witnesses are rendered <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>and are executed on the basis of their,</b> i.e., the second set’s, testimony.
If <b>other</b> witnesses, i.e., a third set, <b>came</b> and corroborated the testimony of the first set of witnesses, and the second set of witnesses testified that this third set of witnesses were also with them elsewhere that day <b>and rendered them conspiring</b> witnesses, and similarly, if yet <b>other</b> witnesses, i.e., a fourth set, <b>came</b> and corroborated the testimony of the first set of witnesses <b>and</b> the second set <b>rendered them conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>even</b> if <b>one hundred</b> sets of witnesses were all rendered conspiring witnesses by the same second set of witnesses, <b>all of them are executed</b> on the basis of their testimony, as the authority of two witnesses is equivalent to the authority of numerous witnesses. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: This</b> situation where a set of witnesses renders all the others conspiring witnesses <b>is a conspiracy [<i>istatit</i>],</b> as there is room for suspicion that they simply decided to impeach all witnesses who offer that testimony, <b>and</b> it is <b>only the first set alone</b> that is <b>executed.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the <i>halakha</i> that witnesses are rendered conspiring witnesses only if the second set testifies that the first set was with them elsewhere at that time, and not if they directly contradict the testimony of the first set, the Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? <b>Rav Adda said:</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as the verse states</b> with regard to conspiring witnesses: <b>“And the witness is a false witness; he testified falsely</b> against his brother” (Deuteronomy 19:18), indicating that they are not rendered conspiring witnesses <b>until the body of the testimony is rendered false,</b> i.e., the testimony with regard to the actual witnesses was proven wrong, as they were not there at the time of the event in question.
<b>The school of Rabbi Yishmael taught</b> a different source for this <i>halakha</i>. It is stated in that passage: “If an unrighteous witness rise up against any man <b>to bear perverted witness against him”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:16), indicating that the witnesses are not rendered conspiring witnesses <b>until the body of the testimony is rendered perverted,</b> but not through contradiction of any aspect of the testimony.
§ <b>Rava says: Two</b> witnesses <b>came and said: So-and-so killed a person to the east of a building [<i>bira</i>], and two</b> other witnesses <b>came</b> to court <b>and said</b> to the first set: <b>But were you not with us to the west of the building</b> at that time? How can you testify to an incident that transpired on the other side of the building? <b>We see: If, when</b> people <b>are standing to the west of the building they see to the east of the building, these</b> witnesses <b>are not conspiring</b> witnesses. <b>But if</b> it is <b>not</b> possible to see from one side of the building to the other, <b>these</b> witnesses <b>are conspiring</b> witnesses.
The Gemara asks: Isn’t that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say: Let us be concerned about</b> the possibility that these witnesses have particularly <b>good eyesight</b> and were able to see that far despite a typical person’s being unable to see that far. Therefore, Rava <b>teaches us</b> that one does not take that possibility into account.
<b>And Rava says:</b> If <b>two</b> witnesses <b>came and said: So-and-so killed a person in Sura in the morning on Sunday, and two</b> other witnesses <b>came</b> to court <b>and said</b> to the first set: <b>In the evening on Sunday you were with us in Neharde’a, we see: If</b> one <b>is able to travel from Sura to Neharde’a from morning until evening they are not conspiring</b> witnesses, as conceivably they could have witnessed the murder in Sura and traveled to Neharde’a by evening. <b>And if</b> it is <b>not</b> possible to travel that distance in that period of time, <b>they are conspiring</b> witnesses.
The Gemara asks: Isn’t that <b>obvious?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Lest you say: Let us be concerned about</b> the possibility that these witnesses traveled on <b>a flying camel,</b> i.e., one that runs so quickly that it enabled them to traverse the distance faster than the typical person. Therefore, Rava <b>teaches us</b> that one need not take that possibility into account.
<b>And Rava says:</b> If <b>two</b> witnesses <b>came and said: On Sunday so-and-so killed a person, and two</b> other witnesses <b>came and said: You were with us on Sunday, but on Monday</b> that same <b>so-and-so killed</b> that same <b>person. Moreover, even</b> if the second set of witnesses <b>said:</b> On <b>Shabbat eve so-and-so killed</b> that same <b>person,</b> the first set of witnesses <b>is executed,</b> despite the fact that the person against whom they testified was liable to be executed without their testimony. The reason they are liable is <b>that at the time that they testified,</b> conspiring to have him executed by the court, <b>the man</b> was <b>not</b> yet <b>liable</b> for <b>execution</b> by the court.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> Rava <b>teaching us? We learn</b> in a mishna (6b) with regard to two sets of witnesses who testified that one person killed another: <b>Therefore,</b> if <b>one of</b> the sets of witnesses <b>was found</b> to be a set of <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>he,</b> the accused, <b>and they,</b> the conspiring witnesses, <b>are executed</b> by the court, <b>and the second</b> set of witnesses <b>is exempt.</b> The accused is executed because the testimony of the witnesses who were not rendered conspiring witnesses remains unchallenged. The first set of witnesses is executed because they were rendered conspiring witnesses. Clearly, conspiring witnesses may be executed even though they testified against a guilty person who was sentenced to death, provided that their testimony was delivered before he was sentenced.
The Gemara answers: It was <b>the latter clause</b> of this <i>halakha</i>, teaching <b>that it is not so with regard to</b> the <b>verdict,</b> that contains a novel element, and therefore it <b>was necessary for</b> Rava to teach it, and in the context of teaching the latter <i>halakha</i> he taught the first case as well. The latter clause is: If <b>two</b> witnesses <b>came</b> on Tuesday <b>and said: On Sunday so-and-so was sentenced</b> to death in a certain court, <b>and two</b> other witnesses <b>came and said: On Sunday you were with us</b> elsewhere and you could not have witnessed the verdict in that court, <b>but on Shabbat eve</b> the same <b>so-and-so was sentenced</b> to death, the first set of witnesses are not executed. <b>Moreover, even</b> if the second set of witnesses <b>said: On Monday</b> the same <b>so-and-so was sentenced</b> to death, the first set of witnesses <b>are not executed, as at the time that they testified</b> against him, on Tuesday, <b>the man was</b> already <b>liable to be executed,</b> and it is as though they conspired to kill a dead man.
The Gemara adds: <b>And likewise,</b> the same <i>halakha</i> that applies with regard to witnesses who testify about one who is sentenced to death applies <b>to the matter of payments of a fine.</b>
The Gemara explains: If <b>two</b> witnesses <b>came and said: On Sunday</b> this person <b>stole and</b> then <b>slaughtered or sold</b> an ox or a lamb, thereby rendering him liable to pay a fine of four or five times the value of the stolen animal (see Exodus 21:37), <b>and</b> then <b>two</b> other witnesses <b>came and said</b> to the first set of witnesses: <b>On Sunday you were with us</b> elsewhere, <b>but on Monday</b> this same person <b>stole and</b> then <b>slaughtered or sold</b> an ox or a lamb, the first set of witnesses <b>pay</b> the accused four or five times the value of the stolen animal. This is because at the time that according to their testimony he stole the animals, he had not yet stolen. <b>Moreover, even</b> if the second set of witnesses <b>said:</b> It was earlier, <b>on Shabbat eve,</b> that he <b>stole and</b> then <b>slaughtered or sold</b> the animals, the first set of witnesses <b>pay</b> the accused the amount of the fine he would have had to pay, <b>as at the time that they testified the</b> accused <b>man</b> had <b>not</b> yet been found <b>liable for payment</b> by the court, and he could have admitted his guilt and exempted himself from paying the fine. They conspired to render him liable.
But if <b>two</b> witnesses <b>came</b> on Tuesday <b>and said: On Sunday</b> this person <b>stole and</b> then <b>slaughtered or sold</b> an ox or a lamb <b>and was sentenced</b> to pay a fine of four or five times the value of the stolen animal, <b>and two</b> other witnesses <b>came and said: On Sunday you were with us, but</b> that person <b>stole and</b> then <b>slaughtered or sold</b> an ox or a lamb, <b>and was sentenced on Shabbat eve,</b> the first set of witnesses are exempt from payment. <b>Moreover, even</b> if the second set of witnesses <b>said: On Sunday he stole and</b> then <b>slaughtered or sold</b> an ox or a lamb, corroborating the testimony of the first set, <b>and on Monday he was sentenced</b> to pay the fine, the first set of witnesses <b>do not pay</b> the accused. This is <b>because at the time that they testified</b> against him, on Tuesday, <b>the man</b> had already been found <b>liable for payment</b> by the court, and therefore they are not conspiring witnesses.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that if a single set of witnesses rendered numerous sets of witnesses, all of whom testified that one person killed another, conspiring witnesses, <b>this</b> situation <b>is a conspiracy,</b> and only the first set of conspiring witnesses is executed.

Daf 5b

The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> Rabbi Yehuda states that <b>this</b> situation <b>is a conspiracy,</b> and there is suspicion that the second set is not truthful, let <b>even</b> the <b>first set</b> of witnesses who were rendered conspiring witnesses based on their testimony <b>also not</b> be executed. <b>Rabbi Abbahu said:</b> The mishna is referring to a case <b>where</b> the judges <b>already executed</b> the first set of witnesses. Rabbi Yehuda is saying that no witnesses are executed other than the first set of witnesses, who were already executed.
The Gemara challenges: If so, <b>what was,</b> already <b>was;</b> there is no point in stating it as a <i>halakha</i>. <b>Rather, Rava said this</b> is what Rabbi Yehuda <b>is saying: If it is only one set</b> of witnesses that is rendered conspiring witnesses by the second set, the witnesses <b>are executed; if there is more</b> than one set <b>they are not executed</b> at all. The Gemara asks: <b>But doesn’t</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>say:</b> It is only the first set <b>alone</b> that is executed? This indicates that contrary to Rava’s explanation, it is a case involving more than one set of witnesses. The Gemara notes: Indeed, this matter is <b>difficult.</b>
Apropos the dispute in the mishna, the Gemara relates: There was <b>a certain woman who brought witnesses</b> to testify on her behalf, <b>and they were</b> proven to be <b>liars. She brought</b> other <b>witnesses, and they</b> too <b>were</b> proven to be <b>liars. She went</b> and <b>brought</b> yet <b>other witnesses, who were not</b> proven to be <b>liars.</b> There is an amoraic dispute whether the testimony of the third set of witnesses is accepted. <b>Reish Lakish said: This</b> woman <b>has assumed the presumptive status</b> of dishonesty because of her repeated reliance on false witnesses; therefore, the testimony of the third set is rejected. <b>Rabbi Elazar said to him: If she has assumed the presumptive status</b> of dishonesty, <b>has the entire Jewish people assumed</b> that <b>presumptive status?</b> Why assume that these witnesses are dishonest?
The Gemara relates: On another <b>occasion,</b> Reish Lakish and Rabbi Elazar <b>were sitting before Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> and <b>an incident similar to this</b> one <b>came before them</b> for judgment. <b>Reish Lakish said: This</b> woman <b>has assumed the presumptive status</b> of dishonesty. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said to him: If she has assumed the presumptive status</b> of dishonesty, <b>has the entire Jewish people assumed</b> that <b>presumptive status?</b> When Reish Lakish heard Rabbi Yoḥanan respond in a manner identical to the earlier response of Rabbi Elazar, <b>he turned</b> his head and <b>glared angrily at Rabbi Elazar,</b> and <b>he said to him: You heard</b> this <b>matter from bar Nappaḥa,</b> i.e., Rabbi Yoḥanan, <b>and you did not say</b> it <b>to me in his name?</b> Had I known that you were stating Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion I would have accepted it.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that <b>Reish Lakish stated</b> his opinion that this woman has assumed the presumptive status of dishonesty <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who invalidates the testimony of witnesses based on suspicion that arises due to the circumstances even though there is no proof that they lied. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan stated</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who do not invalidate testimony based on unsubstantiated suspicion.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>Reish Lakish</b> could <b>say to you: I state</b> my opinion <b>even in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> as <b>the Rabbis say</b> that one relies on witnesses who render multiple sets of witnesses conspiring witnesses <b>only there,</b> in the mishna, in a case <b>where there is no one who is seeking</b> to hire witnesses to testify on his behalf, and one could assert that their testimony is true. <b>But here, there is this</b> woman <b>who is seeking</b> to hire witnesses to testify on her behalf, which arouses suspicion that she hired them to lie on her behalf.
<b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could <b>say to you: I state</b> my opinion <b>even in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> as <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that the testimony of the second set is invalid based on unsubstantiated suspicion <b>only there,</b> in the mishna, where circumstances exacerbate the suspicion that they are lying, <b>as we say: Is that to say</b> that <b>everyone,</b> the numerous sets of witnesses, <b>was standing near these</b> witnesses who testify in order to render them conspiring witnesses? <b>But here,</b> perhaps <b>these</b> witnesses who came last and were not proven to be liars <b>know</b> the content <b>of the testimony, and these</b> witnesses who were proven to be liars <b>do not know</b> the content <b>of the testimony.</b> The fact that the testimony of the first sets of witnesses was rendered void has no bearing on the status of other witnesses.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The conspiring witnesses are executed only</b> if they are rendered conspiring witnesses after <b>the verdict</b> of the accused <b>is concluded.</b> This is in contrast to the opinion of the Sadducees, <b>as the Sadducees say:</b> Conspiring witnesses are executed <b>only</b> if they are rendered conspiring witnesses after the accused <b>is killed</b> on the basis of their testimony, <b>as it is stated: “A life for a life”</b> (Exodus 21:23; see Deuteronomy 19:21).
<b>The Rabbis said to</b> the Sadducees: <b>But wasn’t it already stated: “And you shall do to him as he conspired to do to his brother”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:19), <b>and</b> this latter verse indicates that <b>his</b> accused <b>brother is alive? And if so, why is it stated: “A life for a life”?</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that if they are rendered conspiring witnesses <b>from the moment</b> the judges <b>accepted their testimony</b> in court, <b>they will be executed,</b> even though no verdict was concluded. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “A life for a life,”</b> teaching that <b>they are executed only</b> if they are rendered conspiring witnesses after <b>the verdict</b> of the accused <b>will be concluded,</b> from the moment that the court is on the verge of taking his life.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> It is <b>taught</b> with regard to the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna that a Sage referred to as <b>the Distinguished</b> [<b><i>Beribbi</i></b>] <b>says:</b> If the conspiring witnesses have <b>not</b> yet <b>killed</b> the accused with their testimony <b>they are executed,</b> but if <b>they killed</b> the accused with their testimony <b>they are not executed. The father of</b> that Sage, who was also a prominent Sage, <b>said</b> to him: <b>My son, is</b> this matter <b>not</b> derived through <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference? If, when they were unsuccessful in their attempt to kill the accused they are executed, all the more so if they were successful in killing him should they be executed.
<b>He said to</b> his father: <b>You have taught us, our teacher, that one does not administer punishment</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b> The punishment must be stated in the Torah. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that among the relatives with whom engaging in intercourse is forbidden it states: <b>“A man who takes his sister, the daughter of his father or the daughter of his mother”</b> (Leviticus 20:17): <b>I have</b> derived <b>only the daughter of his father who is not the daughter of his mother, or the daughter of his mother who is not the daughter of his father. From where</b> is it derived that one is liable for engaging in intercourse with his sister who is both <b>the daughter of his mother and the daughter of his father?</b> It is derived from a verse, as <b>the verse states: “He has uncovered the nakedness of his sister”</b> (Leviticus 20:17), indicating that one is liable for engaging in intercourse with any sister.
