The <i>baraita</i> continues: Even <b>if</b> the verse <b>had not stated</b> that one is liable for engaging in intercourse with his sister, i.e., one’s sister with whom he has both parents in common, <b>I have</b> proof <b>from</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference: If</b> the Torah <b>punished</b> an individual for engaging in intercourse <b>with the daughter of his father who is not the daughter of his mother, or</b> for engaging in intercourse with <b>the daughter of his mother who is not the daughter of his father,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> clear that he should be punished for engaging in intercourse with his sister who is both <b>the daughter of his father and the daughter of his mother?</b> From the fact that the Torah explicitly prohibited intercourse in that case and did not rely on the inference, <b>you learn that one does not administer punishment</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>We heard</b> from that verse (Leviticus 20:17) the <b>punishment</b> for engaging in intercourse with one’s sister with whom he has both parents in common. <b>From where</b> is the <b>prohibition</b> against engaging in those acts of intercourse derived? It is derived from a verse, as <b>the verse states: “The nakedness of your sister, the daughter of your father or the daughter of your mother…</b>you shall not uncover” (Leviticus 18:9). <b>I have</b> derived the prohibition against engaging in intercourse <b>only</b> with <b>the daughter of his father who is not the daughter of his mother, or</b> with <b>the daughter of his mother who is not the daughter of his father. From where</b> do I derive a prohibition against engaging in intercourse with his sister who is both <b>the daughter of his father and the daughter of his mother?</b> It is derived from a verse, as <b>the verse states: “The nakedness of the daughter of your father’s wife, born of your father; she is your sister”</b> (Leviticus 18:11), indicating that engaging in intercourse with any sister is prohibited.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: Even <b>if</b> the verse <b>had not stated</b> that engaging in intercourse with one’s sister with whom he has both parents in common is forbidden, <b>I have</b> proof for this <b>from</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference: If one was prohibited</b> from engaging in intercourse <b>with the daughter of his mother who is not the daughter of his father, and</b> with <b>the daughter of his father who is not the daughter of his mother,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> clear that he is prohibited from engaging in intercourse with his sister who is both <b>the daughter of his father and the daughter of his mother? You learn</b> from this <b>that one does not</b> derive <b>a prohibition</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>From where</b> is it derived that one does not administer punishment to <b>those liable</b> to receive <b>lashes</b> based on an <i>a fortiori</i> inference, and that the principle is not limited to capital punishment? <b>The verse states</b> a verbal analogy between the term “wicked” written with regard to those liable to be executed and the term “wicked” written with regard to those liable to receive lashes. With regard to those liable to be executed, it is written: “Who is <b>wicked</b> and deserves to die” (Numbers 35:31). With regard to those liable to receive lashes, it is written: “And it shall be if the <b>wicked</b> is deserving of lashes” (Deuteronomy 25:2).
<b>From where</b> is it derived that one does not administer punishment to <b>those liable to be exiled</b> based on an <i>a fortiori</i> inference? It is <b>derived</b> by means of a verbal analogy between the term <b>“murderer”</b> written with regard to those who kill intentionally (see Numbers 35:21) and the term <b>“murderer”</b> written with regard to those who kill unwittingly (see Numbers 35:11). The conclusion is that one does not administer any punishment based on an <i>a fortiori</i> inference.
§ Apropos the dispute between the Sadducees and the Sages, <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai says</b> in the form of an oath: <b>I will</b> not <b>see the</b> future <b>consolation</b> of the Jewish people <b>if I did not</b> as a member of the court <b>kill</b> a single <b>conspiring witness,</b> in order <b>to eradicate</b> this reasoning <b>from the hearts of the Sadducees, who would say: The conspiring witnesses are executed only</b> if they are rendered conspiring witnesses after the accused <b>will be killed.</b> Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai killed the conspiring witness while the accused remained alive.
<b>Shimon ben Shataḥ said to him: I will</b> not <b>see the consolation</b> of the Jewish people <b>if you did not shed</b> thereby <b>innocent blood, as the Sages said: Conspiring witnesses are not executed unless both of them are rendered conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>and they are not flogged unless both of them are rendered conspiring</b> witnesses. In this case, only one was rendered a conspiring witness.
<b>Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai immediately accepted</b> a commitment <b>upon himself that he would issue a halakhic ruling only</b> when he was <b>before Shimon ben Shataḥ,</b> to avoid mistakes in the future. <b>And</b> throughout <b>all of Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai’s days he would</b> tearfully <b>prostrate himself on the grave of that witness</b> whom he executed, to request forgiveness for having done so, <b>and his voice was heard</b> from a distance. <b>And the people thought to say</b> that it was <b>the voice of</b> the <b>executed</b> witness that was heard. Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai <b>said</b> to them: <b>It is my voice. Know</b> that this is so, as <b>tomorrow,</b> i.e., sometime in the future, <b>he,</b> referring to himself, <b>will die,</b> and <b>his voice will no</b> longer <b>be heard.</b>
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, said to Rav Ashi:</b> The fact that the voice will cease after Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai’s death is inconclusive as proof that the voice is not that of the executed witness. <b>Perhaps</b> the reason that the voice of the executed person will no longer be heard is that <b>he confronted</b> Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai <b>in trial</b> before the heavenly court, obviating the need for crying from his grave. <b>Alternatively,</b> perhaps Rabbi Yehuda ben Tabbai <b>appeased</b> the executed witness in the World-to-Come, and there is silence because no grievances remained.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> It is written: <b>“At the mouth of two witnesses or three witnesses shall he who is to die be executed”</b> (Deuteronomy 17:6). The question is: <b>If the testimony is valid with two</b> witnesses, <b>why did the verse specify</b> that it is valid <b>with three? Rather,</b> it is <b>to juxtapose</b> and liken <b>three to two: Just as three</b> witnesses <b>can render the two</b> witnesses <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>so too, the two</b> witnesses <b>can render the three</b> wit-nesses <b>conspiring</b> witnesses. <b>And from where</b> is it derived that two witnesses can render <b>even one hundred</b> witnesses conspiring witnesses? It is derived from a verse, as <b>the verse states:</b> “Three <b>witnesses.”</b> Since the verse is obviously discussing witnesses, the term witnesses is superfluous, as it could have stated: Two or three. The term “witnesses” teaches that two witnesses can render a set of witnesses conspiring witnesses irrespective of their number.
<b>Rabbi Shimon says</b> that three witnesses are mentioned in the verse in order to teach: <b>Just as two</b> witnesses who testified that a person is liable to be executed <b>are not killed</b> for this testimony <b>unless both of them are</b> found to be <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, <b>so too, three</b> witnesses who testified together <b>are not killed unless</b> all <b>three of them are</b> found to be <b>conspiring</b> witnesses. <b>And from where</b> is it derived that the same <i>halakha</i> applies <b>even</b> to <b>one hundred</b> witnesses? It is derived from a verse, as <b>the verse states:</b> “Three <b>witnesses.”</b> The superfluous term “witnesses” teaches that the status of all witnesses who come to court as a single set of witnesses is that of one testimony with regard to this <i>halakha</i>.
<b>Rabbi Akiva says: The third</b> witness mentioned in this verse <b>does not come</b> for the judges <b>to be lenient</b> concerning him; <b>rather,</b> its mention comes for the judges <b>to be stringent concerning him and to render his halakhic status like</b> that of <b>these</b> two witnesses who testified with him. One could claim that since the testimony of the third witness is superfluous, as the testimony of the other two witnesses sufficed, the third witness and any other witnesses beyond the first two should be exempt. Therefore, the verse teaches that since he testified with them and was rendered a conspiring witness with them, he too is executed.
One can learn a moral from this <i>halakha</i>: <b>And if the verse punished one who associates with transgressors</b> with a punishment <b>like</b> the one received by the <b>transgressors,</b> even though his role in the transgression is ancillary, <b>all the more so will</b> God <b>pay a reward to one who associates with those who perform a mitzva like</b> the reward of those <b>who perform</b> the <b>mitzva</b> themselves, even though his role in performing the mitzva is ancillary.
The mishna cites another derivation based on the juxtaposition of two to three: <b>And just as</b> with regard to <b>two</b> witnesses, if <b>one of them is found</b> to be <b>a relative or</b> is otherwise <b>disqualified, their</b> entire <b>testimony is voided,</b> as it is no longer the testimony of two witnesses, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>three</b> witnesses who came to testify as one set, if <b>one of them is found</b> to be <b>a relative or</b> is otherwise <b>disqualified, their</b> entire <b>testimony is voided,</b> even though two valid witnesses remain. <b>From where</b> is it derived that the same <i>halakha</i> applies <b>even</b> in the case of <b>one hundred</b> witnesses? It is derived from a verse, as <b>the verse states: “Witnesses.”</b>

Daf 6a

<b>Rabbi Yosei says: In what</b> case <b>is this statement,</b> that if one of the three witnesses is disqualified the entire testimony is voided, <b>said?</b> It is said <b>with regard to</b> cases of <b>capital law,</b> which are adjudicated stringently. <b>But with regard to</b> cases of <b>monetary law,</b> which are adjudicated more leniently, even if one of the witnesses is disqualified, <b>the testimony will be validated with</b> the testimony of <b>the rest</b> of the witnesses, and if it is sufficient the case can be adjudicated on that basis. <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi disagrees and <b>says:</b> If one of the three witnesses is disqualified the entire testimony is voided in <b>both</b> cases of <b>monetary law and</b> cases of <b>capital law.</b>
<b>And when</b> does one disqualified witness void the entire testimony? Only <b>when</b> the witnesses <b>forewarned them</b> before they performed the transgression, thereby demonstrating their desire to fill the role of witnesses in that case. <b>But when they did not forewarn them, what shall two brothers do</b> in a case <b>where</b> they, together with others, <b>saw someone who killed a person?</b> Will the murderer escape punishment because two relatives happened to be there at the time of the murder and their presence voids the entire testimony? No, the testimony is voided by the presence of relatives or disqualified witnesses only when their intent was to testify. If that was not their intent, they do not void the testimony.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> Apropos the statement in the mishna that the halakhic status of one hundred witnesses equals that of two witnesses with regard to the <i>halakha</i> of conspiring witnesses among other <i>halakhot</i>, <b>Rava says: And that</b> is their status only in a case <b>where all</b> the witnesses <b>testified within</b> the time required <b>for speaking</b> a short phrase. If their testimony extended over a longer period, the testimony of the witnesses does not constitute one testimony. <b>Rav Aḥa of Difti said to Ravina: Now, what are the circumstances</b> of the period of time indicated by the words: <b>Within</b> the time required <b>for speaking</b> a short phrase? It is a period whose duration is <b>equivalent</b> to the duration of <b>the greeting of a student to a rabbi:</b> <i>Shalom alekha</i>, <i>rabbi</i>, which means: Greetings to you, my teacher. The duration of the testimony of <b>one hundred</b> witnesses <b>is</b> always <b>greater</b> than the duration of that greeting. How, then, can one hundred witnesses testify within the time required for speaking a short phrase?
Ravina <b>said to him:</b> Rava means that <b>each and every</b> witness must begin testifying <b>within</b> the time required <b>for speaking</b> a short phrase <b>after the other</b> witness completes his testimony. The result is an uninterrupted series of testimonies that combine into a single testimony.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: The third</b> witness mentioned in this verse <b>does not come</b> for the judges to be lenient concerning him; rather, its mention comes for the judges to be stringent concerning him and to render his halakhic status like that of these two witnesses who testified with him. <b>And just as</b> with regard to <b>two</b> witnesses, if one of them was found to be a relative or otherwise disqualified their entire testimony is voided, the same is true for a set consisting of numerous witnesses. <b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye: But if that is so,</b> that a relative or other disqualified witness who witnesses an incident voids the entire testimony, the <b>murdered</b> victim <b>should spare</b> the accused from execution because the victim himself witnessed his own murder, and he is considered a disqualified witness because of his personal stake in the verdict. Therefore, the entire testimony should be voided. Abaye said to Rav Pappa: A murderer is executed only <b>when he killed</b> the victim <b>from behind him</b> and the victim did not witness his murder.
Rav Pappa said to Abaye: It is not uncommon for a murder to take place without the knowledge of the victim. But in a case where one is sentenced to death for sodomizing a male, why is he executed? <b>The one who was sodomized should spare</b> the accused from execution. Here too, since the victim witnessed the sodomy, and he is disqualified from bearing witness due to his personal stake in the verdict, the entire testimony should be voided. The Gemara answers: The sodomizer, too, is executed only <b>when he sodomized</b> the victim <b>from behind him,</b> and the victim did not witness the act of sodomy.
Rav Pappa said to Abaye: Although it is possible to construct a scenario where the victim did not witness the act, it is difficult to construct a scenario where the perpetrator did not witness the act. Therefore, the <b>murderer and the one who sodomizes should spare</b> themselves from execution and void the testimony because they witnessed the act, and they are disqualified from bearing witness on the grounds that a person is not capable of testifying about himself. Abaye <b>was silent</b> and was unable to respond. <b>When</b> Rav Pappa <b>came before Rava</b> with this question, Rava <b>said to him:</b> It is written: “According to two witnesses or three witnesses <b>shall a matter be established”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:15); it is <b>with regard to</b> those who <b>establish a matter</b> as legal fact in court that <b>the verse is speaking.</b> It is only witnesses who are relatives or otherwise disqualified who void the entire testimony, not subjects of the matter. Therefore, there is no basis for the questions of Rav Pappa.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Yosei says: In what</b> case <b>is this statement said…what shall two brothers do</b> in a case where they, together with others, saw someone who killed a person? Rabbi Yosei says that it is only with regard to cases of capital law that one disqualified witness voids the entire testimony, and Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi says that this is the <i>halakha</i> even with regard to cases of monetary law. The mishna continues and says that it is only if the witnesses forewarned the perpetrators that they are classified as witnesses capable of voiding the entire testimony. The Gemara poses a question: <b>How do we,</b> the members of the court, formulate what we <b>say to</b> the witnesses in order to ascertain whether their intent was to testify?
<b>Rava says: This</b> is what <b>we say to</b> the witnesses who come to the court: <b>Did you come to observe</b> the proceedings <b>or did you come to testify? If</b> the witnesses <b>say they came to testify,</b> then if <b>one of them is found</b> to be <b>a relative or</b> otherwise <b>disqualified, their</b> entire <b>testimony is voided. If</b> the witnesses <b>say</b> that <b>they came to observe,</b> in that situation, <b>what shall two brothers do</b> in a case <b>where they saw someone who killed a person?</b> It is certainly unusual for those who witnessed the murder to not even attend the court hearing.
With regard to the dispute in the mishna, <b>it was stated</b> that there is an amoraic dispute: <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Shmuel says:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, and Rav Naḥman says:</b> The <b><i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.

Daf 6b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In a case where there <b>were two</b> witnesses <b>observing</b> an individual violating a capital transgression <b>from this window</b> in a house, <b>and two observing him from that window</b> in a house, <b>and one</b> person was <b>forewarning</b> the transgressor <b>in the middle</b> between the two sets of witnesses, the <i>halakha</i> depends on the circumstances. In a situation <b>where some of</b> the witnesses observing from the two windows <b>see each other,</b> the testimony of all <b>these</b> witnesses constitutes <b>one testimony, but if</b> they do <b>not</b> see each other, the testimony of <b>these</b> witnesses constitutes <b>two</b> independent <b>testimonies. Therefore,</b> as two independent sets of witnesses, <b>if one of</b> the sets <b>was found</b> to be a set of <b>conspiring</b> witnesses, while the testimony of the other set remained valid, both <b>he,</b> the one accused of violating the capital transgression, <b>and they,</b> the conspiring witnesses, <b>are executed, and the second</b> set, whose testimony remained valid, <b>is exempt.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> Transgressors <b>are never executed unless his two witnesses are</b> the ones <b>forewarning him, as it is stated: “At the mouth of two witnesses…</b>he who is to be put to death shall die” (Deuteronomy 17:6), from which it is derived that it is from the mouths of the two witnesses that the accused must be forewarned, and forewarning issued by someone else is insufficient. <b>Alternatively,</b> from the phrase <b>“at the mouth of two witnesses”</b> one derives <b>that</b> the judges must hear the testimony directly from the witnesses, and the <b>Sanhedrin will not hear</b> testimony <b>from the mouth of an interpreter.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Zutra bar Tuvya says</b> that <b>Rav says: From where</b> is it derived with regard <b>to disjointed testimony,</b> in which each of the witnesses saw the incident independent of the other, <b>that it is not valid?</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as it is stated: “He shall not die at the mouth of one witness”</b> (Deuteronomy 17:6). The exposition is as follows: <b>What</b> is the meaning of <b>“one</b> witness”? <b>If we say</b> that it means <b>one witness literally, we learn it from the first</b> portion of the verse: <b>“At the mouth of two witnesses,”</b> indicating that the testimony of fewer than two witnesses is not valid. <b>Rather, what</b> is the meaning of <b>“one</b> witness”? It means that the accused is not executed based on the testimony of people who witnessed an incident with <b>one</b> witness here and <b>one</b> witness elsewhere.
The Gemara notes: <b>This is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: It is written: <b>“He shall not die at the mouth of one witness,”</b> from which it is derived <b>to include</b> the <i>halakha</i> that in the case of <b>two</b> witnesses <b>who observe</b> an individual violating a capital transgression, <b>one from this window and one from that window, and they do not see each other, that they do not join</b> to constitute a set of witnesses. <b>Moreover, even</b> if they witnessed the same transgression from the same perspective, watching the incident not at the same time but <b>one after the other in one window, they do not join</b> to constitute a set of witnesses.
<b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye:</b> Why is it necessary to mention both cases? <b>Now if</b> in the case where <b>one</b> witness views the incident <b>from this window and one</b> witness views the incident <b>from that window, where this</b> witness <b>sees the entire incident and that</b> witness <b>sees the entire incident, you say</b> that <b>they do not join</b> to testify together as two witnesses, if they see the incident <b>one after the other, where this</b> witness <b>sees half</b> the <b>incident and that</b> witness <b>sees half</b> the <b>incident,</b> is it <b>necessary</b> to say that the witnesses do not join together? Abaye <b>said to him:</b> It <b>is necessary</b> to state this <i>halakha</i> <b>only</b> with regard <b>to</b> a case where they witnessed one who <b>engages in intercourse with a forbidden relative,</b> which is a continuing act, and each of the witnesses saw sufficient behavior to render the transgressor liable. The <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> teaches that even in that case, they do not join to constitute a set of witnesses.
Apropos witnesses joining to constitute a set of witnesses, <b>Rava says:</b> Even if the witness in either window is unable to see the witness in the other window, <b>if</b> the witness in each window <b>sees the one who is forewarning</b> the accused, <b>or</b> if <b>the one who is forewarning</b> the accused <b>could see</b> the two disjointed witnesses, <b>they join</b> to constitute a set of witnesses. <b>Rava says:</b> The one <b>forewarning</b> the accused of <b>whom</b> the Sages <b>spoke</b> need not be a third witness, but <b>even</b> if the victim forewarns the murderer <b>from his own mouth, and even</b> if the forewarning emerged <b>from the mouth of a demon,</b> meaning the source of the forewarning is unknown, the forewarning is legitimate.
<b>Rav Naḥman says: Disjointed testimony</b> of two witnesses, each of whom observed an incident independent of the other, <b>is valid in</b> cases of <b>monetary law, as it is written: “He shall not die at the mouth of one witness”</b> (Deuteronomy 17:6). This indicates that <b>it is</b> only <b>with regard to</b> cases of <b>capital law that</b> disjointed testimony <b>is not valid, but with regard to</b> cases of <b>monetary law</b> that testimony <b>is valid.</b>
<b>Rav Zutra objects to this: But if that is so,</b> and disjointed testimony is effective in certain cases, <b>in</b> cases of <b>capital law</b> disjointed testimony <b>should spare</b> the accused from execution. Since one must exploit every avenue possible to prevent executions, in a case where some of the disjointed witnesses were rendered conspiring witnesses, the entire testimony should be voided on their account. <b>Why,</b> then, <b>did we learn</b> in the mishna that if one set witnessed the capital transgression from one window and one set from the other window, and one set was found to be a set of conspiring witnesses, <b>he,</b> the accused, <b>and they,</b> the conspiring witnesses, <b>are executed?</b> The Gemara comments: Indeed, that is <b>difficult</b> according to Rav Naḥman.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> Perpetrators are never executed unless his two witnesses are the ones forewarning him. <b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye: And is Rabbi Yosei of</b> the opinion that <b>this</b> line of <b>reasoning</b> is correct, and forewarning by the witnesses is indispensable? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (9b): <b>Rabbi Yosei says: An enemy</b> who commits murder cannot claim that he killed the victim unwittingly. Rather, <b>he is executed</b> even if there was no forewarning, <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that his</b> halakhic status is <b>like</b> that of one who is <b>cautioned and forewarned.</b> Apparently, Rabbi Yosei does not always require that there be forewarning.
Abaye <b>said to him: That</b> statement in the mishna you cited that is attributed to Rabbi Yosei <b>is</b> actually the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda says: A <i>ḥaver</i> does not require forewarning, as forewarning was instituted only to distinguish between</b> one who commits a transgression <b>unwittingly and</b> one who does so <b>intentionally.</b> A <i>ḥaver</i>, who is a Torah scholar, does not require forewarning to distinguish between them. Rabbi Yosei ben Ḥalafta, whose opinion is cited in the mishna here, is of the opinion that forewarning is a necessary prerequisite to executing someone who is judged liable, and that forewarning must be issued by the witnesses.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>Alternatively,</b> from the phrase in the verse <b>“at the mouth of two witnesses”</b> one derives <b>that</b> the <b>Sanhedrin will not hear</b> testimony <b>from the mouth of an interpreter.</b> The Gemara relates: There were <b>certain</b> people who spoke <b>a foreign</b> language <b>who came before Rava</b> for judgment. <b>Rava installed an interpreter between them</b> and heard the testimony through the interpreter. The Gemara asks: <b>And how did he do so? But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna <b>that</b> the <b>Sanhedrin will not hear</b> testimony <b>from the mouth of an interpreter?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Rava knew what they were saying,</b> as he understood their language, <b>but he did not know</b> how to <b>respond</b> to them in their language. He posed questions through the interpreter but understood the answers on his own, as required by the mishna.

Daf 7a

Apropos disqualified witnesses, the Gemara relates: Two people called <b>Ile’a and Tuviyya,</b> who signed as witnesses on a promissory note, <b>were relatives of the guarantor</b> of the loan. <b>Rav Pappa thought to say</b> that since <b>vis-à-vis the borrower and lender</b> these witnesses <b>are distant</b> and are not related, their testimony on the document is valid. <b>Rav Huna, son of Rav Yehoshua, said to Rav Pappa: If the borrower does not have</b> the means to repay the loan, <b>doesn’t</b> the <b>lender pursue the guarantor</b> to claim his debt? Therefore, the guarantor is party to the loan, and his relatives are not eligible to serve as witnesses on the promissory note.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> This mishna continues to discuss the matter of testimony in the case of one who is liable to be executed. Concerning <b>one whose verdict was delivered</b> and he was sentenced to death <b>and he fled, and he</b> then <b>came before the same court</b> that sentenced him, <b>they do not overturn his verdict</b> and retry him. Rather, the court administers the previous verdict. Consequently, in <b>any place where two</b> witnesses <b>will stand and say: We testify with regard to a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that his verdict was delivered</b> and he was sentenced to death <b>in the court of so-and-so, and so-and-so and so-and-so</b> were <b>his witnesses, that</b> person <b>shall be executed</b> on the basis of that testimony.
The mishna continues: The mitzva to establish <b>a Sanhedrin</b> with the authority to administer capital punishments <b>is in effect</b> both <b>in Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>and outside Eretz</b> Yisrael. <b>A Sanhedrin that executes</b> a transgressor <b>once in seven years is characterized</b> as <b>a destructive</b> tribunal. Since the Sanhedrin would subject the testimony to exacting scrutiny, it was extremely rare for a defendant to be executed. <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says:</b> This categorization applies to a Sanhedrin that executes a transgressor <b>once in seventy years. Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Akiva say: If we had been</b> members <b>of the Sanhedrin,</b> we would have conducted trials in a manner whereby <b>no person would have ever been executed. Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says:</b> In adopting that approach, <b>they too</b> would <b>increase</b> the number of <b>murderers among the Jewish people.</b> The death penalty would lose its deterrent value, as all potential murderers would know that no one is ever executed.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara infers: <b>It is</b> in the case of one who comes <b>before the same court that they do not overturn</b> the verdict, <b>but</b> if one comes <b>before a different court they overturn</b> the verdict and retry the case. The Gemara asks: <b>Isn’t it taught in the latter clause</b> of the mishna: <b>Any place where two</b> witnesses <b>will stand and say: We testify with regard to a man</b> called <b>so-and-so that his verdict was delivered</b> and he was sentenced to death <b>in the court of so-and-so, and so-and-so and so-and-so</b> were <b>his witnesses, that</b> person <b>shall be executed</b> on the basis of that testimony? This indicates that the verdict is not overturned and the defendant is not retried even before another court.
<b>Abaye said:</b> This apparent contradiction is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>here,</b> in the first clause of the mishna, from which it was inferred that the second court overturns the initial verdict, it is referring to a case where the initial verdict was outside Eretz Yisrael and the defendant came before a court <b>in Eretz Yisrael. There,</b> in the latter clause, which indicates that the second court sustains the initial verdict and does not retry the defendant, it is referring to a case where the initial verdict was in Eretz Yisrael, and the case subsequently came before a court <b>outside Eretz</b> Yisrael.
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, that <b>Rabbi Yehuda ben Dostai says in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Shataḥ:</b> If a convicted defendant <b>fled from Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>to outside Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>one does not overturn his verdict.</b> Rather, they implement the initial verdict. But if one fled <b>from outside Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>to Eretz</b> Yisrael, <b>one overturns his verdict</b> and the defendant is retried. Perhaps, <b>due to the merit of Eretz Yisrael,</b> the court will discover a reason to exonerate him.
§ The mishna teaches: The mitzva to establish <b>a Sanhedrin</b> with the authority to administer capital punishments <b>is in effect</b> both in Eretz Yisrael and outside Eretz Yisrael. The Gemara clarifies: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? It is <b>as the Sages taught:</b> It is written with regard to the sentencing of murderers: <b>“And these shall be for you as a statute of justice for your generations</b> in all your dwelling places” (Numbers 35:29), from which <b>we learn that</b> the mitzva to establish <b>a Sanhedrin is in effect</b> both <b>in Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>and outside Eretz</b> Yisrael.
<b>If so, what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states:</b> “Judges and officers you shall appoint <b>in all your gates”</b> (Deuteronomy 16:18), which indicates that the mitzva to establish a Sanhedrin is in effect where the gates are yours, i.e., only in Eretz Yisrael? The explanation is as follows: <b>In your gates,</b> in Eretz Yisrael, <b>you establish courts in each and every district and in each and every city, and outside Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>you establish</b> courts <b>in each and every district, but you do not establish</b> courts <b>in each and every city.</b> The requirement to establish courts in every city is only in Eretz Yisrael.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>A Sanhedrin that executes</b> once in seven years is characterized as a destructive tribunal. Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says: This categorization applies to a Sanhedrin that executes once in seventy years. <b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: Is Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya saying that a Sanhedrin that executes <b>once in seventy,</b> rather than seven, <b>years is characterized as a destructive</b> tribunal? <b>Or perhaps</b> he is saying that <b>standard conduct is</b> for a Sanhedrin to execute once in seventy years, and only if it executes more than one person during that period is it characterized as destructive? The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Akiva say: If we had been</b> members of the Sanhedrin, we would have conducted the trials in a manner where no person would have ever been executed. The Gemara asks: <b>How would they have acted</b> to spare the accused from execution if witnesses testified that he intentionally committed murder? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Elazar both say</b> that they would have asked the witnesses: <b>Did you see</b> whether the accused <b>killed a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> i.e., a person with a condition that would lead to his death within twelve months, or if <b>he killed</b> someone who was <b>intact?</b> The halakhic status of a <i>tereifa</i> is like that of one who is dead, in the sense that one who kills him is not executed. Since no witness can be certain with regard to the victim’s physical condition, they would invalidate any testimony to a murder.
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> Even <b>if you say</b> that they examined him postmortem and <b>he was intact</b> the testimony could be challenged, as <b>perhaps in the place</b> that the <b>sword</b> pierced the victim’s body <b>there was a perforation</b> in one of the organs that renders the person a <i>tereifa</i>, but which was rendered undetectable by the wound caused by the sword.
The Gemara asks: <b>With regard to one who engages in intercourse with a forbidden relative, how would they have acted</b> to spare the accused from execution? <b>Abaye and Rava both say</b> that they would have asked the witnesses: <b>Did you see</b> the intercourse, <b>like a brush</b> entering <b>into a tube?</b> Since witnesses rarely witness the act that closely, one could claim that the testimony is incomplete. The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> concerning <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Tarfon and Rabbi Akiva, <b>how would they</b> have <b>adjudicated</b> that case? The Gemara answers: They hold <b>in accordance with</b> the statement of <b>Shmuel, as Shmuel says: In</b> cases involving <b>adulterers</b> one can testify and convict them <b>from when they will appear as adulterers,</b> without any need for him to witness the act in graphic detail.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>These are</b> the people <b>who are exiled:</b> Anyone <b>who kills a person unintentionally.</b> Whether one is liable to be exiled depends on the particular circumstances of the case: If <b>one was rolling a roller</b> to smooth the covering of mortar that he applied to seal his roof <b>and</b> the roller <b>fell upon</b> a person <b>and killed him,</b> or if <b>one was lowering a barrel</b> from the roof <b>and it fell on</b> a person <b>and killed him,</b> or if he <b>was descending a ladder and he fell on</b> a person <b>and killed him,</b> in all of these cases, <b>he is exiled. But if one was pulling a roller</b> toward him <b>and it fell</b> from his hands <b>upon</b> a person <b>and killed him,</b> or if <b>one was lifting a barrel and the rope was severed and it fell upon</b> a person <b>and killed him,</b>

Daf 7b

or if <b>one was climbing a ladder and he fell upon</b> a person <b>and killed him, that</b> unintentional murderer <b>is not exiled. This is the principle: Any</b> murderer <b>who</b> kills unintentionally <b>through his downward</b> motion <b>is exiled, and</b> one <b>who</b> kills <b>not through his downward</b> motion <b>is not exiled.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks with regard to the principle that one is exiled only if he killed unintentionally through a downward motion: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? It is derived from a verse, <b>as Shmuel says that the verse states</b> with regard to those exiled to a city of refuge: <b>“And he cast it down upon him and dies”</b> (Numbers 35:23), indicating that one is not liable to be exiled <b>unless</b> the item <b>falls in a downward</b> motion.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> derivations from verses written with regard to the unintentional murderer: <b>“Unintentionally”</b> (Numbers 35:11); to <b>exclude</b> from exile <b>the</b> one who kills <b>intentionally. “Unawares”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:4); to <b>exclude</b> from exile <b>the</b> one who kills <b>with intent.</b>
The Gemara asks: Why is a derivation necessary to exclude one who kills <b>intentionally?</b> It is <b>obvious</b> that he is not exiled; <b>he is subject to</b> the <b>death</b> penalty. <b>Rather, Rava said: Say</b> that the type of intentional killer referred to is meant to <b>exclude the</b> one who <b>says</b> that <b>it is permitted</b> to kill the victim. The verse teaches that this person is neither executed nor is he exiled. <b>Abaye said to</b> Rava: <b>If</b> the reference is to one who <b>says</b> that <b>it is permitted, he</b> is <b>a victim of circumstances beyond his control,</b> as he did not know any better. How could that be characterized as intentional? Rava <b>said to him:</b> That is not a problem, <b>as I say</b> that with regard to <b>one who says</b> that <b>it is permitted,</b> his action <b>borders on the intentional.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> states: <b>“Unawares”;</b> to <b>exclude</b> from exile <b>the</b> one who kills <b>with intent.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>With intent?</b> It is <b>obvious</b> that he is not exiled; <b>he is subject to</b> the <b>death</b> penalty. <b>Rabba said:</b> The reference is to <b>exclude the</b> one who acted <b>with</b> the <b>intent to kill an animal and he killed a person</b> inadvertently, or one who acted with the intent <b>to</b> kill <b>a gentile and he killed a Jew,</b> or one who acted with the intent <b>to</b> kill <b>a non-viable newborn and he killed a viable</b> newborn.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> based on the verse written with regard to an unintentional murderer: “And if suddenly, without enmity, he thrust him or cast upon him any vessel without lying in wait” (Numbers 35:22). <b>“If suddenly”;</b> this serves to <b>exclude</b> one who unintentionally kills another that he encounters at <b>a corner. “Without enmity”;</b> this serves to <b>exclude</b> one who unintentionally kills his <b>enemy,</b> as even if the act appears unintentional, the presumption is that it was not. <b>“He thrust him”;</b> this indicates <b>that</b> even if he unintentionally <b>shoved him with his body</b> and killed him, he is liable to be exiled. <b>“Or cast upon him”;</b> this serves to <b>include</b> the case of <b>a downward</b> motion <b>that is for the purpose of an upward</b> motion, e.g., if one bent down in order to lift an item from the ground, and in the process of bending down he killed another unintentionally, he is exiled. <b>“Without lying in wait”;</b> this serves to <b>exclude</b> one who <b>had</b> the <b>intent</b> to throw a stone <b>to this side and it went to a different side</b> and killed a person.
And it is written with regard to an unintentional murderer: <b>“And who did not lie in wait”</b> (Exodus 21:13); this serves to <b>exclude</b> one who <b>had intent to throw</b> a stone <b>two</b> cubits <b>and he</b> inadvertently <b>threw</b> it <b>four</b> cubits and killed a person. And it is written concerning an unintentional murderer: <b>“And as one who goes with his neighbor into the forest”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:5), from which it is derived: <b>Just as a forest</b> is ownerless property, and there is <b>permission for the victim and for the assailant to enter there, so too,</b> unintentional murder that occurs in <b>any</b> place where there is <b>permission for the victim and for the assailant to enter there,</b> is punishable with exile. The unintentional murder of one who entered the assailant’s property without his permission is not cause for the murderer to be exiled.
§ The Gemara cites a related discussion. <b>Rabbi Abbahu raised a dilemma before Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> If <b>one was ascending a ladder and the rung was displaced from beneath him, and</b> the rung <b>fell and killed</b> another, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to his being exiled? <b>In a case like this, is</b> it considered killing in <b>an upward</b> motion, as he was climbing the ladder, and therefore he is exempt, <b>or is</b> it considered killing in <b>a downward</b> motion, as when he stepped on the rung he pushed it down, and therefore he is liable? Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to him:</b> In the scenario you described, <b>you already touched upon</b> the case in the <i>baraita</i> cited above, the case of unintentional murder that is performed with <b>a downward</b> motion <b>that is for the purpose of an upward</b> motion. The ruling in the <i>baraita</i> is that one is liable to be exiled in that case.
Rabbi Abbahu <b>raised an objection to</b> the explanation of Rabbi Yoḥanan from the mishna, which states: <b>This is the principle: Any</b> murderer <b>who</b> kills unintentionally <b>through his downward</b> motion <b>is exiled, and</b> one <b>who</b> kills <b>not through his downward</b> motion <b>is not exiled.</b> Rabbi Abbahu clarifies: With regard to the phrase in the mishna: One <b>who</b> kills <b>not through his downward</b> motion, <b>what</b> case that was not already specified does this serve <b>to add? Is it not to add a case like this,</b> and to teach that even though the death was caused by the falling rung, since the assailant was ascending the ladder at the time, he is not exiled? Rabbi Yoḥanan replied: <b>And according to your reasoning</b> that this apparently extraneous phrase serves to include a case that is not addressed explicitly, with regard to the previous phrase in the mishna: <b>Any</b> murderer <b>who</b> kills unintentionally <b>through his downward</b> motion, what case that was not already specified does this serve <b>to add?</b>
<b>Rather,</b> one may say that it serves <b>to add</b> the case of <b>a butcher. Here too,</b> in the latter clause, the phrase: One who kills not through his downward motion, serves <b>to add</b> the case of <b>a butcher, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a butcher who was chopping</b> animal limbs with a cleaver, <b>one</b> Sage <b>taught</b> that if he killed a person <b>in front of him,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to be exiled; if he killed a person <b>behind him,</b> he is <b>exempt. And it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: If he killed a person <b>behind him,</b> he is <b>liable;</b> if he killed a person <b>in front of him,</b> he is <b>exempt. And it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>Both</b> if he killed a person <b>in front of him and</b> if he killed a person <b>behind him,</b> he is <b>liable. And it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>Both</b> if he killed a person <b>in front of him and</b> if he killed a person <b>behind him,</b> he is <b>exempt. And</b> although these <i>baraitot</i> appear contradictory, the apparent contradiction is <b>not difficult.</b>
When a butcher is about to cut the meat of an animal, he raises the cleaver and lowers it behind him in order to create momentum that will generate power. Then he raises the cleaver from behind him and brings it down forcefully onto the meat. In that process, the butcher lowers the cleaver in front of him and behind him and raises the cleaver both in front of him and behind him. Each of the four <i>baraitot</i> addresses a different stage of the process. <b>Here,</b> the <i>baraita</i> that says he is liable if he unintentionally murders a person in front of him and exempt if he unintentionally murders a person behind him, is referring to a case where the butcher swings the cleaver <b>with a downward</b> motion <b>in front of him and an upward</b> motion <b>behind him. There,</b> the <i>baraita</i> that says he is exempt if he unintentionally murders a person in front of him and liable if he unintentionally murders a person behind him, is referring to a case where the butcher swings the cleaver <b>with an upward</b> motion <b>in front of him and a downward</b> motion <b>behind him.</b>
In addition, <b>here,</b> the <i>baraita</i> where he is liable in both instances, is referring to a case where the butcher swings the cleaver <b>with a downward</b> motion both <b>in front of him and behind him. There,</b> the <i>baraita</i> where he is exempt in both instances, is referring to a case where the butcher swings the cleaver <b>with an upward</b> motion both <b>in front of him and behind him.</b> The two phrases that constitute the principle in the mishna teach that the determining factor is whether the motion is upward or downward, not whether it is in front of him or behind him. This is Rabbi Yoḥanan’s reply to the objection of Rabbi Abbahu.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that Rabbi Abbahu’s dilemma with regard to unintentional murder committed by one climbing a ladder is <b>the subject of</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>:</b> If <b>one was ascending a ladder and its rung was displaced from beneath him, it is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i> that he is <b>liable, and it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i> that he is <b>exempt. What, is it not</b> that the <i>tanna’im</i> <b>disagree with regard to this, as</b> one <b>Sage holds: It is</b> unintentional murder in <b>a downward</b> motion, and therefore he is liable, <b>and</b> one <b>Sage holds: It is</b> unintentional murder in <b>an upward</b> motion, and therefore he is exempt?
The Gemara rejects that parallel: <b>No,</b> that is not the point of contention, <b>as everyone agrees</b> that <b>it is an upward</b> motion, <b>and</b> there is no dispute between the Sages in the <i>baraitot</i>. Nevertheless, the apparent contradiction between them is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>here,</b> in the <i>baraita</i> that rules him liable, the reference is <b>to</b> payment of <b>damages,</b> as with regard to one’s liability to pay damages, there is no difference between upward and downward motions, or between intentional and unwitting damage; and <b>there,</b> in the <i>baraita</i> that rules him exempt, the reference is <b>to exile,</b> from which one who murders unintentionally in an upward motion is exempt by Torah edict.
<b>If you wish, say</b> instead that in both <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>and that</b> <i>baraita</i> the reference is <b>to exile, and</b> it is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i>, where the ruling is that he is liable, is referring to a case <b>where</b> the rung of the ladder <b>was worm infested</b> and he should have been cautious before stepping on it, and <b>that</b> <i>baraita</i>, where the ruling is that he is exempt, is referring to a case <b>where</b> the rung <b>was not worm infested,</b> as in that case it is murder due to circumstances beyond his control, as he could not have anticipated that the rung would be displaced.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> that in both <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>and that</b> <i>baraita</i> the reference is to a rung that <b>was not worm infested, and</b> it is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i>, where the ruling is that he is exempt, is referring to a case <b>where</b> the rung <b>was tightly</b> inserted into the ladder, and therefore he could not have foreseen that it would be displaced, <b>and that</b> <i>baraita</i>, where the ruling is that he is liable, is referring to a case <b>where</b> the rung <b>was not tightly</b> inserted and he should have anticipated its displacement.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>the blade</b> of an ax or hatchet <b>was displaced from its handle,</b> and it flew through the air <b>and killed</b> a person, <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says: He is not exiled, and the Rabbis say:</b> He is <b>exiled.</b> If part <b>of a tree that is being split</b> flew through the air and killed a person, <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> The murderer is <b>exiled, and the Rabbis say: He is not exiled.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said to the Rabbis: Is it stated</b> in the verse: <b>And the blade displaces from its wood</b> handle? <b>But isn’t it stated:</b> “And the blade displaces <b>from the wood”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:5), indicating that it is a wood chip from the tree that causes the death of the person? <b>And furthermore: <i>Etz</i> is stated below:</b> “And the blade displaces from the <i>etz</i>,” <b>and <i>etz</i> is stated above,</b> earlier in the same verse: “And his hand wields the ax to cut down the <i>etz</i>.” <b>Just as</b> the term <b><i>etz</i> stated above</b> is referring to wood <b>from the tree that is being split, so too,</b> the term <b><i>etz</i> stated below</b> is referring to wood <b>from the tree that is being split,</b> not to the wood of the ax handle.
<b>Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi says</b> that <b>Rav says: And both of them,</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi and the Rabbis, <b>interpreted one verse</b> to arrive at their rulings. The verse states: <b>“And the blade displaces [<i>venashal</i>] from the wood.” Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds:</b> The <b>tradition</b> of the manner in which the verses in the Torah are written <b>is authoritative,</b> and one derives <i>halakhot</i> based on the manner in which the words are written, not on the manner in which they are vocalized. <b>And it is written <i>venishel</i>,</b> a transitive verb, indicating that the blade displaced wood chips from the tree. <b>And the Rabbis maintain:</b> The <b>vocalization</b> of the Torah <b>is authoritative, and we read</b> the term as <b><i>venashal</i>,</b> an intransitive verb indicating that the blade is displaced from its wooden handle and kills a person.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> does <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>hold</b> that the <b>tradition</b> of the manner in which the verses in the Torah are written <b>is authoritative?</b>

Daf 8a

<b>But doesn’t Rav Yitzḥak, son of Rabbi Yosef, say</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>and Rabbi Yehuda ben Roetz, and Beit Shammai, and Rabbi Shimon, and Rabbi Akiva all hold</b> that the <b>vocalization</b> of the Torah <b>is authoritative,</b> not the manner in which it is written? How can Rav Ḥiyya bar Ashi ascribe Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s ruling to the contrary opinion? The Gemara answers: <b>That is</b> the reason <b>that</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>said to</b> the Rabbis: <b>And furthermore.</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi said to them: If the tradition of the manner in which the verses in the Torah are written is authoritative, that supports my interpretation of the verse; if not, there is an additional proof.
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> In the case of <b>one who cast a clod</b> of earth <b>onto a palm tree, and he severed dates</b> from the tree, <b>and the dates went</b> upon a person <b>and killed him, we have arrived at the dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>and the Rabbis</b> in a case where one was splitting wood and a wood chip flew through the air and killed a person In that case, Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi deems him liable to be exiled, and the Rabbis deem him exempt from exile. The Gemara asks: Isn’t that <b>obvious?</b> The cases are identical. What novel element is Rav Pappa introducing? The Gemara answers: The parallel between the cases drawn by Rav Pappa is necessary. <b>Lest you say</b> that these severed dates were not propelled by the force of his action but <b>are like</b> an item propelled by <b>a force</b> generated by the <b>force of his</b> action, and even Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi would concede that in that case he is exempt from exile, Rav Pappa <b>teaches us</b> that the dates are considered to have been severed by the force of his action.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>how can you find</b> circumstances of <b>a force</b> generated by the <b>force of his</b> action where he would be exempt from exile? The Gemara answers: It can be found in a case <b>where one cast a clod</b> of earth <b>and it struck a branch</b> of the palm tree, <b>and the branch,</b> propelled by the clod, <b>proceeded to strike a cluster of dates [<i>likhevasa</i>] and severed</b> the <b>dates</b> from the branch they were on <b>and</b> the <b>dates proceeded to kill</b> a person.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who threw a stone into the public domain and killed</b> a person <b>is exiled. Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: If after the stone left his hand the other</b> person <b>placed his head out</b> into the public domain <b>and received</b> a blow from the stone, <b>he is exempt,</b> as when he cast the stone into the public domain there was no one there.
In the case of one who <b>threw the stone into his courtyard and killed</b> a person, <b>if the victim had permission to enter into there,</b> the murderer is <b>exiled, but if not, he is not exiled, as it is stated</b> with regard to the cities of refuge: <b>“And as one who goes with his neighbor into the forest”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:5), from which it is derived: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>a forest, the victim and the assailant</b> both <b>have</b> equal <b>permission to enter there, so too,</b> with regard to <b>all</b> places that <b>the victim and the assailant have permission to enter there,</b> the killer is liable. This serves <b>to exclude</b> the <b>courtyard of the homeowner, where the victim and the assailant do not</b> both <b>have permission to enter there.</b> Since the victim had no right to enter his courtyard, the unintentional murderer is exempt from exile.
<b>Abba Shaul says:</b> Another <i>halakha</i> can be derived from that verse: <b>Just as the cutting of wood</b> that is mentioned in the verse is <b>optional, so too, all</b> those liable to be exiled are examples of cases where the unintentional murderer was engaged in an activity that is <b>optional.</b> This serves <b>to exclude a father who strikes his son, and a teacher who oppresses his student, and an agent of the court</b> deputized to flog transgressors. If, in the course of performing the mitzva with which they are charged, they unintentionally murdered the son, the student, or the person being flogged, respectively, they are exempt.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches: One who threw a stone into the public domain and killed a person is exiled. The Gemara asks: If he threw the stone <b>into the public domain,</b> why is he exiled? <b>He is an intentional</b> murderer. He knows that there are generally people in the public domain and is aware that he is likely to harm someone with the stone. <b>Rav Shmuel bar Yitzḥak says:</b> The reference in the mishna is not to one who threw a stone for no reason; rather, it is a case <b>where one is demolishing his wall,</b> and one of the stones struck a person. The Gemara counters: That too is intentional, as <b>he should have examined</b> the other side of the wall to determine if there was anyone there. The Gemara answers: It is a case <b>where one is demolishing his wall at night,</b> when passersby are scarce. The Gemara asks: <b>At night too he is required to examine</b> the other side of the wall to determine if there is anyone there, as although it is uncommon, people are apt to walk through a public domain at all hours.
The Gemara answers: The reference in the mishna is to a case <b>where one demolishes his wall into a scrap heap,</b> where there is no reasonable expectation to find anyone there, as it is located out of the way. The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances of this scrap heap? If it is frequented by the multitudes</b> because it is utilized as a bathroom, <b>he is negligent; if it is not frequented by the multitudes,</b> although it is a public domain in the sense that it is not private property, <b>he is a victim of circumstances beyond his control,</b> as there was no reason to consider the possibility that someone was there.
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> It is <b>necessary</b> to state this <i>halakha</i> <b>only in</b> the case of <b>a scrap heap that is utilized</b> for people <b>to defecate there at night but is not utilized</b> for people <b>to defecate there during the day, but it happens</b> on occasion that one <b>will sit</b> there and defecate during the day. One who demolishes his wall into the scrap heap during the day <b>is neither negligent, as</b> the scrap heap <b>is not utilized</b> for people <b>to defecate there during the day, nor is he a victim of circumstances beyond his control, as it happens</b> on occasion that one <b>will sit</b> there and defecate during the day, which he should have considered. Therefore, his status is that of an unintentional murderer who is liable to be exiled.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says:</b> If after the stone left his hand the other person placed his head out into the public domain and received a blow from the stone, he is exempt. The Gemara cites a related <i>baraita</i>. <b>The Sages taught</b> that it is written: “And the blade displaces from the wood and finds his neighbor and he dies” (Deuteronomy 19:5), from which it is inferred: <b>“And finds”;</b> this serves <b>to exclude</b> one who <b>presents himself</b> and is thereby killed by the stone. <b>From here Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says: If after the stone left his hand the other</b> person <b>placed his head out</b> into the public domain <b>and received</b> a blow from the stone, the murderer is <b>exempt.</b>
The Gemara asks: Is that <b>to say that</b> the term <b>“and finds” indicates</b> an item that was there <b>initially,</b> prior to the incident in question? <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from that which is written with regard to one who seeks to redeem an ancestral field that he sold: “And he acquires the means and finds sufficient funds for his redemption” (Leviticus 25:26). The term <b>“and finds”</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> one who, in order to accrue funds to redeem his field, sells a field that was <b>found</b> in his possession when he originally sold the field. It means <b>that he may not sell</b> land that he owns <b>in a distant</b> place <b>and redeem</b> land that he owns <b>in a proximate</b> place, or sell <b>a low-quality</b> tract of land <b>and redeem with</b> the funds accrued from that sale <b>high-quality</b> land. One may not exploit his right to redeem his land for his own advantage. This <i>baraita</i> indicates that the term “and finds” indicates an item that was not there at the time but presents itself later.
<b>Rava said:</b> This is not difficult, as <b>here,</b> with regard to exile, the term is interpreted in keeping <b>with the context of the verse, and there,</b> with regard to the redemption of land, the term is interpreted in keeping <b>with the context of the verse. There,</b> the term is interpreted in keeping <b>with the context of the verse.</b> The term <b>“and finds”</b> is <b>similar to</b> the phrase that precedes it: <b>“And he acquires the means.” Just as</b> the meaning of the phrase <b>“he acquires the means”</b> is that he acquires the means <b>from now,</b> as had he possessed the means beforehand he would not have sold his ancestral field at all, <b>so too,</b> the meaning of the term <b>“and finds”</b> is <b>also</b> that he finds sufficient funds <b>from now</b> and no earlier. <b>Here,</b> with regard to exile, the term is interpreted in keeping <b>with the context of the verse,</b> as the term <b>“and finds”</b> is <b>similar</b> to <b>a forest: Just as a forest</b> is <b>an entity that is there initially, so too,</b> the term <b>“and finds” also</b> is referring to <b>an entity that is there initially.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>One who throws a stone, etc.</b> Abba Shaul derives from the verse: “And as when one goes with his neighbor into the forest” (Deuteronomy 19:5): Just as the cutting of wood mentioned in the verse is optional, so too, all those liable to be exiled are in cases where the unintentional murderer was engaged in an activity that is optional. <b>One of the Sages said to Rava: From where</b> do you know <b>that</b> the derivation is <b>from the cutting of wood</b> for a purpose <b>that</b> is <b>optional? Perhaps</b> the derivation is <b>from the cutting of wood for</b> the purpose of building <b>a <i>sukka</i> or from cutting wood for the arrangement</b> of wood on the altar, both of which are obligatory, since they are mitzvot, <b>and even so the Merciful One states: Let him be exiled.</b> Rava <b>said to him: Since if one found</b> wood already <b>cut he does not cut</b> other wood, as in that case it is <b>not a mitzva</b> to cut, <b>now,</b> when there is no wood cut <b>as well,</b> although he is cutting wood to facilitate fulfillment of a mitzva, the act of cutting the wood itself is <b>not a mitzva.</b>
<b>Ravina raised an objection to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava</b> from clause of the mishna that states that the example of the forest serves <b>to exclude a father who strikes his son, and a teacher who oppresses his student, and an agent of the court. Let us say</b> with regard to a father who strikes his son: <b>Since if</b> the son was <b>learned,</b> it is <b>not a mitzva</b> to strike him, <b>now,</b> in a case where the son is not learned and the father strikes him to facilitate his education <b>too,</b> it is <b>not a mitzva,</b> and therefore he should be exiled. Rava replied: <b>There, even though</b> the son <b>is learned,</b> it is <b>a mitzva</b> to strike him from time to time, <b>as it is written: “Chastise your son, and he will give you rest; and he will give delight to your soul”</b> (Proverbs 29:17).
<b>Rava then said: That which I said is nothing,</b> as there is a different proof that the verse is not referring to cutting wood in fulfillment of a mitzva. It is written: <b>“And as when one [<i>ve’asher</i>] goes with his neighbor into the forest.”</b> Based on the term <i>asher</i>, which indicates that the entering into the forest may or may not occur, <b>what</b> is the nature of the <b>forest</b> mentioned in the verse? It is a place <b>where if one wants</b> to, <b>he enters</b> the forest, <b>and if one wants</b> to, <b>he does not enter. And if it enters your mind</b> that the verse is referring to one who cuts wood to fulfill <b>a mitzva, would it suffice if he did not enter</b> the forest? He must enter the forest to fulfill the mitzva.
<b>Rav Adda bar Ahava said to Rava:</b> Is it so that <b>wherever</b> the term <b><i>asher</i> is written</b> in the Torah it is a case <b>where if he wants</b> to he does so, and it is not referring to a mitzva or an obligation? <b>If that is so,</b> that which is written: <b>“And a man who [<i>asher</i>] becomes impure and does not purify himself,</b> and that soul shall be excised…as he has impurified the Sanctuary of God” (Numbers 19:20), can be referring only to a case where <b>if he wants</b> to <b>he becomes impure,</b> and <b>if he wants</b> to, <b>he does not become impure.</b> But in the case of <b>a corpse with no one to bury it [<i>met mitzva</i>], where it would not suffice for him not to become impure</b> in the process of burying it, would you say: <b>So too, he is exempt</b> from the punishment of <i>karet</i> if he enters the Temple in a state of impurity?
Rava replied: <b>There it is different, as the verse states</b> with regard to one impure with impurity imparted by a corpse:

Daf 8b

<b>“He shall be impure”</b> (Numbers 19:13), indicating that the same <i>halakhot</i> apply <b>in any case,</b> unrelated to the manner in which he became impure.
The Gemara challenges: <b>He requires that</b> verse for a different derivation, <b>as that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The phrase <b>“He shall be impure”</b> serves <b>to include one who immersed that day</b> and whose purification process will be completed at nightfall. If he enters the Temple before nightfall, he is also liable to receive <i>karet</i>. The phrase later in that verse: <b>“His impurity is upon him,”</b> serves <b>to include one who has not yet</b> brought <b>an atonement</b> offering to complete the purification process, e.g., a leper or a <i>zav</i>. Rava <b>said to</b> Ravina: <b>I said</b> that the <i>halakha</i> that one rendered impure by a <i>met mitzva</i> is liable to receive <i>karet</i> is derived <b>from</b> the extraneous term “yet,” in the phrase later in that same verse: <b>“His impurity is yet</b> upon him,” which indicates that one is liable for entering the Temple in a state of impurity regardless of the manner in which he became impure. That is one version of the discussion.
<b>Some teach</b> this exchange <b>with regard to this</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>“In plowing and in harvest you shall rest”</b> (Exodus 34:21). <b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> This verse is referring to the Sabbatical Year. The Torah <b>does not need to state</b> the prohibition against <b>plowing</b> during the <b>Sabbatical</b> Year <b>or harvesting</b> during the <b>Sabbatical</b> Year, <b>as it is already stated: “You shall neither sow your field, nor prune your vineyard”</b> (Leviticus 25:4). What, then, is derived from this verse? <b>Rather,</b> it is derived that <b>even plowing</b> on the <b>eve of</b> the <b>Sabbatical</b> Year, <b>which</b> facilitates growth of crops when the <b>Sabbatical</b> Year <b>enters, and</b> the <b>harvest of</b> crops that grew during the <b>Sabbatical</b> Year, <b>which emerged</b> and were reaped after the <b>conclusion of the Sabbatical</b> Year, are prohibited.
<b>Rabbi Yishmael says:</b> This verse in Exodus is not referring to the Sabbatical Year; rather, the reference is to plowing and harvesting on Shabbat. <b>Just as plowing</b> is <b>optional,</b> as there is no case where there is a mitzva to plow per se, <b>so too,</b> the <b>harvesting</b> mentioned in the verse is <b>optional.</b> This serves to exclude from the prohibition <b>the harvesting of</b> barley for <b>the <i>omer</i></b> offering, <b>which is a mitzva,</b> and is therefore permitted on Shabbat.
<b>One of the Sages said to Rava: From where</b> does Rabbi Yishmael ascertain <b>that</b> the <b>plowing</b> mentioned in the verse is referring to plowing <b>that</b> is <b>optional? Perhaps</b> the reference is to <b>plowing</b> a field to grow barley for use in the <b><i>omer</i></b> offering, <b>which</b> is <b>a mitzva, and even so the Merciful One states: “You shall rest.”</b> Rava <b>said to him: Since if</b> one <b>found</b> the field already <b>plowed,</b> he need <b>not plow</b> the field but he may directly proceed to sow the barley in the plowed field, clearly, even if one plows a field to facilitate fulfillment of a mitzva, plowing is <b>not a mitzva</b> per se.
<b>Ravina raised an objection to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava</b> from the clause of the mishna that states that the example of the forest serves <b>to exclude a father who strikes his son, and a teacher who oppresses his student, and an agent of the court. And</b> according to your opinion, <b>why</b> are a father who strikes his son and a teacher who oppresses his student excluded from exile? <b>Let us say</b> with regard to a father who strikes his son: <b>Since if</b> the son was <b>learned,</b> it is <b>not a mitzva</b> to strike him, <b>now,</b> in a case where the son is not learned and he strikes him to facilitate his education <b>too,</b> it is <b>not a mitzva,</b> and therefore he should be exiled. Rava replied: <b>There, even if</b> the son <b>is learned,</b> the father <b>is performing a mitzva</b> by striking him from time to time, <b>as it is written: “Chastise your son, and he will give you rest; and he will give delight to your soul”</b> (Proverbs 29:17).
<b>Rava then said: That which I said is nothing,</b> as there is a different proof that the verse is not referring to plowing that is a mitzva. Based on the juxtaposition of the two, it is derived that <b>harvesting</b> is <b>similar</b> to <b>plowing: Just as</b> in the case of <b>plowing,</b> if <b>one found</b> the field already <b>plowed, he</b> need <b>not plow,</b> as there is no case where there is a mitzva to plow per se, <b>so too</b> with regard to <b>harvesting as well,</b> as if <b>one found</b> the barley already <b>harvested, he</b> need <b>not harvest. And if it enters your mind</b> that the reference in the verse is to plowing and harvesting for the purpose of <b>a mitzva,</b> is it so that if <b>one found</b> the barley already <b>harvested, he</b> need <b>not harvest?</b> There is <b>a mitzva to harvest and bring</b> the barley for the <i>omer</i> offering. The prohibition in the verse applies only to harvesting that is optional.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The father is exiled</b> to a city of refuge <b>due to</b> his unintentional murder of his <b>son. And the son is exiled due to</b> his unintentional murder of his <b>father. Everyone is exiled due to</b> their unintentional murder of <b>a Jew, and a Jew is exiled due to</b> his unintentional murder of any of <b>them, except for</b> the unintentional murder of <b>a gentile who resides in Eretz Yisrael and observes the seven Noahide mitzvot [<i>ger toshav</i>]. And a <i>ger toshav</i> is exiled only due to</b> his unintentional murder of <b>a <i>ger toshav</i>.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches: <b>The father is exiled</b> to a city of refuge <b>due to</b> his unintentional murder of his <b>son.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t you say</b> in the previous mishna: <b>To exclude a father who strikes his son,</b> who is not exiled? The Gemara answers: This mishna is referring to a son <b>who is learned,</b> and there is no mitzva to strike him; therefore, since the striking is optional, the father is exiled. The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t you say: Even if</b> the son <b>is learned,</b> the father <b>performs a mitzva</b> by striking his son? The Gemara answers: This ruling of the mishna is stated <b>with regard to a carpenter’s apprentice [<i>bishevaleya</i>].</b> Since the father is teaching his son carpentry, not Torah, there is no mitzva to strike him to spur him to study.
The Gemara challenges: In the case of <b>a carpenter’s apprentice,</b> his father <b>is teaching him a livelihood,</b> which is also a mitzva. The Gemara answers: The reference is to a son <b>who has</b> already <b>learned another craft,</b> and since he is able to earn his livelihood there is no mitzva to teach him a second craft.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And the son is exiled due to</b> his unintentional murder of his <b>father. And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a <i>baraita</i>. It is written: “And a murderer shall flee there, one who strikes a person unintentionally” (Numbers 35:11), from which it is derived that <b>one who strikes a person</b> is exiled. This serves <b>to exclude one who strikes his father,</b> whose punishment for doing so intentionally is more severe than that of the standard intentional murderer, and therefore the son is not subject to exile. <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> This is <b>not difficult; this</b> <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, and that</b> mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> Rabbi Shimon and the Rabbis disagree (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 50a) whether a son who kills his father intentionally is executed by strangulation or by the sword.
<b>According to Rabbi Shimon, who says: Strangulation</b> is more <b>severe than</b> execution by the <b>sword,</b> which is why a son who kills his father is liable to be executed by strangulation, one who commits the <b>unwitting</b> violation of a prohibition generally punishable by execution by the <b>sword,</b> i.e., intentional murder of one who is not a parent, <b>is eligible for atonement</b> by means of exile; one who commits the <b>unwitting</b> violation of a prohibition generally punishable by the more severe death of <b>strangulation,</b> i.e., intentional murder of a parent, <b>is not eligible for atonement</b> by means of exile.
By contrast, <b>according to the Rabbis, who say:</b> Execution by the <b>sword</b> is more <b>severe than strangulation,</b> which is why a son who kills his father is liable to be executed by the sword, a son who <b>unintentionally kills his father</b> has performed <b>an unwitting</b> violation of a prohibition generally punishable by execution by the <b>sword. And</b> one who commits <b>an unwitting</b> violation of a prohibition generally punishable by execution by the <b>sword,</b> i.e., intentional murder of one who is not a parent, <b>is eligible for atonement</b> by means of exile.
<b>Rava says:</b> That which was derived in the <i>baraita</i> that one who strikes a person is exiled, to exclude one who strikes his father, who is not exiled, is not referring to a son who unintentionally kills his father, as in that case the son is certainly exiled. Rather, it is <b>to exclude one</b> who <b>inflicts a wound on his father unwittingly.</b> The novel element of this derivation is that <b>it enters your mind to say: Since</b> in a case <b>where</b> he wounded his father <b>intentionally, he is liable to be executed</b> like a murderer (see Exodus 21:15), if he did so <b>unwittingly, let him also be exiled</b> like an unintentional murderer. Therefore, this derivation <b>teaches us</b> that exile is administered specifically to an unintentional murderer, but it is not administered to the unwitting performer of any other transgression.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>Everyone is exiled due to</b> their unintentional murder of <b>a Jew,</b> and a Jew is exiled due to his unintentional murder of any of them. The Gemara asks: <b>Everyone is exiled due to</b> their unintentional murder of <b>a Jew: What</b> case that was not already specified does this phrase serve <b>to add?</b> The Gemara answers: It serves <b>to add</b> a Canaanite <b>slave and a Samaritan.</b> The Gemara notes: <b>We learn</b> by inference from this mishna <b>that which the Sages taught</b> explicitly in a <i>baraita</i>: A Canaanite <b>slave and a Samaritan are exiled and flogged due to a Jew, and a Jew is exiled and flogged due to a Samaritan and</b> a Canaanite <b>slave.</b>
The Gemara analyzes the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Granted, a slave and a Samaritan are exiled and flogged due to a Jew;</b> either of them is <b>exiled</b> in a case <b>where he killed</b> a Jew unintentionally, <b>and flogged</b> in a case <b>where he cursed</b> the Jew, thereby violating a Torah prohibition. <b>But</b> with regard to the <i>halakha</i> that <b>a Jew is exiled and flogged due to a Samaritan, granted,</b> the Jew is <b>exiled</b> in a case <b>where</b> he <b>killed</b> the Samaritan unintentionally, <b>but</b> the Jew <b>is flogged for</b> violating <b>what</b> prohibition? <b>For cursing</b> the Samaritan? Isn’t that prohibition derived from the verse: <b>“And a leader in your people you may not curse”</b> (Exodus 22:27), from which it is inferred that the prohibition is in effect only <b>with regard to</b> one who <b>performs an action</b> characteristic <b>of your people,</b> who comports himself as an observant, God-fearing Jew? Even if the conversion of the Samaritans was valid, they regularly violate numerous prohibitions, and they do not comport themselves as observant Jews.
<b>Rather, Rav Aḥa bar Ya’akov said:</b> This <i>baraita</i> is not referring to lashes administered for cursing; rather, one is flogged in a case <b>where</b> a Jew <b>testified against</b> a Samaritan and a Samaritan testified against a Jew <b>and he was rendered a conspiring witness.</b> The Gemara asks: Does that mean that <b>in the corresponding</b> situation <b>with regard to</b> a Canaanite <b>slave,</b> a slave is flogged in a case <b>where</b> he <b>testified against</b> a Jew <b>and was rendered a conspiring witness? Is a slave eligible to</b> give <b>testimony? Rather, Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, said: What are we dealing with here?</b> The Jew is flogged due to a slave and a Samaritan, and the slave and the Samaritan are flogged due to the Jew in a case <b>where one struck</b> the other with <b>a blow</b>

Daf 9a

<b>that does not have</b> the capacity to cause damage <b>worth one <i>peruta</i>, as Rabbi Ami says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> Although one who strikes another pays damages and is not flogged, if <b>he struck him</b> with <b>a blow that does not have</b> the capacity to cause damage <b>worth one <i>peruta</i>,</b> since there is no payment, <b>he is flogged</b> for violating a Torah prohibition. The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> according to this opinion, contrary to those who hold that based on their virtual juxtaposition (see Exodus 21:15, 17) or on an analogy between them, the <i>halakhot</i> of striking and cursing are identical, <b>we do not liken striking to cursing.</b> Therefore, although one is flogged for cursing another only if the other comports himself as an observant, God-fearing Jew, one is liable for striking another even if the other was a sinner, e.g., a Samaritan.
§ The mishna teaches: Everyone is exiled due to their unintentional murder of a Jew, and a Jew is exiled due to all of them, <b>except for</b> when it is <b>due to a <i>ger toshav</i>.</b> And a <i>ger toshav</i> is exiled due to his unintentional murder of a <i>ger toshav</i>. The Gemara comments: <b>Apparently,</b> one may conclude that <b>a <i>ger toshav</i> is a gentile,</b> and therefore he is not exiled when he unintentionally kills a Jew. <b>Say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: <b>A <i>ger toshav</i> is exiled due to</b> his unintentional murder of <b>a <i>ger toshav</i>,</b> indicating that his halakhic status is not that of a gentile, as gentiles are not liable to be exiled. There is an apparent contradiction between the two clauses in the mishna. <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> This is <b>not difficult. Here,</b> in the latter clause of the mishna, it is <b>in</b> the case of <b>a <i>ger toshav</i> who killed a <i>ger toshav</i></b> that he is exiled; <b>there,</b> in the first clause, it is <b>in</b> the case of <b>a <i>ger toshav</i> who killed a Jew.</b> In the case described in the first clause he is not exiled, as his halakhic status is not that of a Jew, for whom the sin of unintentional murder of a Jew can be atoned through exile.
<b>There are</b> those <b>who raised a contradiction between</b> two <b>verses. It is written: “For the children of Israel and for the stranger and for the resident [<i>velatoshav</i>] among them, shall</b> these <b>six cities be</b> for refuge” (Numbers 35:15), indicating that a <i>ger toshav</i> is exiled. <b>And it is written</b> in a previous verse: <b>“And the cities shall be for you for refuge”</b> (Numbers 35:12), from which it is inferred: A refuge <b>for you but not for a <i>ger</i></b> <i>toshav</i>, indicating that a <i>ger toshav</i> is not exiled. <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> This is <b>not difficult.
