The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to this from the mishna’s interpretation of the term: “That he fled <b>there”</b> (Numbers 35:25), from which it is derived: <b>There shall be his dwelling, there shall be his death, there shall be his burial.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>A murderer is different, as the Merciful One revealed concerning him</b> that he is to be buried there. That does not apply to the other residents of the city.
The mishna teaches: <b>Just as</b> an unintentional murderer <b>is admitted</b> to <b>the city</b> of refuge, so is he admitted to its outskirts, located within the Shabbat boundary. <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a <i>baraita</i> with regard to that which is written concerning the unintentional murderer: <b>“And he shall dwell in it”</b> (Numbers 35:25), from which it is inferred: <b>“In it,” but not within its boundary. Abaye said:</b> This is <b>not difficult. Here,</b> the mishna is referring to the unintentional murderer being <b>admitted</b> to the city, which will provide refuge from the blood redeemer, who may not kill him there. <b>There,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring to the place where it is permitted for the murderer <b>to dwell,</b> i.e., within the city itself and not on its outskirts.
The Gemara asks: Why is it necessary to state that the murderer may not <b>dwell</b> on the outskirts of the city? <b>Let him derive</b> that <i>halakha</i> from the fact <b>that one may not render the field</b> of a Levite city <b>an open space, nor an open space a field, nor an open space</b> part of the <b>city, nor</b> the <b>city an open space.</b> Apparently, the outskirts of the city, whose status is that of an open space, may not be utilized for residential purposes. <b>Rav Sheshet said: It is necessary</b> to state this <i>halakha</i> <b>only for tunnels.</b> If a murderer excavated a tunnel on the outskirts of the city, although he did not violate the prohibition against ruining the fields of the city, he may not reside there based on the <i>halakha</i> by Torah law that he must reside inside the city.
§ The mishna teaches a dispute between Rabbi Yosei HaGelili and Rabbi Akiva with regard to a case <b>where</b> the unintentional <b>murderer emerged beyond the</b> Shabbat <b>boundary</b> of the city of refuge, and the blood redeemer found him there. <b>The Sages taught</b> that it is written: “And the blood redeemer finds him outside the border of his city of refuge <b>and the blood redeemer murders the murderer,</b> he has no blood” (Numbers 35:27): It is <b>a mitzva for the blood redeemer</b> to kill him, and if <b>there is no blood redeemer</b> available to fulfill this mitzva, <b>it is optional for any person</b> to do so; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili. Rabbi Akiva says:</b> It is <b>optional for the blood redeemer</b> to kill him, <b>and any</b> other <b>person is liable for</b> killing <b>him.</b>
The Gemara analyzes the mishna: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili?</b> He says: <b>Is it written: If</b> the blood redeemer <b>murders</b> the unintentional murderer, he has no blood? It states: “And the blood redeemer murders the murderer,” indicating that it is a mitzva. <b>And Rabbi Akiva</b> says: <b>Is it written:</b> The blood redeemer <b>shall murder,</b> in the imperative? It merely states: “And the blood redeemer murders,” which is merely relating the scenario under discussion.
On a related note, the Gemara cites that <b>Mar Zutra bar Toviyya says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> In the case of <b>a murderer who emerged beyond the</b> Shabbat <b>boundary</b> of the city of refuge, <b>and the blood redeemer found him</b> there <b>and killed him,</b> the blood redeemer is <b>executed for</b> killing <b>him.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion did Rav issue this ruling? He issued it <b>neither in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili,</b> who maintains that there is a mitzva for the blood redeemer to kill him, <b>nor in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> who says the blood redeemer has the option of killing him.
The Gemara answers: Rav <b>states</b> this ruling <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>that <i>tanna</i>, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says</b> with regard to the verse: “And the murderer shall not die, <b>until he stands before the congregation for judgment”</b> (Numbers 35:12): <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state</b> this? It is necessary <b>since it is stated:</b> “And the blood redeemer finds him…<b>and the blood redeemer murders the murderer”</b> (Numbers 35:27). One <b>might</b> have thought that the blood redeemer may murderer him <b>immediately;</b> therefore, <b>the verse states: “Until he stands before the congregation for judgment,”</b> from which it is derived that the blood redeemer may kill the murderer after only he is convicted in court. Mar Zutra bar Toviyya says that Rav says that the blood redeemer is liable if he kills the murderer before he is convicted.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> as for <b>Rabbi Yosei</b> HaGelili <b>and Rabbi Akiva,</b> with regard to <b>this</b> verse: <b>“Until he stands before the congregation for judgment,” what do</b> they <b>derive from it?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> verse <b>is necessary for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: From where</b> is it derived in the case of <b>a Sanhedrin that saw one kill a person that</b> they <b>may not execute him until he stands</b> trial <b>in a different court?</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as the verse states: “Until he stands before the congregation for judgment,”</b> meaning: <b>Until he stands</b> before <b>a different court.</b> Since they themselves witnessed the murder, they are no longer capable of considering the possibility that he may be innocent.
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse: <b>“And if the murderer emerges [<i>yatzo yetze</i>]</b> …and the blood redeemer murders the murderer, he has no blood” (Numbers 35:26–27): <b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that the blood redeemer may kill the unintentional murderer if the unintentional murderer emerges from the city <b>intentionally. From where</b> is it derived that the same applies if he emerges <b>unwittingly?</b> It is derived from this verse, as <b>the verse states: “If <i>yatzo yetze</i>”;</b> the doubled form of the verb serves to teach that this <i>halakha</i> applies <b>in any case</b> where the unintentional murderer emerges from the city of refuge.
The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to an unintentional murderer who emerged from the city of refuge unwittingly: <b>And one who kills him intentionally is executed,</b> and one who kills him <b>unintentionally is exiled?</b> The Gemara answers: This is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> second <i>baraita</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the one who says</b> that <b>we say: The Torah spoke in the language of people,</b> and no <i>halakha</i> is derived from the doubled form of the verb: <i>Yatzo yetze</i>, as it is merely a rhetorical flourish, and <b>that</b> first <i>baraita</i> <b>is in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the one who says</b> that <b>we do not say: The Torah spoke in the language of people,</b> and the compound verb was employed in order to derive that the blood redeemer may kill the unintentional murderer even if he emerged from the city of refuge unwittingly.
<b>Abaye said: It stands to reason</b> that the <i>halakha</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the one who says: The Torah spoke in the language of people,</b> and the blood redeemer is liable for killing an unintentional murderer who emerged from the city of refuge unwittingly, in order to ensure <b>that</b> the <b>ultimate</b> punishment of the unintentional murderer, when he emerges from the city of refuge, <b>will not be more severe than his initial</b> punishment, when he is sentenced in court. <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>his initial</b> punishment for murder, if he killed <b>intentionally he is executed,</b> and if he killed <b>unintentionally he is exiled, so too,</b> with regard to <b>his ultimate</b> punishment, if he emerges from the city of refuge <b>intentionally he is killed</b> by the blood redeemer, and if he emerges <b>unwittingly he is</b> returned to <b>exile</b> in the city of refuge.
§ It <b>is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a father who killed</b> his son, <b>his</b> surviving <b>son becomes his blood redeemer</b> and may kill him. <b>And it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: <b>His son does not become his blood redeemer.</b> The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i>, which states that his son does become his blood redeemer, is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili.</b> Since there is a mitzva for the blood redeemer to kill the murderer, this mitzva applies equally to a son. <b>And that</b> <i>baraita</i>, which states that a son does not become a blood redeemer to kill his father, is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> who holds that the blood redeemer has only the option, not a mitzva, to kill the murderer.
The Gemara rejects that understanding. <b>And</b> how can <b>you understand</b> it in that manner? <b>Both according to the one who says</b> that there is <b>a mitzva</b> for the blood redeemer to kill the unintentional murderer <b>and according to the one who says</b> that it is <b>optional, is it permitted</b> for a son to do so? <b>But doesn’t Rabba bar Rav Huna say, and likewise the school of Rabbi Yishmael taught: With regard to all</b> transgressions of the Torah, even if the father is liable to receive lashes or be ostracized, <b>a son does not become an agent</b> of the court <b>to flog his father or to curse him, apart from</b> the case of a father who acted as <b>one who incites</b> others to engage in idol worship, <b>as the Torah states</b> in his regard: <b>“You shall neither spare nor conceal him”</b> (Deuteronomy 13:9)?
<b>Rather,</b> the Gemara suggests that the apparent contradiction between the two <i>baraitot</i> is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i>, which says that the son does not become a blood redeemer to kill his father, is referring <b>to his son, and that</b> <i>baraita</i>, which says that the son does become a blood redeemer, is referring <b>to the son of his son,</b> who can become a blood redeemer to kill his grandfather, as the grandson is not required to honor his grandfather as he is required to honor his father.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> The previous mishna teaches that the halakhic status of the outskirts of the city is like that of the city itself in terms of the unintentional murderer being provided refuge there. The mishna adds: With regard to <b>a tree that stands within the</b> Shabbat <b>boundary</b> of a city of refuge, <b>whose boughs extend outside the boundary, or</b> a tree that <b>stands outside the boundary and its boughs extend inside the boundary,</b> the status of the tree, whether it is considered inside or outside the boundary, in <b>all</b> cases <b>follows the boughs.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a mishna (<i>Ma’aser Sheni</i> 3:7) taught with regard to second tithe. Second-tithe produce must be consumed within Jerusalem or redeemed outside of Jerusalem: With regard to <b>a tree that stands within</b> Jerusalem, <b>and</b> whose boughs <b>extend outside</b> the city wall, <b>or</b> a tree that <b>stands outside</b> the city wall <b>and</b> whose boughs <b>extend inside</b> the wall, the principle is: The halakhic status of any part of the tree that is <b>above the wall and inward</b> is that of an area <b>within</b> the wall and the halakhic status of any part of the tree that is <b>above the wall and outward</b> is that of an area <b>outside</b> the wall. Apparently, the trunk does not follow the boughs and the boughs do not follow the trunk. The status of each part of the tree is determined by its position relative to the wall.
The Gemara rejects the parallel between the cases. <b>Are you raising a contradiction</b> between the <i>halakha</i> of second <b>tithe and</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>cities of refuge?</b> With regard to the <i>halakha</i> of second <b>tithe, the Merciful One made</b> the status of the tree <b>dependent on the wall,</b> and with regard to <b>cities of refuge, the Merciful One made</b> the status of the tree <b>dependent on dwelling. One can dwell in its boughs,</b> but <b>one cannot dwell in its trunk.</b> Therefore, with regard to cities of refuge, the halakhic status of the tree is determined by the boughs.
<b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> between the previous <i>baraita</i> with regard to the <i>halakha</i> of second <b>tithe and</b> another <i>baraita</i> with regard to the <i>halakha</i> of second <b>tithe, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (see <i>Ma’asrot</i> 3:10): <b>In Jerusalem, follow the boughs</b> in determining the status of the tree with regard to consuming second-tithe produce, and likewise, <b>with regard to cities of refuge, follow the boughs</b> in determining the status of the tree with regard to providing refuge for an unintentional murderer. <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> This apparent contradiction is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, and that</b> <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to partaking of second tithe in Jerusalem:

Daf 12b

<b>Rabbi Yehuda says: With regard to a cave, follow its entrance;</b> if the entrance is inside the city, the status of the entire cave is that of part of the city, and one may partake of tithes in it. <b>With regard to a tree, follow its boughs.</b> The <i>baraita</i> that states that with regard to second tithe in Jerusalem and cities of refuge one follows the boughs is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda.
The Gemara rejects that explanation. <b>Say that you heard Rabbi Yehuda</b> express this opinion <b>with regard to</b> second-<b>tithe</b> produce in a situation where his ruling is <b>a stringency,</b> as in the case where the <b>trunk</b> of a tree <b>is outside</b> Jerusalem <b>and its boughs are inside</b> Jerusalem, <b>just as among its boughs, one may not redeem</b> second-tithe produce, and he must partake of it in Jerusalem, <b>so too at its trunk he may not redeem</b> second-tithe produce, even though it stands outside of Jerusalem. So too in a case where the <b>trunk</b> of a tree <b>is inside and its boughs outside,</b> there is a stringency: <b>Just as among its boughs, one may not partake</b> of second-tithe produce <b>without redemption, so too at its trunk he may not partake</b> of second-tithe produce <b>without redemption,</b> even though it stands inside Jerusalem.
<b>But with regard to cities of refuge,</b> it may be otherwise: <b>Granted,</b> if <b>its trunk</b> is <b>outside</b> the boundary <b>and its boughs</b> are <b>inside, just as among its boughs,</b> the blood redeemer <b>may not kill</b> the unintentional murderer, <b>so too at its trunk, he may not kill him. But</b> if <b>its trunk</b> was <b>inside and its boughs outside,</b> would one say that <b>just as among its boughs,</b> the blood redeemer <b>may kill him, at its trunk, he may also kill him? Isn’t</b> the unintentional murderer <b>standing inside</b> the city of refuge? How could one say that it is permitted for the blood redeemer to kill him inside the city?
<b>Rava said</b> that it can be explained as follows: In the case where its trunk is inside the boundary and its boughs outside, and the unintentional murderer was standing <b>at its trunk, everyone agrees that</b> the blood redeemer <b>may not kill</b> him, and when Rabbi Yehuda said that the trunk follows the boughs, he did not intend to include that case. If the murderer <b>is standing among the boughs</b> of the tree, <b>and</b> the blood redeemer <b>is able to kill him with arrows and pebbles, everyone,</b> including the Rabbis, <b>agrees that</b> the blood redeemer <b>may kill him,</b> as the boughs are outside the city.
<b>When they disagree is with regard to</b> whether <b>its trunk can become a step for its boughs,</b> enabling the blood redeemer to gain access to the unintentional murderer there. One <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>holds: Its trunk can become a step for its boughs;</b> the blood redeemer may gain access to the boughs extending outside the boundary and kill the unintentional murderer by climbing the trunk inside the city. It was in that context that Rabbi Yehuda says that the trunk follows the boughs. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the Rabbis, <b>holds: Its trunk cannot become a step for its boughs.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi said: What</b> is the meaning of Rabbi Yehuda’s statement: <b>Follow its boughs?</b> It does not mean that the location of the boughs is the only determining factor; rather, it means that in addition to the trunk, <b>follow the boughs as well</b> in a case where it is a stringency. Therefore, with regard to a city of refuge a tree whose trunk is inside the boundary and its boughs extend beyond the boundary, the halakhic status of the boughs is the same as what it would be were they inside the boundary.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If an unintentional murderer, exiled to a city of refuge, unintentionally <b>killed</b> a person <b>in the same city,</b> he <b>is exiled from</b> that <b>neighborhood</b> where he resided <b>to</b> another <b>neighborhood</b> within that city. <b>And a Levite</b> who is a permanent resident of a city of refuge and unintentionally killed a person <b>is exiled from</b> that <b>city to</b> another <b>city.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> Apropos the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna that a Levite is exiled from one city to another city, the Gemara cites that which <b>the Sages taught</b> with regard to the verse: “And one who did not lie in wait…<b>and I will appoint for you a place</b> where he may flee” (Exodus 21:13). <b>“And I will appoint for you”;</b> God said to Moses: There will be a place that provides refuge for unintentional murderers already <b>during your lifetime. “A place”;</b> it will be <b>from your place,</b> meaning the Levite camp served as the place that provided refuge in the wilderness. <b>“Where he may flee”;</b> this <b>teaches that Israel would exile</b> unintentional murderers <b>in the wilderness</b> as well, before they entered the land. <b>To where</b> did <b>they exile</b> unintentional murderers when they were in the wilderness? They exiled them <b>to the Levite camp,</b> which provided refuge.
<b>From here</b> the Sages <b>said: A Levite who killed</b> unintentionally <b>is exiled from</b> one <b>district to</b> another <b>district,</b> to a different Levite city in the other district. <b>And if he was exiled to</b> a city in <b>his</b> own <b>district, he is admitted to</b> the city in <b>his district,</b> which provides him with refuge. <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, said: What is the verse</b> from which it is derived that a murderer who unintentionally killed in the city of refuge where he was exiled is exiled to another neighborhood in that same city? It is derived from a verse, as it is stated: <b>“For in his city of refuge he shall dwell”</b> (Numbers 35:28), indicating that it is <b>a city</b> in <b>which he was already admitted,</b> as the verse is referring to it as his city, and he shall continue to reside there as well.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Similarly,</b> in the case of <b>a murderer who was exiled to a city of refuge and the people of the city sought to honor him</b> due to his prominence, <b>he shall say to them: I am a murderer.</b> If the residents of the city <b>say to him:</b> We are aware of your status and <b>nevertheless,</b> we wish to honor you, <b>he may accept</b> the honor <b>from them, as it is stated: “And this is the matter [<i>devar</i>] of the murderer”</b> (Deuteronomy 19:4), from which it is derived that the murderer is required to say [<i>ledabber</i>] to them that he is a murderer. He is not required to tell them any more than that.

Daf 13a

The unintentional murderers <b>would pay a fee to the Levites</b> as rent for their living quarters in the cities of refuge, which were Levite cities; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Meir says: They would not pay a fee to them,</b> but would reside rent free, as they are required to live there by Torah law. They also disagreed with regard to the status of the unintentional murderer when he returns home after the death of the High Priest. <b>He returns to the</b> same public <b>office that he occupied</b> prior to his exile; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yehuda says: He does not return to the office that he occupied.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> This <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Meir is with regard to payment of rent to the Levite landlords <b>in</b> the <b>six</b> cities of refuge designated in the Torah and in the book of Joshua, <b>as</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>holds</b> that in the verse: “They shall be cities of refuge for you” (Numbers 35:11), the term <b>“for you”</b> means that the cities shall be for you only <b>for providing refuge,</b> and therefore they must pay rent to the Levites. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>holds</b> that the term <b>“for you”</b> means <b>for all your needs;</b> therefore, they are not required to pay rent. <b>But with regard to the forty-two</b> additional Levite cities, which also served as cities of refuge, <b>everyone agrees</b> that the unintentional murderers <b>would pay rent to</b> the Levite landlords.
<b>Rava said to him: But</b> the term <b>“for you” certainly indicates for all your needs;</b> therefore, the dispute cannot be as Rav Kahana explains it. <b>Rather, Rava said:</b> The <b>dispute</b> is only <b>with regard to</b> the <b>forty-two</b> Levite cities, <b>as</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Yehuda, <b>holds</b> that from the verse: “They shall be the six cities of refuge…<b>and beside them you shall give</b> forty-two cities” (Numbers 35:6), it is derived that the forty-two cities are <b>like these</b> original six cities, only insofar as <b>with regard to</b> the unintentional murderer being <b>admitted. And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> Rabbi Meir, <b>holds</b> that from the verse: “They shall be the six cities of refuge…<b>and beside them you shall give</b> forty-two cities,” it is derived that the forty-two cities are <b>like these</b> original six cities in every sense: <b>Just as those</b> six cities were given to you, i.e., the unintentional murderers, <b>for all your needs, so too, these</b> forty-two cities were given to you, i.e., the unintentional murderers, <b>for all your needs. But with regard to</b> the <b>six</b> cities specifically designated as cities of refuge, <b>everyone agrees</b> that unintentional murderers <b>would not pay</b> the Levites <b>a fee.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that there is a dispute as to whether the unintentional murderer <b>returns to the</b> same public <b>office that he occupied</b> prior to his exile. On a related note, <b>the Sages taught</b> with regard to a Hebrew slave liberated during the Jubilee Year, about whom it is written: <b>“And he returns to his family, and to the estate of his fathers he shall return”</b> (Leviticus 25:41): <b>He returns to his family, but he does not return to that</b> status of prominence and honor <b>that his ancestors held;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Meir says: He even returns to that</b> status of prominence and honor <b>that his ancestors held.</b> From the phrase <b>“to the estate of his fathers</b> he shall return,” it is derived that he returns to be <b>like his fathers.</b>
<b>And likewise,</b> the same is true <b>with regard to an exile</b> sent to a city of refuge, as <b>when</b> the verse <b>states:</b> “To the estate of his fathers <b>he shall return,”</b> the term “he shall return” is redundant and it serves <b>to include the</b> unintentional <b>murderer.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of: <b>And likewise,</b> the same is true <b>with regard to an exile?</b> The Gemara explains: It is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the verse: <b>“The murderer shall return to his ancestral land”</b> (Numbers 35:28), from which it is derived that <b>he returns to his ancestral land, but he does not return to that</b> status of prominence and honor <b>that his ancestors held;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda. Rabbi Meir says: He even returns to that</b> status of prominence and honor <b>that his ancestors held.</b> Rabbi Meir <b>derives</b> this by means of a verbal analogy <b>from there,</b> i.e., between the term of <b>“return”</b> written with regard to the unintentional murderer, and the term of <b>“return”</b> written with regard to the Hebrew slave. The verbal analogy teaches that just as a Hebrew slave returns to his father’s estate and the status of prominence held by his ancestors, so too, the unintentional murderer returns to his ancestral land and to the status of prominence held by his ancestors.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> After enumerating in tractate <i>Sanhedrin</i> those liable to be executed and in the previous chapter those liable to be exiled, the mishna proceeds to enumerate those liable to receive lashes. <b>These are</b> the people <b>who are flogged</b> by Torah law for violating a prohibition: <b>One who engages in intercourse with his sister, or with his father’s sister, or with his mother’s sister, or with his wife’s sister, or with his brother’s wife, or with the wife of his father’s brother, or with a menstruating woman.</b> Likewise, one is flogged in the case of <b>a widow</b> who married <b>a High Priest, a divorcée or a <i>ḥalutza</i></b> who married <b>an ordinary priest, a <i>mamzeret</i>,</b> i.e., a daughter born from an incestuous or adulterous relationship, <b>or a Gibeonite woman</b> who married <b>a Jew</b> of unflawed lineage, and <b>a Jewish woman</b> of unflawed lineage who married <b>a Gibeonite or a <i>mamzer</i>,</b> i.e., a son born from an incestuous or adulterous relationship.
The mishna elaborates: If a woman was both <b>a widow and a divorcée,</b> as after she was widowed she remarried and was divorced, a High Priest <b>is liable</b> to receive two sets of lashes <b>for</b> marrying <b>her due to</b> the violation of <b>two</b> different <b>prohibitions,</b> that of his marrying a widow and that of his marrying a divorced woman. If a woman was both <b>a divorcée and a <i>ḥalutza</i>,</b> from two different men, an ordinary priest who marries her <b>is liable</b> to receive <b>only</b> one set of lashes, <b>due to</b> the violation of <b>one</b> prohibition <b>alone.</b>
The mishna continues enumerating those liable to receive lashes: <b>A ritually impure person who ate sacrificial</b> food <b>and one who entered the Temple</b> while <b>ritually impure. And one</b> who <b>eats</b> the forbidden <b>fat</b> of a domesticated animal; <b>or blood; or <i>notar</i>,</b> leftover flesh from an offering after the time allotted for its consumption; <b>or <i>piggul</i>,</b> an offering invalidated due to intent to sprinkle its blood, burn its fats on the altar, or consume it, beyond its designated time; <b>or</b> one who partakes of an offering that became <b>impure,</b> is flogged.
<b>And one who slaughters</b> a sacrificial animal <b>or sacrifices</b> it on an altar <b>outside</b> the Temple courtyard, <b>and one who eats leavened bread on Passover, and one who eats</b> on Yom Kippur <b>and one who performs labor on Yom Kippur, and one who blends the</b> anointing <b>oil</b> for non-sacred use, <b>and one who blends the incense</b> that was burned on the altar in the Sanctuary for non-sacred use, <b>and one who applies the anointing oil, and one who eats unslaughtered</b> animal or bird <b>carcasses, or <i>tereifot</i>,</b> which are animals or birds with a condition that will lead to their death within twelve months, or <b>repugnant creatures, or creeping animals,</b> is liable to receive lashes.
If <b>one ate untithed produce,</b> i.e., produce from which <i>terumot</i> and tithes were not separated; <b>or first-tithe</b> produce <b>whose <i>teruma</i></b> of the tithe <b>was not taken; or second-tithe</b> produce <b>or sacrificial</b> food <b>that was not redeemed;</b> he is liable to receive lashes.
With regard to the measure for liability for eating forbidden food, the mishna asks: <b>How much does one</b> need to <b>eat from untithed produce and be liable</b> to receive lashes? <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> If one ate <b>any amount</b> of untithed produce he is liable to receive lashes. <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> He is liable only if he eats <b>an olive-bulk,</b> which is the minimum measure characterized as eating. <b>Rabbi Shimon said to them: Do you not concede to me with regard to one who eats an ant of any size that he is liable</b> to receive lashes? The Rabbis <b>said to</b> Rabbi Shimon: He receives lashes for eating an ant of any size <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that it is</b> an intact entity in the form <b>of its creation,</b> and that is what the Torah prohibited. Rabbi Shimon <b>said to them: One</b> kernel of <b>wheat</b> is <b>also</b> in the form <b>of its creation,</b> and therefore one should be liable to receive lashes for eating any intact entity.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> Apropos the list in the mishna of those liable to receive lashes, the Gemara notes: The <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> those <b>liable to</b> receive <b>excision from the World-to-Come [<i>karet</i>],</b> as most of the cases enumerated at the beginning of the mishna include actions that not only entail violation of a prohibition but are also punishable by <i>karet</i>. But the <i>tanna</i> <b>does not teach</b> those <b>liable to</b> be executed with <b>court</b>-imposed <b>death</b> penalties among those liable to receive lashes. Apparently, lashes are not administered to those who violate a prohibition punishable by execution. The Gemara asks: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in <b>the mishna? It</b> is the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that there is a tannaitic dispute: <b>Both those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i> and those liable to</b> be executed with <b>court</b>-imposed <b>death</b> penalties

Daf 13b

<b>are</b> included <b>in the category</b> of those liable to receive <b>forty lashes</b> for violating a Torah prohibition. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yishmael.</b>
<b>Rabbi Akiva says: Those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i> are</b> included <b>in the category</b> of those liable to receive <b>forty lashes, because if they repented, the heavenly court absolves them</b> of the punishment of <i>karet</i>. Therefore, <i>karet</i> does not absolve them of the punishment of lashes. <b>Those liable to</b> be executed with <b>court</b>-imposed <b>death</b> penalties <b>are not</b> included <b>in the category</b> of those liable to receive <b>forty lashes, as</b> even <b>if they repented, the earthly court does not absolve them</b> of execution; and one is not punished by the court twice for performing the same transgression.
<b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says</b> that like those liable to be executed, those liable to receive <i>karet</i> are not flogged. <b>Those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i></b> for incest <b>were</b> included <b>in</b> the <b>generalization:</b> “For anyone who performs any of these abominations, the souls who do so shall be excised [<i>venikhretu</i>] from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). Included in that category is one who engages in intercourse with his sister. <b>And why</b> then did the <i>halakha</i> of <b><i>karet</i> with regard to</b> intercourse with <b>one’s sister emerge</b> from the generalization and receive specific mention: “And a man who takes his sister…and they shall be excised before the eyes of the children of their people” (Leviticus 20:17)? It is in order <b>to sentence one</b> who engages in intercourse with his sister <b>with <i>karet</i>, and not with lashes.</b> This is the source for the opinion that those liable to receive <i>karet</i> are not flogged.
The Gemara elaborates: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yishmael,</b> who holds that even those liable to be executed are liable to receive lashes? It is <b>as it is written: “If you will not observe to perform all the matters of this Torah”</b> (Deuteronomy 28:58), <b>and it is written</b> immediately thereafter, with regard to the punishment of one who violates that verse: <b>“And God will make your plagues wondrous [<i>vehifla</i>]”</b> (Deuteronomy 28:59). The Sages interpreted that verse: <b>This</b> term, <b><i>hafla’a</i>, I do not know what its</b> meaning <b>is. When</b> the verse <b>states</b> concerning those liable to receive lashes: <b>“And the judge shall cause him to lie down [<i>vehippilo</i>] and to be beaten before him”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), <b>you must say</b> that <b>this <i>hafla’a</i> is</b> a term referring to <b>lashes, and it is written</b> that this <i>hafla’a</i> is administered in any case where one does not fulfill the verse <b>“if you will not observe to perform all</b> the matters of this Torah,” including those prohibitions punishable by death.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> and Rabbi Yishmael interprets the verses in that manner, <b>those liable</b> for failing to perform <b>positive</b> mitzvot should <b>also</b> be flogged, as those mitzvot too are included in “all the matters of this Torah.” The Gemara answers: Positive mitzvot are not included in this phrase, because <b>“if you will not observe” is written, and</b> this is <b>in accordance with</b> the principle <b>that Rabbi Avin</b> says that <b>Rabbi Elai says, as Rabbi Avin says</b> that <b>Rabbi Elai says: Wherever it is stated</b> in the Torah the terms: <b>Observe, lest, or do not, it is nothing other than a prohibition.</b> Since in this verse “if you will not observe” is written, only those who violate prohibitions are liable to receive lashes.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> one should <b>also</b> be flogged for violating <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action,</b> but the <i>halakha</i> is that one is not flogged in that case. The Gemara rejects that contention: “If you will not observe <b>to perform” is written,</b> and in violating a prohibition that does not involve an action, one performed no action.
The Gemara objects: One should <b>also</b> be flogged for violating <b>a prohibition that entails</b> fulfillment <b>of a positive</b> mitzva, where the Torah provides the means to rectify the transgression through performance of a positive mitzva, as it does involve an action. Yet the <i>halakha</i> is that one is not flogged for violating that type of prohibition. The Gemara rejects that contention: One is not flogged in that case because lashes are administered only for violation of prohibitions <b>similar to the prohibition of muzzling</b> an ox while it is threshing grain (see Deuteronomy 25:4). The Torah juxtaposed that prohibition to the <i>halakhot</i> of lashes in the same passage, from which it is derived that lashes are administered only for violations similar to muzzling, i.e., a prohibition that does not entail fulfillment of a positive mitzva.
The Gemara comments: <b>Now that you have arrived at this</b> understanding, <b>all</b> the previous objections can <b>also</b> be resolved in the same manner: No lashes are administered for failure to fulfill a positive mitzva or for violation of a prohibition that does not entail an action, because one is flogged for violation only of prohibitions <b>similar to the prohibition of muzzling,</b> which is a prohibition and involves an action.
The Gemara continues its analysis of the opinions cited in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>Rabbi Akiva, what is the reason</b> he maintains that those liable to be executed are not flogged? It is written: “And the judge shall cause him to lie down and to be beaten before him, <b>according to the measure of his wickedness”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), from which it is derived with regard to one who commits one transgression: <b>For one evildoing you can render him liable, but you cannot render him liable for two evildoings,</b> i.e., one cannot receive two punishments for the same act. Therefore, execution suffices and he is not flogged.
<b>And Rabbi Yishmael</b> holds that <b>this matter</b> applies only with regard to one transgression punishable by both <b>death and monetary restitution, or</b> punishable by both <b>lashes and monetary restitution. But death and lashes</b> are not considered two separate punishments, rather both forms of physical punishment <b>are</b> together considered <b>an extended death,</b> with the lashes followed by the execution.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to Rabbi Akiva, if that is so,</b> that one does not receive two punishments for one transgression, <b>those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i> too</b> should not be flogged. <b>What did you say</b> in response, <b>that if they repented</b> they are exempt from <i>karet</i>, and therefore they are flogged instead? <b>Now, in any event,</b> at the point when they are flogged <b>they have not</b> yet <b>done</b> so, i.e., repented.
<b>Rabbi Abbahu says: The Torah explicitly included with regard to lashes those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i>, as is derived</b> by means of a verbal analogy: “And they shall be excised [<i>venikhretu</i>] <b>before the eyes of [<i>le’einei</i>]</b> the children of their people” (Leviticus 20:17), <b>from:</b> “Forty he shall flog him…and your brother shall be dishonored <b>before your eyes [<i>le’einekha</i>]”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:3). <b>Rabbi Abba bar Memel objects to this: If so,</b> the inclusion of <b>those liable to</b> be executed with <b>court</b>-imposed <b>death</b> penalties <b>should also be learned</b> by means of a verbal analogy: “If <b>from the eyes of [<i>me’einei</i>]</b> the assembly it was performed unwittingly” (Numbers 15:24) stated with regard to idol worship, for which one is liable to be executed, <b>from: “Before your eyes,”</b> stated with regard to lashes. On that basis, one should derive that those liable to be executed with a court-imposed death penalty should also be flogged.
The Gemara rejects that objection: <b>One derives</b> by means of a verbal analogy <b><i>le’einei</i> from <i>le’einekha</i>,</b> due to the similar prefix, <b>but one does not derive <i>me’einei</i> from <i>le’einekha</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And what</b> significant <b>difference is there</b> between them that prevents derivation by means of a verbal analogy? <b>But didn’t the school of Rabbi Yishmael teach</b> a verbal analogy with regard to leprosy of houses? The verse states: <b>“And the priest shall return [<i>veshav</i>]</b> on the seventh day” (Leviticus 14:39), and another verse with regard to the priest’s visit seven days later states: <b>“And the priest shall come [<i>uva</i>]</b> and look” (Leviticus 14:44). <b>This returning</b> and <b>this coming</b> have the same meaning, and one can therefore derive by verbal analogy that the <i>halakha</i> that applies if the leprosy had spread at the conclusion of the first week applies if it had spread again by the end of the following week. Obviously, the less pronounced difference between <i>me’einei</i> and <i>le’einekha</i> should not prevent the teaching of a verbal analogy.
<b>And furthermore,</b> why not <b>derive</b> by means of a verbal analogy <b><i>me’einei</i></b> written with regard to court-imposed death penalties <b>from <i>le’einei</i></b> written with regard to <i>karet</i>, since those two terms are more similar, just <b>as</b> a verbal analogy of <b><i>le’einei</i> from <i>le’einekha</i> was derived.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Shmuel bar Rav Yitzḥak received</b> a tradition <b>from</b> Rabbi Abbahu that the reason those liable to receive <i>karet</i> are flogged and it is not considered two punishments for one transgression is that when the verse states: <b>“According to the measure of his wickedness”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), from which it is derived: <b>For one evildoing you can render him liable, but you cannot render him liable for two evildoings, the verse is speaking with regard to an evildoing that is given to</b> the jurisdiction of <b>the court,</b> not to an act of wickedness punishable at the hand of Heaven.
§ <b>Rava says</b> with regard to the dispute between Rabbi Yishmael and Rabbi Akiva: If the witnesses or other onlookers <b>forewarned him</b> that if he performs the transgression he will be sentenced <b>to death, everyone,</b> even Rabbi Yishmael, <b>agrees that he is not</b> both <b>flogged and executed. When they disagree,</b> it is in a case <b>where they forewarned him</b> that he will be sentenced <b>to lashes. Rabbi Yishmael maintains</b> that with regard to <b>a prohibition</b> in the Torah <b>that</b> potentially <b>serves as a mandate</b> for <b>court</b>-imposed <b>capital punishment, one is flogged for its</b> violation in a case where there is no actual death penalty, <b>and Rabbi Akiva maintains</b> that with regard to <b>a prohibition</b> in the Torah <b>that</b> potentially <b>serves as a mandate</b> for <b>court</b>-imposed <b>capital punishment, one is not flogged for its</b> violation even if no death penalty is imposed, as that prohibition is punishable only by death.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>Rabbi Akiva, if so,</b> with regard to <b>those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i> as well,</b> they violated <b>a prohibition</b> in the Torah <b>that serves as a mandate</b> for <b><i>karet</i></b> and not for lashes. <b>Rav Mordekhai said to Rav Ashi: This</b> is what <b>Avimi from Hagronya said in the name of Rava: Those liable to</b> receive <b><i>karet</i> do not require forewarning, as</b> in the case of one who fails to fulfill the positive mitzvot of the <b>Paschal offering and circumcision</b> the Torah <b>punished</b> them with <i>karet</i> <b>even though it did not warn</b> that it is prohibited to fail to fulfill the mitzva. Therefore, the prohibitions written in cases punishable with <i>karet</i> are written to teach that one can receive lashes for their violation, not to mandate the punishment of <i>karet</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>But perhaps</b> the <b>prohibition</b> is written concerning those liable to receive <i>karet</i> with regard to liability <b>to</b> bring <b>an offering</b> for unwitting violation of the prohibition, not to teach that the transgressor will receive lashes, <b>as</b> for failure to fulfill the positive mitzvot of the <b>Paschal offering and circumcision, for which there is no</b> biblical <b>prohibition, one does not bring an offering.</b>
The Gemara rejects that suggestion: <b>There,</b> with regard to the Paschal offering and circumcision, <b>that is not the reason</b> that one does not bring an offering. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that all</b> the mitzvot in <b>the entire Torah</b> whose unwitting violation requires the transgressor to bring a sin-offering <b>are juxtaposed to</b> and likened to <b>idol worship. Just as idol worship</b> is a prohibition about which the Torah commands: <b>Sit and do not perform</b> the transgression, and one who unwittingly performs the transgression is liable to bring a sin-offering, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>any</b> prohibition about which the Torah commands: <b>Sit and do not perform</b> the transgression, one who unwittingly performs the transgression is liable to bring a sin-offering. This serves <b>to exclude these</b> mitzvot of the Paschal offering and circumcision, concerning <b>which</b> the Torah commands: <b>Arise</b> and <b>perform</b> the mitzva, and one violates the mitzva by refraining from action. In those cases, one who unwittingly fails to perform these mitzvot is not liable to bring a sin-offering.
<b>Ravina says: Actually,</b> the reason Rabbi Akiva rules that those liable to receive <i>karet</i> are flogged is

Daf 14a

<b>as we stated initially, that if they repented the heavenly court absolves them</b> of the punishment of <i>karet</i>, and therefore this is not a case of two punishments for one transgression, and there is no exemption from lashes. <b>What do you say</b> in response, <b>that they did not</b> yet <b>repent?</b> Nevertheless, since <b>the matter is not clear-cut with regard to <i>karet</i>,</b> as perhaps they repented, they are therefore not exempt from lashes.
§ It is taught in the <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says: Those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i></b> in cases of incest <b>were included in</b> the principle: “For anyone who performs any of these abominations, the souls who do so shall be excised from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). <b>And why was</b> the punishment of <b><i>karet</i>,</b> when administered to one who engages in intercourse <b>with his sister, excluded</b> from this verse and mentioned independently (Leviticus 20:17)? It is <b>to sentence him</b> to be punished <b>with <i>karet</i> and not to</b> be punished <b>with lashes.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>the Rabbis,</b> i.e., Rabbi Yishmael and Rabbi Akiva, who maintain that those liable to receive <i>karet</i> are flogged, <b>why do I</b> need <b><i>karet</i></b> to be written in the case of one who engages in intercourse <b>with his sister;</b> what does it teach? The Gemara answers: It teaches <b>to divide</b> the various prohibitions of sexual intercourse with forbidden relatives, <b>and is in accordance with</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan, as Rabbi Yoḥanan says that if one performed all</b> the transgressions described in the passage in the Torah enumerating forbidden relatives <b>during one lapse of awareness,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to bring a separate sin-offering <b>for each and every one,</b> as one is liable to receive <i>karet</i> for each and every one.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> concerning <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak,</b> who derives the <i>halakha</i> that one who is liable to receive <i>karet</i> is not flogged from the fact that <i>karet</i> for one who engages in intercourse with his sister emerged from the generalization, <b>from where</b> does <b>he</b> derive the concept <b>to divide</b> the various prohibitions? The Gemara answers: <b>He derives it from</b> the verse written with regard to a menstruating woman: <b>“And to a woman in the separation of her impurity</b> you shall not approach” (Leviticus 18:19). From the superfluous expression “and to a woman,” Rabbi Yitzḥak derives <b>that one is liable for</b> intercourse with <b>each and every</b> forbidden <b>woman.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>the Rabbis as well, let them derive</b> the division of the prohibitions <b>from this</b> verse, rather than from the fact that <i>karet</i> for one who engages in intercourse with his sister emerged from the generalization. The Gemara answers: <b>Yes,</b> it <b>is indeed so,</b> they derive division from the verse with regard to a menstruating woman; <b>but rather,</b> the question again arises: <b>Why do I</b> need <b><i>karet</i></b> to be written in the case of one who engages in intercourse <b>with his sister;</b> what does it teach? The Gemara answers: It teaches <b>to render him liable</b> to bring three separate sin-offerings for engaging in intercourse <b>with his sister, and with his father’s sister, and with his mother’s sister,</b> during a single lapse of awareness.
The Gemara asks: Isn’t the fact that he is liable to bring three sin-offerings <b>obvious?</b> The actions were performed with <b>separate bodies,</b> i.e., three different women, and those actions violated the <b>names</b> of three <b>separate</b> prohibitions; no derivation is required. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>to render him liable</b> to bring three separate sin-offerings for engaging in intercourse <b>with his sister who is</b> also <b>his father’s sister</b> and <b>who is</b> also <b>his mother’s sister.</b> The Gemara inquires: <b>And how can you find these</b> circumstances? The Gemara answers: It is found <b>in</b> the case of <b>a wicked person, son of a wicked person.</b> How so? If a man engages in intercourse with his mother, and she bears him two daughters, and he then engages in intercourse with one of the daughters and fathers a son, that son could engage in intercourse with the other daughter, who is his half sister through his father, as well as being his father’s half sister and his mother’s half sister.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> with regard to <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, from where does he</b> derive this? <b>He derives it by means of an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference, <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Akiva says: I asked Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua</b> a question <b>in the meat market [<i>itliz</i>] of</b> the town of <b>Emmaus, where they went to purchase an animal for the</b> wedding <b>feast of Rabban Gamliel’s son.</b> Rabbi Akiva asked: In the case of <b>one who engages in intercourse with his sister who is</b> also <b>his father’s sister</b> and <b>who is</b> also <b>his mother’s sister, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to bringing a sin-offering? Is <b>he liable</b> to bring <b>only one</b> sin-offering <b>for</b> engaging in intercourse that violated <b>all of</b> the prohibitions, <b>or</b> is <b>he liable</b> to bring a sin-offering <b>for each and every</b> prohibition that he violated when he engaged in intercourse with that woman?
Rabban Gamliel and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>said to him: We did not hear</b> the <i>halakha</i> in <b>that</b> case. <b>But we heard</b> the <i>halakha</i> in a similar case: <b>One who engages in intercourse with five menstruating women during one lapse of awareness</b> is <b>liable</b> to bring a sin-offering <b>for each and every one</b> of the women with whom he engaged in intercourse, <b>and it appears</b> with regard to the <b>matter</b> of your inquiry that he is liable to bring a sin-offering for each and every prohibition, <b>by means of an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference. How so? <b>If</b> in the case of <b>a menstruating woman, which</b> involves violation of the <b>name of one</b> prohibition, he is <b>liable for</b> intercourse with <b>each and every one, here, where</b> the woman is forbidden due to the <b>names</b> of <b>three</b> prohibitions, is it <b>not all the more so</b> logical that he is liable to bring three sin-offerings?
The Gemara explains: <b>And</b> according to <b>the other</b> opinion of the Rabbis, who maintain that liability for intercourse with each and every one is derived from an explicit verse, <b>it is a refuted <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>a menstruating woman?</b> It is notable <b>in that</b> the actions were performed with <b>separate bodies,</b> i.e., different women, while in the case in question, the person engaged in intercourse with one woman.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according <b>to the other</b> opinion of Rabbi Yitzḥak <b>too, this is certainly a refuted <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference, from which no <i>halakha</i> can be derived. <b>Rather,</b> as for the <i>halakha</i> that a person is liable to bring three sin-offerings for engaging in intercourse with his sister who is also his father’s sister and who is also his mother’s sister, Rabbi Yitzḥak <b>derives it from</b> the phrase <b>“his sister”</b> written <b>in the latter clause</b> of that verse. The verse begins: “And a man who takes his sister, the daughter of his father or the daughter of his mother…and they shall be excised…the nakedness of his sister he has revealed, he shall bear his transgression” (Leviticus 20:17). From the repetition of the term sister, it is derived that he is liable to bring a sin-offering for each and every prohibition that he violated.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>the other</b> opinion of the Rabbis, who derive this <i>halakha</i> from the term “sister” at the beginning of the verse, <b>why do I</b> need the phrase <b>“his sister”</b> to be written <b>in the latter clause</b> of that verse; what does it teach? The Gemara answers: It teaches <b>to render him liable for</b> engaging in intercourse with <b>his sister</b> who is also <b>the daughter of his father and the daughter of his mother,</b> which comes <b>to say that one does not administer punishment based on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b> Although the Torah specified liability for one who engaged in intercourse with his paternal sister and for one who engaged in intercourse with his maternal sister, an independent derivation is necessary for liability for one who engaged in intercourse with a woman who is both his paternal and his maternal sister.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>the other</b> opinion of Rabbi Yitzḥak, who derives another matter from the term “sister” in the latter verse, from where does he derive liability for one who engaged in intercourse with a woman who is both his paternal and his maternal sister? The Gemara answers: <b>If you wish, say he derives</b> the <b>punishment from the prohibition;</b> just as the prohibition includes intercourse with a woman who is both his paternal and his maternal sister, the same applies to the punishment. <b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that <b>he derives it</b>

Daf 14b

<b>from</b> the term <b>“his sister” at the beginning</b> of the verse (Leviticus 20:17), as the verse could have been formulated: And a man who takes the daughter of his father or the daughter of his mother, and there is no need to mention a sister at all. Liability for intercourse with a woman who is the daughter of both his father and his mother is derived from the term “his sister.”
<b>And</b> according to <b>the other</b> opinion of the Rabbis, since nothing relevant to the topic of intercourse with one’s sister is derived from the term “sister,” <b>that</b> term <b>is necessary</b> in order to derive an unrelated matter, i.e., <b>to divide</b> the various prohibitions and establish liability <b>for</b> either <b>one who blends</b> anointing oil <b>or for one who applies</b> anointing oil to receive <b><i>karet</i>,</b> if he performed either intentionally, and to bring a sin-offering if he performed either unwittingly, even though only a single punishment of <i>karet</i> is written in the verse: “A man who blends a mixture like it and who places any of it upon a non-priest, and he shall be excised from his people” (Exodus 30:33).
<b>And the other,</b> Rabbi Yitzḥak, <b>holds</b> in this regard <b>in accordance with</b> that which <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> says that <b>Rabbi Hoshaya says, as Rabbi Elazar says</b> that <b>Rabbi Hoshaya says</b> that there is a principle: <b>Any place where you find two</b> different <b>prohibitions and one</b> punishment of <b><i>karet</i></b> stated concerning them, <b>they are distinct with regard to</b> liability to bring <b>an offering;</b> if he performed both unwittingly, he is liable to bring two sin-offerings. According to Rabbi Yitzḥak, there is no need for an additional derivation to render one who unwittingly blends and applies anointing oil liable to bring two sin-offerings.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that <b>he does not hold in accordance with</b> that which <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> says that Rabbi Hoshaya says in this regard, <b>and he derives</b> liability to bring two sin-offerings for one who unwittingly blends and applies anointing oil <b>from</b> a superfluous term elsewhere: <b>“And a man who lies with a woman who is afflicted,</b> and…both of them shall be excised” (Leviticus 20:18). Intercourse with a menstruating woman is already included in the verse: “For anyone who performs any of these abominations, the souls who do so shall be excised from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). Since this verse does not introduce any nuance concerning the punishment of <i>karet</i> for one who engages in intercourse with a menstruating woman, an unrelated matter is derived, which establishes liability for one who blends anointing oil and for one who applies anointing oil to receive <i>karet</i> if he performed either act intentionally, and to bring a sin-offering if he performed either act unwittingly.
<b>And</b> according to <b>the other</b> opinion of the Rabbis, what is derived from the verse? <b>That</b> verse <b>is necessary for him to</b> derive the <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan, as Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of Rabbi Shimon ben Yoḥai: From where</b> is it derived <b>that a woman is impure</b> as a menstruating woman <b>only if the blood of menstruation is discharged through her vagina?</b> Blood discharged from any other orifice does not render her impure. It is derived <b>as it is stated: “And a man who lies with a woman who is afflicted, and uncovers her nakedness”</b> (Leviticus 20:18), which <b>teaches that a woman is impure only if the blood of menstruation is discharged through her vagina.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And a ritually impure person who ate sacrificial</b> food, and one who entered the Temple while ritually impure, are flogged. The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, one who entered the Temple</b> while ritually <b>impure</b> is flogged, as <b>a punishment is written and a prohibition is written.</b> The Gemara elaborates: There is <b>a punishment, as it is written: “He has rendered impure the Tabernacle of the Lord, and</b> that soul <b>shall be excised”</b> (Numbers 19:13). And there is <b>a prohibition,</b> as it is written: <b>“And they shall not render their camp impure”</b> (Numbers 5:3). <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a ritually impure person who ate sacrificial</b> food, <b>granted, a punishment is written: “And the soul that eats from the flesh of a peace-offering that pertains to the Lord and his impurity is upon him, and</b> that soul <b>shall be excised”</b> (Leviticus 7:20). <b>But from where is a prohibition</b> derived?
<b>Reish Lakish says:</b> The prohibition is derived from that which is written with regard to a woman after childbirth who has not yet completed the purification process: <b>“No consecrated item shall she touch”</b> (Leviticus 12:4), and the reference is not merely to touching, but to eating.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> that a Sage named <b>Bardela teaches:</b> The matter <b>is derived</b> by means of a verbal analogy between the term <b>“his impurity”</b> written here, and the term <b>“his impurity”</b> written there. <b>Here,</b> with regard to a ritually impure person who eats sacrificial food, <b>it is written: “And his impurity is upon him, and</b> that soul <b>shall be excised,” and there,</b> with regard to a ritually impure person entering the Temple, <b>it is written: “He shall be impure, his impurity is yet upon him”</b> (Numbers 19:13). <b>Just as there,</b> with regard to a ritually impure person who entered the Sanctuary, there is <b>a punishment and</b> there is <b>a prohibition,</b> as noted earlier, <b>so too here,</b> with regard to a ritually impure person who ate sacrificial food, it is derived that there is <b>a punishment and</b> there is <b>a prohibition.</b>
The Gemara analyzes the dispute: <b>Granted, Reish Lakish did not say</b> that the source for the prohibition is a verbal analogy <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> as <b>he did not receive</b> this <b>verbal analogy</b> as a tradition from his teachers and one may not derive a verbal analogy on his own. <b>But what is the reason that Rabbi Yoḥanan did not say</b> that the source of the prohibition is the verse “No consecrated item shall she touch,” <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> as that appears to be a more straightforward source? The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yoḥanan could <b>say to you: That</b> is not a prohibition for partaking of sacrificial food in a state of impurity; rather, it is <b>a prohibition for</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i></b> in a state of impurity.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Reish Lakish, from where</b> does <b>he</b> derive <b>a prohibition against</b> partaking of <b><i>teruma</i></b> in a state of impurity? The Gemara answers: <b>He derives it from</b> that which is written: <b>“Any man from the descendants of Aaron who is leprous or a <i>zav</i></b> may not partake of the sacred food until he will be pure” (Leviticus 22:4). The phrase “descendants of Aaron” indicates that the prohibition applies to all descendants, including women. <b>What,</b> then, <b>is an item whose</b> status <b>is equal</b> with regard <b>to all descendants of Aaron,</b> as opposed to certain sacrificial foods that may be eaten only by males? <b>You must say it is <i>teruma</i>.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>the other</b> opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan, what is derived from this verse? The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> verse serves <b>to</b> prohibit <b>partaking</b> of <i>teruma</i> while impure; <b>and that</b> verse: “No consecrated item shall she touch,” serves <b>to</b> prohibit <b>touching</b> <i>teruma</i> while impure.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>Reish Lakish,</b> does <b>this</b> verse: <b>“No consecrated item shall she touch”</b> (Leviticus 12:4), <b>come to</b> teach <b>this</b> prohibition concerning partaking of sacrificial food? <b>He requires that</b> verse in order <b>to</b> derive the prohibition of <b>a ritually impure person who touched sacrificial</b> food. <b>As it was stated</b> that there is an amoraic dispute with regard to <b>a ritually impure person who touched sacrificial</b> food. <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> He is <b>flogged. Rabbi Yoḥanan says: He is not flogged.</b> The Gemara elaborates: <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> He is <b>flogged,</b> as it is written: <b>“No consecrated item shall she touch.” Rabbi Yoḥanan says: He is not flogged,</b> because it <b>is</b> to teach <b>a prohibition for</b> touching <b><i>teruma</i> that this</b> verse <b>comes.</b>
The Gemara answers: Reish Lakish derives both prohibitions from this verse. The prohibition concerning <b>a ritually impure person who touched sacrificial</b> food is derived <b>from</b> the fact <b>that the Merciful One formulates</b> the prohibition <b>with the term touching.</b> The <b>prohibition for</b> a ritually impure person who <b>partakes</b> of sacrificial food is derived because the matter of a ritually impure person eating <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>is juxtaposed to</b> the matter of entering <b>the Temple</b> while ritually impure, as the Gemara explains below.
The Gemara asks: <b>And still, does</b> the verse <b>come for that</b> derivation? Reish Lakish <b>requires that</b> verse in order <b>to</b> derive the prohibition for <b>a ritually impure person who ate sacrificial meat before the sprinkling of</b> the <b>blood</b> of the offering on the altar, when it is not permitted to partake of the meat. <b>As it was stated,</b> there is an amoraic dispute with regard to <b>a ritually impure person who ate sacrificial meat before the sprinkling of</b> the <b>blood. Reish Lakish says:</b> He is <b>flogged</b> for doing so; <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: He is not flogged.</b>
<b>Reish Lakish says:</b> He is <b>flogged,</b> as it is written: <b>“No consecrated item shall she touch,”</b> which is stated in general terms, indicating that it <b>is no different</b> whether one touches the consecrated item <b>prior to sprinkling</b> the blood, <b>and</b> it <b>is no different</b> if one touches the consecrated item <b>after sprinkling</b> the blood. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: He is not flogged. Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> conforms <b>to his</b> standard line of <b>reasoning,</b> that the prohibition is derived by means of the verbal analogy cited above, <b>as the verse states “his impurity,”</b> written with regard to a ritually impure person who eats sacrificial food, and the meaning of that term is derived from <b>“his impurity,”</b> written with regard to a ritually impure person entering the Temple. <b>And when “his impurity” is written,</b> it is with regard to partaking of sacrificial meat <b>after the sprinkling</b> of the blood <b>that it is written.</b> The Gemara explains that according to Reish Lakish, <b>that</b> prohibition <b>is derived from</b> the term: <b>“No [<i>bekhol</i>] consecrated item,”</b> as the term <i>bekhol</i> is inclusive and prohibits one from partaking of sacrificial meat even before the sprinkling of the blood.
The Gemara comments: <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Reish Lakish.</b> It is written: <b>“No consecrated item shall she touch”;</b> this is <b>a prohibition for</b> a ritually impure person who <b>eats</b> sacrificial food. <b>Do you say</b> that it is <b>a prohibition for</b> a ritually impure person who <b>eats</b> sacrificial food, <b>or</b> perhaps <b>it is only a prohibition for</b> a ritually impure person who <b>touches</b> sacrificial food? <b>The verse states: “No consecrated item shall she touch, and to the Temple</b> she may not come” (Leviticus 12:4). The verse <b>juxtaposes</b> the matter of a ritually impure person eating <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>to</b> the matter of entering <b>the Temple</b> while ritually impure. <b>Just as</b> entering <b>the Temple</b> is <b>a matter</b> that entails a punishment <b>that involves the taking of a life,</b> i.e., <i>karet</i>, <b>so too, every matter</b> in that verse entails a punishment <b>that involves the taking of a life.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>And if</b> the prohibition is <b>with regard to touching</b> sacrificial food, <b>is there</b> a punishment that entails <b>the taking of a life? Rather,</b> the prohibition is <b>with regard to eating.</b>
§ <b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> With regard to <b>any prohibition that</b> has <b>a positive mitzva</b> that <b>preceded it,</b> everyone agrees that <b>one is flogged for its</b> violation, as it is not classified as a prohibition that entails fulfillment of a positive mitzva.

Daf 15a

<b>They said to</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>Did you say</b> this <i>halakha</i>? Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to them: No. Rabba</b> bar bar Ḥana <b>said</b> in the form of an oath: <b>By God, he said it, and</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>is written</b> in the Torah <b>and we learn</b> it in the mishna. <b>It is written: “And they shall send from the camp</b> any man who is leprous, and any <i>zav</i>…<b>and they shall not render their camp impure”</b> (Numbers 5:2–4). There is a prohibition against rendering the camp impure, and there is a positive mitzva to send them out of the camp. Since the positive mitzva precedes and is independent of the prohibition, one is flogged for the violation of that prohibition, as <b>we learn</b> in the mishna among those liable to receive lashes: <b>One who entered the Temple</b> while ritually <b>impure.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>Rather, what is the reason he retracted</b> his statement and claimed he did not say it? The Gemara answers: It is <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>halakha</i> concerning one who <b>rapes</b> a virgin young woman, who is required by Torah law to marry her and for whom it is prohibited to divorce her as long as he lives, <b>is difficult for him, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a rapist who divorced</b> the woman he raped, <b>if he is a non-priest, he remarries</b> her, <b>and he is not flogged</b> for violating the prohibition: “He may not send her away all his days” (Deuteronomy 22:29). <b>If he is a priest,</b> he is <b>flogged</b> for violating the prohibition, <b>and he does not remarry</b> her because it is prohibited for him to marry a divorcée.
The Gemara elaborates: <b>If he is a non-priest, he remarries</b> her, <b>and he is not flogged. Why? It is a prohibition that</b> has <b>a positive mitzva</b> which <b>preceded it,</b> as the positive mitzva: “And she shall be his wife” (Deuteronomy 22:29), precedes the prohibition “He may not send her away.” <b>But let him be flogged,</b> since he violated the prohibition by divorcing her. Apparently, one is not flogged even if the positive mitzva precedes the prohibition.
<b>Ulla says</b> in response: The positive mitzva in the case of a rapist is not a mitzva that precedes the prohibition. Rather, it follows the prohibition and rectifies it, as it is referring to a case of one who married his victim and then divorced her. <b>Let</b> the verse <b>not state: “And she shall be his wife,” in</b> the case of <b>a rapist, and let us derive</b> it by means of an <i>a fortiori</i> inference <b>from</b> the case of <b>one who defames</b> his bride, claiming that she was not a virgin when he consummated the marriage: <b>If</b> in the case of <b>a defamer, who did not perform an action</b> but sinned through speech, <b>the Merciful One states: “And she shall be his wife”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:19), then in the case of <b>a rapist,</b> who performed an action, <b>is it not all the more so</b> that he is obligated to take her as a wife?
Ulla continues: <b>Why,</b> then, <b>is</b> the mitzva <b>stated</b> in the case of the rapist? <b>If it is not</b> relevant to the <b>matter of before</b> the rapist marries the victim, <b>apply it</b> to the <b>matter of after</b> the rapist marries the victim, teaching <b>that if he divorced</b> her <b>he must remarry</b> her. Although in the verse it appears prior to the prohibition “He may not send her away,” it actually serves to rectify that prohibition.
The Gemara asks: <b>But still,</b> this affords no proof, as <b>one cannot derive</b> the case of <b>a rapist from</b> the case of <b>a defamer, as</b> the <i>a fortiori</i> inference <b>can be refuted: What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>a defamer,</b> which is more stringent than the case of a rapist? It is notable <b>in that he is flogged and</b> is liable to <b>pay</b> retribution, which is contrary to the principle that in general, two punishments are not administered for one transgression.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Rather, let</b> the verse <b>not state: “And she shall be his wife,” in</b> the case of the <b>defamer, and let us derive</b> it by means of an <i>a fortiori</i> inference <b>from</b> the case of <b>a rapist: And if</b> in the case of <b>a rapist, who is not</b> both <b>flogged and</b> liable to <b>pay</b> restitution, but is liable to receive only one punishment, <b>the Merciful One states: “And she shall be his wife,”</b> in the case of <b>a defamer,</b> who is flogged and liable to pay retribution, <b>is it not all the more so</b> that he is obligated to take her as a wife? <b>And why,</b> then, <b>is it stated</b> in the case of the defamer? Rather, <b>if</b> the mitzva <b>is not</b> relevant to the <b>matter of</b> the <b>defamer, apply it</b> to the <b>matter of</b> the <b>rapist, and if</b> the mitzva <b>is not</b> relevant to the <b>matter of before</b> the rapist marries the victim, <b>apply it</b> to the <b>matter of after</b> the rapist marries the victim, teaching that if he divorced her he must remarry her. Although in the verse it appears prior to the prohibition “He may not send her away,” it actually serves to rectify that prohibition.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> this affords no proof, as <b>one cannot derive</b> the case of <b>a defamer from</b> the case of <b>a rapist either, as</b> the <i>a fortiori</i> inference <b>can be refuted: What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>a rapist?</b> It is notable in <b>that he performed an action,</b> while the defamer performed no action.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Rather, let</b> the verse <b>not state: “And she shall be his wife,” in</b> the case of the <b>defamer</b> because the expression is redundant, <b>as she is</b> already <b>his wife.</b> It would have been sufficient for the verse to state that it is prohibited for him to send her away. <b>Why,</b> then, <b>is it stated</b> in the case of the defamer? Rather, <b>if</b> the mitzva <b>is not</b> relevant to the <b>matter of</b> the <b>defamer, apply it</b> to the <b>matter of</b> the <b>rapist, and if</b> the mitzva <b>is not</b> relevant to the <b>matter of before</b> the rapist marries the victim, <b>apply it</b> to the <b>matter of after</b> the rapist marries the victim, teaching that if he divorced her he must remarry her. Although the positive mitzva appears in the verse prior to the prohibition “He may not send her away,” it actually serves to rectify that prohibition.
The Gemara questions that derivation: <b>And say</b> instead: <b>And if</b> the mitzva <b>is not</b> relevant to the <b>matter of</b> the <b>defamer before</b> he marries his bride, as they are married, <b>apply it</b> to the <b>matter of</b> the defamer <b>himself after</b> they are married, teaching that if he divorced her and violated the prohibition against divorcing her, he is obligated to remarry her, and it teaches <b>that he is not flogged,</b> as the positive mitzva rectifies the prohibition. But in the case of a rapist, the mitzva is to marry her after the rape but there is no mitzva to marry her after he divorces her, and nevertheless, he is not flogged.
The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so;</b> the <i>halakha</i> is derived with regard to the defamer himself, teaching that if he divorces his bride he is obligated to remarry her. <b>And</b> the case of the <b>rapist comes and is derived from</b> the case of the defamer, and the same <i>halakha</i> applies there as well. The Gemara asks: <b>With what</b> derivation is the case of a rapist <b>derived from</b> the case of a defamer? <b>If</b> it is <b>by means of an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference or <b>if</b> it is <b>by means of</b> the inductive hermeneutical principle: <b>What do we find,</b> those derivations <b>can be refuted, as we refuted</b> the derivations earlier in the Gemara: <b>What</b> is notable <b>about</b> the case of <b>a defamer?</b> It is notable <b>in that he did not perform an action.</b>
<b>Rather, Rava says:</b> From where is it derived that a rapist must remarry his victim if he divorced her because the positive mitzva rectifies the prohibition “He may not send her away”? It is derived from the superfluous phrase: “All his days” (Deuteronomy 22:29), indicating that <b>all his days,</b> even after he marries and divorces her, he is obligated <b>to arise and remarry</b> her and is consequently not liable to receive lashes. The Gemara adds: <b>And likewise, when Ravin came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, he said that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: All his days</b> he is obligated <b>to arise and remarry</b> her.
<b>Rav Pappa said to Rava</b> that a question arises with regard to Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement that one is flogged for violating a prohibition preceded by a positive mitzva: <b>But isn’t its prohibition dissimilar to the prohibition of muzzling</b> an ox while it is threshing grain (see Deuteronomy 25:4), which is the paradigm for all prohibitions for whose violation one is flogged, as no positive mitzva is stated in conjunction with the prohibition of muzzling? Rava <b>said to him:</b> Is it reasonable to say that <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that the Merciful One wrote in its</b> regard <b>an additional positive mitzva,</b> the stringency of the prohibition <b>lessens,</b> so that no lashes are administered?
Rav Pappa objected to this claim: <b>If so,</b> with regard to <b>a prohibition that entails</b> fulfillment <b>of a positive mitzva as well, let us say:</b> Is it reasonable to say that <b>due to</b> the fact <b>that the Merciful One wrote in its</b> regard <b>an additional positive mitzva,</b> the stringency of the prohibition <b>lessens,</b> so that no lashes are administered? Rava <b>said to him:</b> In <b>that</b> case, the transgressor is not flogged because the mitzva <b>comes to sever the prohibition</b> from the punishment of lashes.
The Gemara returns to the statement of Rava, who said with regard to the rapist: All his days he is obligated to arise and remarry her, and that is why he is not flogged for violating the prohibition “He may not send her away,” and asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who says</b> that the criterion for determining whether one is flogged for violating a prohibition that entails fulfillment of a positive mitzva is whether <b>he nullified</b> the mitzva <b>or did not nullify</b> the mitzva. According to this opinion, one is flogged only if fulfillment of the mitzva is no longer possible, e.g., a priest who divorced the woman whom he raped. By contrast, a non-priest would not be flogged, because the option of remarriage remains viable.
<b>But according to the one who says</b> that the criterion for determining whether one is flogged in that case is whether <b>he fulfilled</b> the mitzva <b>or did not fulfill</b> the mitzva, and if he did not immediately fulfill the mitzva he is flogged, <b>what can be said?</b> Even in the case of a non-priest who divorced the rape victim, once he fails to remarry her immediately, he is liable to receive lashes.

Daf 15b

The Gemara answers: <b>The only reason</b> Rava stated his explanation, that all his days he is obligated to arise and remarry her and that is why the rapist is not flogged even though it is a prohibition preceded by a positive mitzva, <b>is to</b> explain the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who said that for any prohibition that has a positive mitzva which preceded it, everyone agrees that one is flogged for its violation. <b>Didn’t Rabbi Yoḥanan say to</b> the <b><i>tanna</i></b> who would recite the <i>mishnayot</i> and <i>baraitot</i> in the study hall: <b>Teach</b> that if he <b>nullified</b> the mitzva, he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes, and <b>if he did not nullify</b> the mitzva, he is <b>exempt</b> from lashes? According to Rabbi Yoḥanan, it works out well.
What is the dispute to which the Gemara is alluding? <b>The <i>tanna</i> taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> With regard to <b>any prohibition that entails</b> a command to <b>arise</b> and <b>perform</b> a mitzva, if <b>he fulfilled the positive</b> mitzva <b>that</b> is entailed <b>therein,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from lashes, and if he <b>nullified the positive mitzva that</b> is entailed <b>therein,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes.
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> the <i>tanna</i>: <b>What</b> is it that <b>you are saying?</b> The <i>baraita</i> that you recited is self-contradictory, as based on the first clause: If <b>he fulfilled</b> the mitzva he is <b>exempt,</b> apparently, if <b>he did not fulfill</b> the mitzva he is <b>liable.</b> Yet based on the latter clause: If <b>he nullified</b> the mitzva, he is <b>liable,</b> apparently, if <b>he did not nullify</b> the mitzva he is <b>exempt,</b> even though he failed to fulfill the mitzva. Rather, <b>teach:</b> If he <b>nullified</b> the mitzva, he is liable to receive lashes, <b>and</b> if <b>he did not nullify</b> the mitzva, he is exempt from lashes. <b>And Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish says:</b> The formulation of the <i>baraita</i> must be consistent; therefore, teach: If <b>he fulfilled</b> the mitzva, he is exempt, <b>and</b> if <b>he did not fulfill</b> the mitzva he is liable.
The Gemara inquires: <b>With regard to what</b> matter <b>do they disagree?
