It is derived <b>as the verse states</b> with regard to second-tithe produce: <b>“Before the Lord your God you shall eat it”</b> (Deuteronomy 12:18), <b>and it is written</b> in the previous verse: <b>“You may not eat within your gates”</b> (Deuteronomy 12:17), from which it is derived: <b>Anywhere that we read concerning it: “Before the Lord your God you shall eat it,” we read concerning it</b> the prohibition: <b>“You may not eat within your gates,”</b> i.e., it may not be eaten outside the walls of Jerusalem; <b>and anywhere that we do not read concerning it: “Before the Lord your God you shall eat it,”</b> as the produce remained outside the walls, <b>we do not read concerning it</b> the prohibition: <b>“You may not eat within your gates.”</b>
<b>The Gemara raises an objection</b> from that which <b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> With regard to <b>a priest that a fig of untithed produce came into his possession,</b> and he seeks to separate <i>terumot</i> and tithes from it, <b>he says: This fig,</b> I designate <b>its <i>teruma</i> at its stem,</b> I designate its <b>first tithe at its north</b> side, <b>and</b> I designate its <b>second tithe at its south</b> side; <b>and it is a year</b> when <b>second tithe</b> is separated, i.e., the first, second, fourth, or fifth years of the Sabbatical Year cycle, <b>and he is in Jerusalem,</b> when he recites this formula. <b>Or</b> he says that poor man’s tithe shall be designated at its south side if it is a year of <b>poor man’s tithe,</b> i.e., the third or sixth years of the Sabbatical Year cycle, <b>and he is</b> located anywhere <b>in the outlying areas,</b> not in Jerusalem. In those cases, if <b>he ate</b> the fig,

Daf 20a

<b>he is flogged</b> with <b>one</b> set of lashes, because he ate it without separating <i>teruma</i> of the tithe from the first tithe and is consequently liable for eating untithed produce. Had he separated <i>teruma</i> of the tithe, it would have been permitted for him to eat the entire fig, as it is permitted for a priest to partake of both <i>teruma gedola</i> and <i>teruma</i> of the tithe.
<b>And a non-priest who eats</b> the fig <b>is flogged</b> with <b>two</b> sets of lashes, for both eating untithed produce and eating the <i>teruma</i> therein, which is forbidden for all non-priests. The <i>baraita</i> notes: <b>As if</b> the non-priest <b>ate</b> the fig <b>initially,</b> before separating <i>teruma</i> and tithes, <b>he is flogged</b> with <b>only one</b> set of lashes, for eating untithed produce. Once he separated the <i>teruma</i> and tithes, he is flogged for eating <i>teruma</i> as well.
The Gemara infers from the statement in the <i>baraita</i>: And it is a year when second tithe is separated and he is in Jerusalem, that <b>the reason</b> he is flogged with two sets of lashes <b>is that he is</b> currently <b>in Jerusalem,</b> where it is permitted to partake of second-tithe produce. <b>But</b> if he ate the fig <b>in the outlying areas,</b> outside of Jerusalem, and he designated second tithe in the fig, <b>he is flogged</b> with <b>three</b> sets of lashes. It is apparent <b>that even if</b> this second-tithe produce <b>has not entered within the wall</b> of Jerusalem, one is liable to receive lashes for its consumption. This contradicts Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion, as he holds that one is liable to receive lashes for eating second-tithe produce only if it entered Jerusalem. The Gemara answers: The case in the <i>baraita</i> is one <b>where they took</b> the fig <b>into</b> Jerusalem <b>and</b> then <b>took it out</b> of Jerusalem. That is why he is liable to receive a third set of lashes.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so, what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> this <i>halakha</i>? If the fig entered and exited Jerusalem there is no novel element in the fact that one is liable to receive lashes. The Gemara answers: There is a novel element in this <i>halakha</i>, as <b>what are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where one took</b> figs into the city while <b>they</b> had the status of <b>untithed produce,</b> before <i>terumot</i> and tithes were separated, <b>and</b> Rabbi Yosei <b>holds</b> that the status of <b>gifts that were not</b> yet <b>separated</b> from the produce is <b>like</b> that of gifts <b>that were</b> already <b>separated.</b> The novel element is that although second tithe was not separated in practice, all the <i>halakhot</i> of second tithe are in effect with regard to the portion that he intends to separate as second tithe. Therefore, if after taking the untithed produce into Jerusalem he took it outside of Jerusalem, and ate it, he is liable to receive lashes.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> does <b>Rabbi Yosei hold</b> that the status of <b>gifts that were not</b> yet <b>separated</b> from the produce is <b>like</b> that of gifts <b>that were</b> already <b>separated? But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Yosei: Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel did not disagree with regard to produce whose labor was not completed</b> and the obligation to tithe had consequently not yet taken effect, <b>and</b> which at that stage <b>passed through Jerusalem, that its second-tithe</b> produce <b>may be redeemed and may be eaten anywhere.</b> As long as the obligation to tithe has not taken effect, the sanctity of Jerusalem does not affect the produce.
<b>With regard to what</b> case <b>did they disagree?</b> It is <b>with regard to produce whose labor was</b> already <b>completed and it passed through Jerusalem</b> before <i>terumot</i> and tithes were separated from it, <b>as Beit Shammai say:</b> Since one is obligated to separate second tithe from this produce, and the produce was in Jerusalem, <b>he must bring its second-tithe</b> produce <b>back</b> to Jerusalem, <b>and it shall be eaten</b> it <b>in Jerusalem; and Beit Hillel say:</b> The produce <b>may be redeemed and eaten anywhere. And if it enters your mind</b> to say the status of <b>gifts that were not</b> yet <b>separated</b> from the produce is <b>like</b> that of gifts <b>that were</b> already <b>separated,</b> how can Beit Hillel hold that the second-tithe produce may be redeemed; <b>wasn’t</b> the produce <b>admitted</b> by <b>the walls</b> of Jerusalem? Therefore, even though second tithe was not yet separated, it can no longer be redeemed. Apparently, Rabbi Yosei, who certainly holds in accordance with the opinion of Beit Hillel, holds that the status of gifts that were not yet separated from the produce is not like that of gifts that were already separated.
<b>Rabba said:</b> The <i>halakha</i> of the <b>wall with regard to eating</b> second-tithe produce, i.e., that second-tithe produce may not be eaten outside the wall of Jerusalem, is <b>by Torah law;</b> the <i>halakha</i> of the <b>wall with regard to the admitting</b> of second-tithe produce, i.e., that once the produce enters within the wall of Jerusalem, it may not be redeemed or taken beyond the wall, is <b>by rabbinic law. And when the Sages issued the decree</b> that one may not remove from Jerusalem second-tithe produce that entered within the walls, they did so <b>when</b> the tithe <b>is in its unadulterated form</b> after it was separated; but in a case where it is <b>in its</b> status of <b>untithed produce, the Sages did not issue a decree.</b>
<b>Ravina said:</b> Rabbi Yosei is referring to the case of second-tithe produce that was already separated and brought into Jerusalem, and the novel element of his statement is that he is referring to a fig that was not physically taken into Jerusalem; rather, it is a case <b>where one is holding</b> the fig <b>on a reed</b> outside Jerusalem, while he is standing inside Jerusalem. <b>And resolve the dilemma of Rav Pappa</b> (19b) and conclude that if one is standing inside Jerusalem and holding second tithe outside the city on a reed, it is as though the produce entered the city.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who creates a bald spot upon his head, and one who rounds the edge of his head</b> by shaving the hair adjacent to the ear, <b>and one who mars the edge of his beard, and one who cuts one incision</b> in a display of mourning <b>over the dead,</b> are all <b>liable</b> to receive lashes. If <b>he cut one incision over five dead</b> people, <b>or five incisions over one dead</b> person, he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for each and every one.</b>
<b>For</b> rounding the edges of his <b>head,</b> one is liable to receive <b>two</b> sets of lashes, <b>one from here,</b> the hair adjacent to one ear, <b>and one from there,</b> the hair adjacent to the other ear. <b>For</b> marring the edges of his <b>beard</b> there are <b>two</b> edges <b>from here,</b> on one side of his face, <b>and two from there,</b> on the other side, <b>and one from below,</b> on his chin. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: If</b> he <b>removed</b> the hair on <b>all</b> the edges of his beard <b>in one</b> action, <b>he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes for all of them.
<b>And one is liable</b> for marring the edges of his beard <b>only if he removes</b> the hair <b>with a razor. Rabbi Eliezer says: Even if he removed</b> the hair <b>with tweezers [<i>malket</i>] or with a plane [<i>rehitni</i>],</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught:</b> From the verse concerning priests: <b>“They shall not create</b> a bald spot upon their heads” (Leviticus 21:5), one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>even</b> if <b>he created four or five bald spots</b> in one action, <b>he will be liable to</b> receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Bald spot,”</b> in the singular, <b>to</b> render one <b>liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for each and every bald</b> spot. The verse continues: <b>“Upon their heads”; what</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> that term? <b>Since it is stated: “You shall neither lacerate yourselves, nor place a bald spot between your eyes for the dead”</b> (Deuteronomy 14:1), one <b>might</b> have thought <b>that one would be liable only for</b> placing a bald spot <b>between the eyes. From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> the prohibition <b>includes</b> placing a bald spot on <b>the entire head? The verse states: “Upon their heads,” to include the entire head.</b>
<b>And I have</b> derived this prohibition <b>only with regard to priests,</b> for <b>whom the Torah established numerous additional mitzvot,</b> since the verse “They shall not create a bald spot” is written in the context of the <i>halakhot</i> of priests. <b>From where</b> do I derive that <b>a non-priest</b> is also liable for violating that prohibition?
<b>“Bald spot” is stated here</b> (Deuteronomy 14:1) with regard to non-priests, <b>and “bald spot” is stated there,</b> with regard to priests; <b>just as there,</b> with regard to priests, one is <b>liable for each and every bald spot</b> that he creates, <b>and is liable for</b> a bald spot created anywhere on his <b>head as</b> he is for a bald spot created <b>between the eyes, so too here,</b> with regard to a non-priest, one is <b>liable for each and every bald spot</b> that he creates, <b>and is liable</b> for a bald spot created anywhere on his <b>head as</b> he is for a bald spot created <b>between the eyes. And just as there,</b> with regard to non-priests, the prohibition is stated with regard to creating a bald spot as an expression of mourning <b>over the dead; so too here,</b> with regard to priests, the prohibition is stated with regard to creating a bald spot as an expression of mourning <b>over the dead.</b>
The Gemara asks: With regard to <b>these four or five bald spots</b> created over one dead person, for which one is liable to receive lashes for each and every spot, <b>what are the circumstances? If we say</b> that he created those bald spots <b>one after the other and with five</b> separate <b>forewarnings,</b> one preceding each action, it is <b>obvious</b> that he is liable for each and every bald spot; in that case he is one who violates the same prohibition several times and is punished for each violation for which he was forewarned.

Daf 20b

<b>Rather,</b> say that he created five bald spots <b>with one forewarning; is one liable</b> in that case? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (21a): <b>A nazirite who was drinking wine all day is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes; if onlookers <b>said to him: Do not drink, do not drink,</b> forewarning him several times, <b>and he drinks</b> after each forewarning, he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for each and every</b> drink. The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>is necessary only</b> with regard to one <b>who,</b> after one forewarning, <b>smeared his five fingers</b> in a <b>depilatory</b> agent <b>[<i>nasha</i>] and</b> then <b>placed</b> his fingers <b>simultaneously</b> on his hair, thereby creating five bald spots, <b>as</b> in that case, <b>it is</b> tantamount to <b>a forewarning for each and every</b> bald spot that he created. The novel element is that each bald spot is an independent transgression.
§ The Gemara asks: <b>And how much is the measure of a bald spot</b> for which one is liable? <b>Rav Huna says:</b> It must be of <b>sufficient</b> size so <b>that</b> bald skin <b>will be visible on his head. Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon:</b> It is the size of <b>a Cilician bean [<i>kigeris</i>].</b> The Gemara comments: This amoraic dispute is <b>parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>: And how much is the measure of a bald spot?</b> It is the size of <b>a Cilician bean. <i>Aḥerim</i> say:</b> It must be of <b>sufficient</b> size so <b>that</b> bald skin <b>will be visible on his head.</b>
<b>Rav Yehuda bar Ḥaviva said</b> that there are <b>three <i>tanna’im</i></b> who <b>disagree concerning</b> this matter. <b>One says:</b> A spot the size of <b>a Cilician bean; and one says:</b> A spot of <b>sufficient</b> size so <b>that</b> bald skin <b>will be visible on his head; and one says:</b> A spot that is the measure <b>of two hairs. And there is one who removed</b> the opinion that it is the measure of <b>two hairs, and inserted</b> an opinion that it is the size <b>of a lentil. And your mnemonic</b> to remember the different opinions is from a mishna with regard to the <i>halakhot</i> of leprosy (<i>Nega’im</i> 6:5): The measure of impurity for <b>a snow-white leprous mark [<i>baheret</i>]</b> is <b>a Cilician bean, and</b> the measure of impurity for the <b>raw</b> flesh that grows within the <i>baheret</i> is <b>a lentil.</b>
In a related <i>halakha</i>, the Sages <b>taught: One who removes a scissorsful [<i>melo pi hazug</i>]</b> of hair from his head <b>on Shabbat is liable. And how much is a scissorsful? Rav Yehuda says: Two</b> hairs. The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>For a bald spot</b> the measure is <b>two</b> hairs, from which it may be inferred that the measure with regard to labor on Shabbat is different than for a bald spot. The Gemara answers: Emend the <i>baraita</i> and <b>say: And likewise with regard to a bald spot</b> the measure is <b>two</b> hairs, indicating that the measure for Shabbat and the measure for a bald spot are the same.
<b>That is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who removes a scissorsful</b> of hair <b>on Shabbat is liable. And how much is a scissorsful?</b> It is <b>two</b> hairs. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: One</b> hair. <b>And the Rabbis concede to Rabbi Eliezer in</b> the case of <b>one who removes white</b> hairs <b>from black</b> ones, <b>even</b> if he removes <b>one</b> hair, <b>that he is liable.</b> Since his intent was to remove that particular hair, its removal constitutes a complete action. The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> for a man, <b>that matter is prohibited even during the week, due to</b> the fact <b>that it is stated: “Neither shall a man don a woman’s garment”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:5). Removal of white hairs for the purposes of beautification is characteristic of women, and it is prohibited for a man to perform those actions.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And one who rounds the edge of his head</b> is flogged. <b>The Sages taught: The edge of his head</b> is <b>the extremity of his head. And what is the extremity of his head? This is</b> a reference to <b>one who levels</b> the hairline of <b>his temples to</b> the hairline <b>behind his ear and to</b> the hairline of <b>his forehead.</b> There is no hair behind the ears or on the forehead. One who removes the hair from the temples so that they are like those areas violates the prohibition against rounding the edges of his head.
<b>The <i>tanna</i> taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rav Ḥisda: Both one who rounds</b> the edges of his head <b>and one</b> for <b>whom</b> the edges of his head <b>are rounded</b> are <b>flogged.</b> Rav Ḥisda <b>said to him: Is one who eats dates</b> that are <b>in a sieve [<i>arbeila</i>] flogged?</b> By analogy, if a person rounded the edges of another’s head, why should the person whose head was rounded receive lashes? He performed no action. Rav Ḥisda proceeded to explain to the <i>tanna</i>: In response to one <b>who says to you: Who is</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i>? Say that <b>it is Rabbi Yehuda, who says:</b> In the case of <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action, one is flogged for its</b> violation.
<b>Rava says:</b> This <i>baraita</i> can be explained as referring <b>to one who rounds</b> the edges of his head <b>for himself,</b> and he is liable both for rounding the edges of his head and for having the edges of his head rounded; <b>and everyone agrees</b> that he is flogged because he performed an action. <b>Rav Ashi says:</b> This <i>baraita</i> can be explained as referring to a case <b>where</b> the one for whom the edges of his head are being rounded <b>assists</b> the person rounding his head, by repositioning his head to facilitate that rounding, <b>and everyone agrees</b> that he is flogged.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And one who mars the edge of his beard</b> is flogged. <b>The Sages taught: The edge of his beard</b> is <b>the extremity of his beard. And what is the extremity of his beard?</b> It is <b>the stalk of his beard,</b> i.e., the five edges of the beard enumerated in the mishna where hair collects in one spot, like grain on stalks.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And one who cuts one incision</b> in a display of mourning <b>over the dead</b> is flogged. <b>The Sages taught</b> a halakhic midrash on the verse: “And an incision for the soul you shall not place in your flesh” (Leviticus 19:28). Had the verse stated only: <b>“And an incision,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that this prohibition applies to any incision cut in sorrow, <b>even</b> if he <b>cut</b> an incision in sorrow <b>over his house that collapsed, or over his ship that sunk at sea.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “For the soul,”</b> from which it is derived that <b>he is liable</b> for an incision in mourning <b>only for the dead alone. And from where</b> is it derived <b>that one who cuts five incisions</b> in mourning <b>over one dead</b> person <b>is liable for each and every</b> incision? <b>The verse states: “And an incision” to render</b> him <b>liable for each and every incision.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei says: From where</b> is it derived <b>concerning one who cuts one incision</b> in mourning <b>over five dead</b> people, <b>that he is liable for each and every</b> person for whom he cut that incision? <b>The verse states: “For the soul,” to render</b> him <b>liable for each and every dead</b> person.
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t you</b> already <b>derive</b> from the term “for the soul” an exemption for one who cut an incision in sorrow <b>over his house that collapsed, or over his ship that sunk at sea?</b>

Daf 21a

The Gemara answers: Rabbi Yosei derives that <i>halakha</i> from a different source, as <b>Rabbi Yosei holds</b> that <b>an incision</b> over the dead <b>and a laceration</b> over the dead <b>are one</b> transgression, <b>and it is written there,</b> with regard to laceration: “You shall neither lacerate yourselves, nor place a bald spot between your eyes <b>for the dead”</b> (Deuteronomy 14:1), indicating that one is liable for cutting oneself in mourning only over a dead person. Therefore he can derive from the term “for the soul” that one is liable for each and every soul over whom he cut the incision.
On a related note, the Gemara cites that which <b>Shmuel says: One who cuts [<i>mesaret</i>]</b> his flesh <b>with a utensil</b> in mourning over the dead is <b>liable.</b> The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>An incision [<i>serita</i>] and a laceration are one</b> transgression, as they are both performed over the dead; <b>but</b> the difference is that <b>an incision is</b> cut <b>by hand,</b> i.e., with one’s fingernails, while <b>a laceration is</b> cut <b>with a utensil.</b> Apparently, cutting an incision is not accomplished with a utensil. The Gemara answers: Shmuel <b>stated</b> his opinion <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> who holds that an incision and a laceration are synonymous in every sense.
The Gemara relates: <b>A <i>tanna</i> taught</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> One who cuts an incision <b>over a dead</b> person, <b>whether</b> he did so <b>by hand or with a utensil,</b> is <b>liable.</b> Concerning one who cuts an incision <b>for idolatry,</b> if he does so <b>by hand,</b> he is <b>liable,</b> but if he does so <b>with a utensil,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan was puzzled by this <i>baraita</i>: <b>But isn’t the reverse written</b> with regard to the priests of Baal: <b>“And they lacerated themselves in accordance with their custom, with swords and lances”</b> (I Kings 18:28), indicating that customarily laceration is with a utensil? Rabbi Yoḥanan instructed the <i>tanna</i>: <b>Rather,</b> emend the <i>baraita</i> and <b>say:</b> Concerning one who cuts an incision for idolatry, if he does so <b>by hand,</b> he is <b>exempt;</b> but if he does so <b>with a utensil,</b> he is <b>liable.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And for</b> rounding the edges of his <b>head,</b> one is <b>liable</b> to receive two sets of lashes, one from here, the hair adjacent to one ear, and one from there, the hair adjacent to the other ear. The Gemara relates that when he taught this mishna, <b>Rav Sheshet</b> would <b>gesture</b> toward his temple, which is the point <b>between the</b> two <b>parts of the head,</b> the front and back. Similarly, with regard to the <i>halakha</i> in the mishna: <b>For</b> marring the edges of his <b>beard</b> there are <b>two</b> edges <b>from here, and two from there, and one from below, Rav Sheshet</b> would <b>gesture</b> toward the point <b>between the parts of the beard.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: If he removed</b> the hair on all the edges of his beard in one action, he is liable to receive only one set of lashes. The Gemara clarifies: <b>He maintains</b> that the prohibition of marring the edges of the beard <b>is a single prohibition,</b> and therefore, one is not liable for marring each edge.
The mishna teaches: <b>And one is liable</b> for marring the edges of his beard <b>only if he removes</b> the hair <b>with a razor. The Sages taught</b> a halakhic midrash: The verse states: <b>“And the edge of their beard they may not shave”</b> (Leviticus 21:5). One <b>might</b> have thought that for any manner of shaving, <b>even if he shaved</b> the beard <b>with scissors, he would be liable;</b> therefore, <b>the verse states: “You may not mar</b> the edge of your beard” (Leviticus 19:27), indicating that one is liable only for shaving that destroys the hair from the root, which is not the case with scissors. <b>If</b> the verse had stated only: <b>“You may not mar,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that even <b>if he removed</b> the hair <b>with tweezers or with a plane</b> he <b>would be liable</b> to receive lashes, as they destroy the hair from its roots; therefore, <b>the verse states: “They may not shave,”</b> indicating that only hair removal by means of shaving is prohibited, and that is not accomplished with a tweezers and a plane.
<b>How so?</b> Based on these two verses, for what form of hair removal is one liable? One is liable only for <b>shaving that involves marring. You must say</b> that <b>this</b> is shaving with <b>a razor.</b>
The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: Even</b> if <b>he removed</b> the hair <b>with tweezers or with a plane, he would be liable</b> to receive lashes. The Gemara objects: <b>Whichever way you</b> look at it, it is difficult. <b>If he derives</b> the <i>halakha</i> by means of <b>a verbal analogy</b> that he received from his teachers between the <i>halakhot</i> of shaving written with regard to priests (Leviticus 21:5) and the <i>halakhot</i> of marring written with regard to non-priests (Leviticus 19:27), <b>let</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>require</b> shaving with <b>a razor</b> in order to render him liable, as did the Rabbis. <b>If he does not derive</b> the <i>halakha</i> by means of <b>a verbal analogy,</b> he should <b>also not</b> exempt one who removes the hair with <b>scissors,</b> as that should be included in the category of shaving.
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, he derives</b> the <i>halakha</i> by means of <b>a verbal analogy, and he holds</b> that <b>these too,</b> the tweezers and the plane, <b>accomplish shaving.</b> His dispute with the Rabbis does not relate to the derivation of the <i>halakha</i>; rather it is a dispute with regard to the definition of shaving.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who imprints a tattoo,</b> by inserting a dye into recesses carved in the skin, is also liable to receive lashes. If <b>one imprinted</b> on the skin with a dye <b>but did not carve</b> the skin, or if <b>one carved</b> the skin <b>but did not imprint</b> the tattoo by adding a dye, <b>he is not liable;</b> he is not liable <b>until he imprints and carves</b> the skin, <b>with ink, or with kohl [<i>keḥol</i>], or with any substance that marks. Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon: He is liable only if he writes the name there, as it is stated: “And a tattoo inscription you shall not place upon you, I am the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 19:28).
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, said to Rav Ashi:</b> Is Rabbi Shimon saying that one is liable only <b>if he actually inscribes</b> the words <b>“I am the Lord”</b> in his skin? Rav Ashi <b>said to him: No,</b> he is saying <b>as bar Kappara teaches: One is liable only if he inscribes a name of</b> an object of <b>idol worship, as it is stated: “And a tattoo inscription you shall not place upon you, I am the Lord,”</b> which means: Do not place an idolatrous name on your skin, as <b>I am the Lord, and no</b> one <b>else.</b>
<b>Rav Malkiyya says</b> that <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava says: It is prohibited for a person to place burnt ashes on his wound</b> to promote healing, <b>because it looks like a tattoo.</b> Since few statements were attributed to Rav Malkiyya, and there was another Sage named Rav Malkiyyu who also cited statements in the name of Rav Adda bar Ahava, the Gemara provides a mnemonic for these <i>halakhot</i>. <b>Rav Naḥman, son of Rav Ika, says:</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>skewer, maidservants, and follicles</b> were stated by <b>Rav Malkiyyu.</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>forelock, burnt ashes, and cheese</b> were stated by <b>Rav Malkiyya.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa says:</b> The distinction is a different one. Those statements that relate to <b>a mishna or a <i>baraita</i></b> were stated by <b>Rav Malkiyya.</b> Independent <b><i>halakhot</i></b> were taught by <b>Rav Malkiyyu. And your mnemonic,</b> to remember that distinction, is: <b>The Mishna is queen [<i>malketa</i>],</b> indicating the connection between Rav Malkiyya and the Mishna. The Gemara asks: <b>What is</b> the practical difference <b>between</b> the criteria of Rav Naḥman and Rav Pappa in this regard? The Gemara answers: The practical difference <b>between them</b> is with regard to the <i>halakha</i> of <b>maidservants,</b> which appears in a mishna. According to Rav Pappa, this <i>halakha</i> was stated by Rav Malkiyya, not Rav Malkiyyu.
The Gemara addresses the matter of placing burnt ashes on a wound. The Gemara relates: <b>Rav Beivai bar Abaye was fastidious</b> and did not place ashes <b>even on the wound of bloodletting [<i>arivda dekhusilta</i>],</b> as that too appears like a tattoo. <b>Rav Ashi says: Any place where there is a wound, his wound proves about itself</b>  that the person’s intent when he covers it with ashes is to promote healing, and it is not a tattoo.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>A nazirite who was drinking wine all day is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If onlookers <b>said to him: Do not drink, do not drink,</b> forewarning him several times, <b>and he drinks</b> after each forewarning, he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for each and every</b> drink. If the nazirite <b>was rendering himself impure</b> through exposure <b>to corpses all day, he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If <b>they said to him: Do not render yourself impure, do not render yourself impure, and he renders himself impure</b> after each forewarning, he is <b>liable for each and every</b> incident. If the nazirite <b>was shaving</b> his hair <b>all day, he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If <b>they said to him: Do not shave, do not shave, and</b> after each forewarning <b>he shaves,</b> he is <b>liable for each and every</b> time he shaves.
If a person <b>was wearing</b> a garment consisting of <b>diverse kinds</b> of wool and linen <b>all day, he is liable</b> to receive <b>only one</b> set of lashes. If <b>they said to him: Do not wear</b> it, <b>do not wear</b> it, <b>and he removes</b> it <b>and dons</b> it after each forewarning, he is <b>liable for each and every</b> time that he dons the garment.

Daf 21b

Apropos the case where one receives several sets of lashes for performing a single action, the mishna continues: <b>There is</b> one who <b>plows a single furrow and is liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for</b> violating <b>eight prohibitions.</b> How so? For <b>plowing with an ox and a donkey,</b> in violation of the prohibition: “You shall not plow with an ox and a donkey together” (Deuteronomy 22:10); <b>and they are consecrated,</b> and therefore he is guilty of misuse of consecrated property; <b>and</b> he is plowing <b>diverse kinds in a vineyard; and</b> it is <b>during the Sabbatical</b> Year, when it is prohibited to work the land; <b>and</b> it is on <b>a Festival,</b> when plowing is a prohibited labor; <b>and</b> he is both <b>a priest and a nazirite</b> and is performing the plowing <b>in a place of impurity</b> imparted by a corpse, which is prohibited for both a priest (see Leviticus 21:1) and a nazirite (see Numbers 6:6).
<b>Ḥananya ben Ḥakhinai says:</b> If he <b>was wearing</b> a garment consisting of <b>diverse kinds</b> of wool and linen while plowing he is <b>also</b> flogged for violating that prohibition. The Sages <b>said to him:</b> That is <b>not</b> a prohibition in <b>the</b> same <b>category</b> as the others, as it is not connected to the act of plowing. Ḥananya ben Ḥakhinai <b>said to them:</b> According to that criterion, the fact that he is <b>a nazirite is also not</b> in <b>the</b> same <b>category,</b> as a nazirite and a priest are not flogged for plowing; rather, they are flogged for contracting impurity imparted by a corpse.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Beivai says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yosei says</b> that when the mishna teaches with regard to wearing a garment of diverse kinds of wool and linen: And <b>he removes</b> it <b>and dons</b> it after each forewarning, does it mean that one is liable for each forewarning only if he <b>actually</b> removes and <b>dons</b> it, <b>or</b> perhaps one is liable <b>even</b> if <b>he inserts and removes</b> his arm from <b>the sleeve of his garment [<i>unkali</i>];</b> perhaps this is also considered removing and donning the garment? The Gemara relates: <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Ika, gestured:</b> One is liable only for actually <b>inserting</b> his body in the garment entirely <b>and</b> then <b>removing</b> it. <b>Rav Ashi says:</b> The reference in the mishna is not to actually removing and donning the garment; rather, <b>even if he only waited</b> an interval <b>equivalent</b> to the period required <b>to remove and to don</b> the garment, he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes for each and every forewarning.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>There is</b> one who <b>plows</b> a single <b>furrow</b> and is liable to receive lashes for violating eight prohibitions. <b>Rabbi Yannai says</b> that when the Sages sat <b>in a group,</b> their opinions <b>were counted and they concluded: One who covers</b> seeds of <b>diverse kinds</b> with dirt <b>is flogged</b> for sowing diverse kinds. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said to them: Isn’t this</b> the <i>halakha</i> in <b>our mishna: There is</b> one who <b>plows a single furrow and is liable</b> to receive lashes <b>for</b> violating eight prohibitions, including: For <b>plowing with an ox and a donkey, and they are consecrated, and</b> he is plowing <b>diverse kinds in a vineyard.</b> With regard to <b>this</b> person who <b>plows who is liable</b> to receive lashes <b>due to</b> violating the prohibition of <b>diverse kinds, how can you find these</b> circumstances? Plowing a field is unrelated to sowing diverse kinds. Is it <b>not</b> a case <b>where one covers</b> the seeds with dirt <b>in the course of</b> his plowing <b>as he proceeds,</b> indicating that one who covers the seeds of diverse kinds is flogged?
Rabbi Yannai <b>said to him:</b> You are correct; but <b>if I had not lifted the earthenware</b> shard <b>for you, would you have found the gem beneath it?</b> It was only after I told you the <i>halakha</i> that you succeeded in finding a source in the mishna. Later, <b>Reish Lakish said to Rabbi Yoḥanan: If</b> it was <b>not</b> for the fact <b>that a great man,</b> Rabbi Yannai, <b>praised your</b> statement, <b>I would say</b> that there is no proof from the mishna, as it is possible to say: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in <b>the mishna? It is</b> that of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who says: One who maintains</b> diverse kinds by performing actions essential for their existence <b>is flogged.</b> Therefore, one who plows and covers seeds of diverse kinds is liable to receive lashes, as he facilitates the existence of the diverse kinds, not because in covering the seeds it is as though he sowed them.
The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the aforementioned statement of <b>Rabbi Akiva?</b> It is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who weeds and one who covers</b> the seeds of <b>diverse kinds</b> with dirt <b>is flogged,</b> as he performed an action that promotes the growth of the diverse kinds, which is tantamount to sowing. <b>Rabbi Akiva says: Even one who maintains</b> diverse kinds violates the prohibition.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Akiva? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that it is written: <b>“Your field you shall not sow with diverse kinds”</b> (Leviticus 19:19), <b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that <b>sowing</b> diverse kinds is prohibited. <b>From where</b> do I derive that <b>maintaining</b> diverse kinds, which does not involve any positive action, is also prohibited? It is as <b>the verse states: “Your animals you shall not breed with different species [<i>kilayim</i>]; your field you shall not sow with diverse kinds [<i>kilayim</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 19:19), which is interpreted as though it is written: Diverse kinds [<i>kilayim</i>] in your field you shall not sow, indicating that one may not allow diverse kinds to remain in his field.
<b>Ulla said to Rav Naḥman: And let him be flogged also for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>sowing on a Festival.</b> Rav Naḥman <b>said to him:</b> Indeed, lashes for that prohibition could have been included in the mishna. The <i>tanna</i> <b>taught</b> certain prohibitions <b>and omitted</b> other prohibitions.
Ulla <b>said to him: The <i>tanna</i> taught</b> and specifically enumerated <b>eight</b> sets of lashes, <b>and you say</b> that <b>he taught</b> some <b>and omitted</b> some? <b>Rava says:</b> The reason that sowing on a Festival was omitted is that <b>there is a division of labors on Shabbat, but there is no division of labors on a Festival.</b> On Shabbat, one who unwittingly performs several prohibited labors during one lapse of awareness is liable to bring one sin-offering for each labor that he performed. On a Festival, if one performs several prohibited labors, he is liable to receive only one set of lashes, as there is one prohibition against performing labor on a Festival. Since the mishna listed plowing on the Festival, it does not also list sowing on the Festival. Ulla <b>said to him: That [<i>ada</i>] is so.</b>
<b>Abaye raised an objection to</b> the opinion of Rava: <b>And is there no division of labors on a Festival? But didn’t we learn</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who cooks</b> a sciatic <b>nerve in milk on a Festival and eats it is flogged</b> with <b>five</b> sets of lashes. How so? <b>He is flogged for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>eating</b> a sciatic <b>nerve</b> (see Genesis 32:33); <b>and he is flogged for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>cooking on a Festival not for</b> the <b>purpose</b> of the Festival, as he is prohibited from eating it; <b>and he is flogged for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>cooking</b> a sciatic <b>nerve,</b> which is meat, <b>in milk; and he is flogged for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>eating meat</b> cooked <b>in milk; and he is flogged</b>

Daf 22a

<b>for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>kindling</b> a fire on a Festival. <b>And if it is so,</b> that there is no division of labors on a Festival, <b>he is not liable for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>kindling</b> a fire, <b>as he is</b> already <b>liable for</b> violating the prohibition of <b>cooking</b> the sciatic nerve on a Festival. Rava said: <b>Remove kindling</b> from the list of five prohibitions for which he is flogged, as he is not liable for violating that prohibition, <b>and insert</b> the prohibition of eating the <b>sciatic nerve of an unslaughtered animal carcass.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t Rabbi Ḥiyya teach</b> a <i>baraita</i> that summarizes the prohibitions listed in this mishna: <b>One is flogged</b> with <b>two</b> sets of lashes <b>for his eating and three</b> sets of lashes <b>for his cooking? And if it is so,</b> that the prohibition of kindling a fire is replaced with the prohibition of eating the sciatic nerve of an unslaughtered animal carcass, <b>he is liable</b> to receive <b>three</b> sets of lashes <b>for his eating;</b> for eating a sciatic nerve, for eating meat cooked in milk, and for eating an unslaughtered animal carcass. <b>Rather,</b> say: <b>Remove kindling</b> from the list of prohibitions, <b>and insert</b> the prohibition of using <b>wood of a tree</b> worshipped as part of idolatrous rites <b>[<i>ashera</i>], whose prohibition is</b> derived <b>from here: “And</b> nothing of the dedicated item <b>shall cleave to your hand”</b> (Deuteronomy 13:18), indicating that it is forbidden to derive benefit from accouterments of idol worship.
<b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, said to Rav Ashi:</b> If the reference is to the wood of an <i>ashera</i>, <b>let him also be flogged for</b> violating the prohibition: <b>“You shall not bring an abomination into your house”</b> (Deuteronomy 7:26), in addition to the other prohibitions enumerated. <b>Rather,</b> say: <b>What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing with a case <b>where one cooked</b> the sciatic nerve <b>with wood consecrated</b> for use in the Temple, <b>whose warning,</b> the source of its prohibition, is <b>from here: “And their <i>asherim</i> you shall burn in fire…you shall not do so to the Lord your God”</b> (Deuteronomy 12:3–4), indicating that one who destroys a consecrated item violates a prohibition.
§ The Gemara provides <b>a mnemonic: <i>Shin</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>beit</i>, <i>alef</i>, <i>yod</i>; <i>shin</i>, <i>nun</i>, <i>zayin</i>;</b> representing the <i>amora’im</i> who suggest additions to the eight prohibitions violated by the person in the mishna who plows the field with diverse kinds: Hoshaya; Ḥananya; Abbahu; Abaye; Ashi; Ravina; Zeira.
<b>Rav Hoshaya objects to this: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate</b> in his list the prohibition of <b>sowing in a forceful stream,</b> the land where a heifer’s neck is broken by the Elders of the city closest to the corpse of a murder victim whose murderer is unknown, <b>and its prohibition is from here:</b> “And the Elders of that city shall bring the heifer down to a forceful stream, <b>which will be neither plowed nor sown”</b> (Deuteronomy 21:4). The verse is written in the future tense as a prohibition for the future: It is prohibited to work the land there after the heifer’s neck is broken.
<b>Rav Ḥananya objects to this: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate one who erases the name</b> of God <b>in</b> the course of <b>his walking</b> and plowing, <b>and its prohibition is from here: “And you shall destroy their names…you shall not do so to the Lord your God”</b> (Deuteronomy 12:3–4).
<b>Rabbi Abbahu objects to this: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate one who severs his snow-white leprous mark</b> in the course of his plowing, <b>and its prohibition is from here: “Take heed of the plague of leprosy”</b> (Deuteronomy 24:8), indicating the prohibition against severing the mark.
<b>Abaye objects to this: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate one who loosens the breastplate from upon the ephod</b> in the course of plowing, <b>or one who removes the staves of the Ark</b> of the Covenant. <b>And its prohibition is from here:</b> “The staves shall be in the rings of the Ark; <b>they shall not be removed</b> from it” (Exodus 25:15), while the relevant verse with regard to the breastplate is: <b>“And the breastplate shall not be loosened</b> from the ephod” (Exodus 28:28).
<b>Rav Ashi objects to this: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate one who plows with</b> a plow crafted from the <b>wood of an <i>ashera</i>, and its prohibition is from here: “And there shall cleave nothing</b> of the dedicated item <b>to your hand”</b> (Deuteronomy 13:18).
<b>Ravina objects to this: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate one who chops down beautiful</b> fruit <b>trees</b> in the course of plowing, <b>and its prohibition is from here: “For you may eat of it, and you shall not chop it down”</b> (Deuteronomy 20:19).
<b>Rabbi Zeira said to Rabbi Mani: And let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>also enumerate</b> a case <b>where one says:</b> On my <b>oath I will not plow on the Festival,</b> and then proceeds to violate his oath. Rabbi Mani said: <b>There, the oath does not take effect,</b> as <b>he is already under oath from Mount Sinai</b> not to plow on a Festival, and an oath does not take effect when another oath is already in effect. Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him:</b> The oath can take effect in a case <b>where one says:</b> On my <b>oath I will not plow whether during the week or on a Festival, as</b> in that case, <b>since</b> the <b>oath takes effect in his</b> regard <b>during the week, it takes effect in his</b> regard <b>on a Festival as well.</b> Rabbi Mani replied: The mishna did not include that prohibition because the <i>tanna</i> <b>is not teaching a matter that is in</b> the category of those matters subject to dissolution by means of posing <b>a request</b> to a Torah scholar. As oaths fall into that category, this case is not enumerated in the mishna.
Rabbi Zeira asked: <b>And</b> did the <i>tanna</i> <b>not</b> teach matters subject to dissolution by means of a Torah scholar? <b>But isn’t there</b> the matter of <b>consecrated</b> animals, whose sanctity can be repealed by means of dissolution of the vow by a Torah scholar, which was included in the mishna? Rabbi Mani answered: The <i>tanna</i> is referring <b>to a firstborn</b> animal, which is consecrated from the womb. Since it was not consecrated by means of a vow, the sanctity cannot be dissolved by a Torah scholar. Rabbi Zeira asked: <b>But isn’t there</b> the matter of the impurity of <b>a nazirite,</b> whose vow of naziriteship can be dissolved by a Torah scholar, which was included in the mishna? Rabbi Mani answered: The reference in the mishna is <b>to</b> one who is <b>a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson,</b> for whom there is no dissolution.
Rabbi Zeira asked: <b>Is a nazirite</b> like <b>Samson subject</b> to the prohibition of <b>contracting impurity</b> imparted <b>by corpses?</b> He is not. As the <i>tanna</i> enumerated the prohibition of a nazirite becoming impure with impurity imparted by a corpse, clearly he is not a nazirite like Samson. <b>Rather,</b> the <i>tanna</i> does not enumerate the case of one who violated an oath not to plow both during the week and on a Festival, because <b>this <i>tanna</i> is not of</b> the opinion that a more <b>inclusive prohibition</b> takes effect when the standard prohibition does not take effect. Just as with regard to one who takes an oath not to perform labor on a Festival, the oath does not take effect, so too, even if he adds to it an oath not to plow during the week, it does not take effect, because he is already under oath from Sinai not to plow on a Festival.
<b>Rabbi Hoshaya says: One who breeds a disqualified consecrated ox</b> with a female even of the same species <b>is flogged</b> with <b>two</b> sets of lashes, one for labor with a disqualified consecrated animal, and one for breeding two animals of diverse kinds. Even after the animal is desacralized through redemption, it remains prohibited to perform labor with it. It is considered diverse kinds because the Torah accorded disqualified consecrated animals the status of two animals, one consecrated and one non-sacred. If one breeds such an animal with an ox, it is as though he bred it with an animal of a different species, thereby violating the prohibition. Likewise, <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says: One who drives a disqualified consecrated ox</b> to plow the field <b>is flogged, as it is one body, and the verse accorded it</b> the status of <b>two bodies.</b> One who plows with two different species of animals together is liable to receive lashes.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With <b>how many</b> lashes <b>does one flog</b> a person sentenced to receive lashes? One flogs him with <b>forty</b> lashes <b>less one, as it is stated:</b> “And he shall strike him before him, in accordance with his wickedness, <b>by number. Forty</b> he shall strike him, he shall not add” (Deuteronomy 25:2–3). The mishna joins the end of the first verse and the beginning of the second, forming the phrase: “By number, forty,” which is interpreted as: <b>A sum adjacent to forty. Rabbi Yehuda says: He is flogged</b> with <b>a full forty</b> lashes. <b>And where is he flogged the extra</b> lash? As the mishna proceeds to explain, the thirty-nine lashes are divided into three and administered in three places on the body of the person being flogged; according to Rabbi Yehuda there is one lash that remains. That lash is administered <b>between his shoulders.</b>
<b>One assesses</b> the number of lashes that the one being punished is capable of withstanding <b>only with</b> a number of <b>lashes fit to be divided into three</b> equal groups. If the assessment was that he can survive twenty lashes, he is flogged with eighteen. Likewise, if doctors <b>assessed</b> concerning <b>him</b> that he is able <b>to receive forty</b> lashes and survive, <b>and he is</b> then <b>flogged some</b> of those forty lashes,

Daf 22b

<b>and</b> then <b>they assessed</b> him again and concluded <b>that he cannot receive forty</b> lashes and survive, he is <b>exempt</b> from the additional lashes. If the doctors initially <b>assessed</b> concerning <b>him</b> that he is able <b>to receive</b> only <b>eighteen</b> lashes, <b>and once he was flogged</b> eighteen times <b>they assessed that he is able to receive forty,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from receiving additional lashes.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara begins with a discussion of the number of lashes. <b>What is the reason</b> that the Rabbis said that he receives forty lashes less one? <b>If it had been written: Forty by number, I would say</b> that it means <b>forty as</b> a precise <b>sum; now that it is written: “By number, forty,”</b> the reference is to <b>a sum that approaches forty.</b> Likewise, <b>Rava said: How foolish</b> are <b>the rest of the people who stand before a Torah scroll</b> that passes before them, <b>and</b> yet <b>they do not stand before a great man,</b> when a Sage passes before them; <b>as in a Torah scroll, forty is written and the Sages came</b> and <b>subtracted one,</b> establishing the number of lashes as thirty-nine. Apparently, the authority of the Sages is so great that they are able to amend an explicit Torah verse.
The mishna teaches: <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> He is flogged with <b>a full forty</b> lashes, with the additional lash administered between his shoulders. <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says: What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda?</b> It is <b>as it is written: “And one shall say to him: What are these wounds between your arms? Then he shall answer: Those with which I was wounded in the house of my friends”</b> (Zechariah 13:6). Rabbi Yehuda understands that this verse is referring to one with wounds from lashes administered between his arms, indicating that there is one lash administered between the shoulders. <b>And</b> how do <b>the Rabbis,</b> who hold that one is flogged only thirty-nine lashes, explain this verse? They explain that <b>this</b> verse <b>is written with regard to schoolchildren</b> struck by their teacher for laxity in their studies, and is not referring to lashes administered by the court.
The mishna teaches: <b>One assesses</b> the number of lashes that the one being punished is capable of withstanding <b>only with</b> a number of <b>lashes fit</b> to be divided into three equal groups. If doctors assessed concerning him that he is able to receive forty lashes and survive, and he is then flogged some of those forty lashes, and they then assessed him again and concluded that he cannot receive forty lashes and survive, he is exempt from any additional lashes. If the doctors initially assessed concerning him that he is able to receive only eighteen lashes, and once he was flogged with eighteen lashes they assessed that he is able to receive forty, he is exempt from receiving further lashes. The Gemara infers: If <b>he was flogged</b> in practice, <b>yes,</b> he is exempt; if <b>he was not flogged, no,</b> he is not exempt from the rest of the forty lashes.
<b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: If doctors <b>assessed</b> concerning <b>him</b> that he is able <b>to receive forty</b> lashes and survive, <b>and they then assessed</b> him again and concluded <b>that he cannot receive forty</b> lashes and survive, he is <b>exempt.</b> If the doctors initially <b>assessed</b> concerning <b>him</b> that he is able <b>to receive</b> only <b>eighteen</b> lashes, <b>and they then assessed that he is able to receive forty,</b> he is <b>exempt.</b> Apparently, even if he did not receive any lashes, if the assessment changes, it is as though he was flogged.
<b>Rav Sheshet said:</b> This is <b>not difficult,</b> as <b>this</b> case in the mishna is one <b>where</b> doctors <b>assessed his</b> fitness to receive lashes <b>for</b> that <b>day,</b> and there was no change in his condition; rather, it was discovered that the initial assessment was mistaken. He is exempt only if he was already flogged; if not, another assessment is performed. <b>That</b> case in the <i>baraita</i> is one <b>where</b> doctors <b>assess his</b> fitness to receive lashes <b>for</b> the <b>next day or for a different day.</b> In that case, the initial assessment was accurate; it is his condition that changed. Therefore, if it is determined that he is unable to receive lashes, he is exempt.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <b>one performed a transgression that involves two prohibitions,</b> and <b>they assessed</b> concerning <b>him a single assessment</b> of the number of lashes that he could withstand in punishment for both transgressions, <b>he is flogged</b> in accordance with their assessment <b>and</b> is <b>exempt</b> from any additional lashes. <b>And if not,</b> if he was assessed with regard to the lashes that he could withstand for one transgression, <b>he is flogged and</b> is allowed to <b>heal, and then is flogged</b> again for violating the second prohibition.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The case in the mishna is one where there is one assessment performed for two sets of lashes. The Gemara asks: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One does not perform one assessment for two prohibitions?</b>
<b>Rav Sheshet said:</b> This is <b>not difficult; this</b> ruling in the <i>baraita</i> that one does not perform a single assessment for two prohibitions is in a case <b>where</b> doctors <b>assessed</b> concerning <b>him</b> that he is able <b>to</b> receive <b>forty-one</b> lashes, two lashes beyond a full set. Since those two additional lashes are not divisible by three, which is a requirement based on the previous mishna, he receives only thirty-nine lashes. That constitutes just one set of lashes. He remains liable to receive another set of lashes after he recovers, requiring another assessment and another set of lashes. <b>That</b> ruling in the mishna that one performs a single assessment for two prohibitions is in a case <b>where</b> doctors <b>assessed</b> concerning <b>him</b> that he is able <b>to</b> receive <b>forty-two</b> lashes. In that case, it is possible to ascribe thirty-nine lashes to one prohibition and three additional lashes to the second prohibition. That is tantamount to two separate assessments, although in practice only one assessment was performed.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>How do they flog him? He ties</b> the <b>two hands</b> of the person being flogged <b>on this</b> side <b>and that</b> side <b>of a post, and the attendant of the congregation takes hold of his garments</b> to remove them. <b>If they were ripped</b> in the process, <b>they were ripped, and if they were unraveled, they were unraveled,</b> and he continues <b>until he bares his chest. And the stone</b> upon which the attendant stands when flogging <b>is situated behind</b> the person being flogged. <b>The attendant of the congregation stands on it with a strap in his hand.</b> It is a strap <b>of calf</b> hide, and is <b>doubled, one into two, and two into four, and two straps of donkey</b> hide <b>go up and down</b> the doubled strap of calf hide. The length of <b>its handle</b> is <b>one handbreadth,</b> and the <b>width</b> of the straps is <b>one handbreadth, and</b> the strap must be long enough so that <b>its end reaches the top of his abdomen,</b> i.e., his navel, when he is flogged from behind.
<b>And</b> the attendant <b>flogs him</b> with <b>one-third</b> of the lashes <b>from the front of him,</b> on his chest, <b>and two</b> one-third <b>portions from behind him,</b> on his back. <b>And he does not flog him</b> when the one receiving lashes is <b>standing, nor</b> when he is <b>sitting; rather,</b> he flogs him when he is <b>hunched, as it is stated: “And the judge shall cause him to lie down,</b> and strike him” (Deuteronomy 25:2), which indicates that the one receiving lashes must be in a position that approximates lying down.
<b>And the</b> attendant <b>flogging</b> the one receiving lashes <b>flogs [<i>makeh</i>] him with one hand with all his strength, and the</b> court <b>crier recites</b> the verses: <b>“If you do not observe to perform</b> all the words of this law that are written in this book, that you may fear this glorious and awesome name, the Lord your God. <b>And the Lord will make your plagues [<i>makkotekha</i>] outstanding, and the plagues</b> of your descendants, and even great plagues, and of long continuance, and severe sicknesses, and of long continuance” (Deuteronomy 28:58–59). <b>And</b> then <b>he returns to the beginning of the verse.</b> He also recites: <b>“And you shall observe the matters of this covenant,</b> and do them, that you may make all that you do to prosper” (Deuteronomy 29:8), <b>and concludes</b> with the verse: <b>“And He is merciful and shall atone for transgression, and destroys not;</b> and many a time does He turn His anger away, and does not stir up all His wrath” (Psalms 78:38), <b>and</b> then <b>returns to the beginning of the verse</b> that starts: “If you do not observe to perform.”
<b>If</b> the one being flogged <b>dies at</b> the <b>hand</b> of the attendant, the latter is <b>exempt,</b> because he acted at the directive of the court. If the attendant <b>added for him an additional</b> lash with <b>a strap and he died,</b> the attendant <b>is exiled</b> to a city of refuge <b>on his account,</b> as an unwitting murderer. <b>If</b> the one being flogged involuntarily <b>sullies himself,</b> due to fear or pain, <b>whether with excrement or with urine,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from further lashes. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says</b> that the threshold of shame for men and women is different: <b>The man</b> is exempted if he sullies himself <b>with excrement, and the woman</b> is exempted even <b>with urine.</b>

Daf 23a

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that the attendant rips the garments of the person about to be flogged. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for this? It is <b>due to</b> the verse: “Forty he shall flog him…and your brother shall be <b>debased</b> before you” (Deuteronomy 25:3), as tearing his garments debases him.
<b>Rav Sheshet says in the name of Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya: From where</b> is it derived <b>with regard to the strap</b> used for flogging <b>that it is</b> a strap from the hide <b>of a calf?</b> It is <b>as it is written: “Forty he shall flog him,” and juxtaposed to it</b> is written: <b>“You shall not muzzle an ox in its threshing”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:4), indicating that the strap is from the hide of an ox.
<b>And Rav Sheshet says in the name of Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya: From where</b> is it derived <b>with regard to a <i>yevama</i> who happened before</b> a <i>yavam</i> <b>afflicted with boils, that one does not compel her</b> to enter into that levirate marriage? It is derived from a verse, <b>as it is written: “You shall not muzzle an ox in its threshing,” and juxtaposed to it</b> is written: <b>“When brothers dwell together”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:5), which is the passage dealing with levirate marriage. The <i>yevama</i> is not muzzled, as it were, when she states that she does not want to enter into levirate marriage with him.
<b>And Rav Sheshet says in the name of Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya:</b> Concerning <b>anyone who treats the Festivals with contempt, it is as though</b> he is <b>worshipping idols, as it is written: “Molten gods you shall not make for yourself”</b> (Exodus 34:17), <b>and juxtaposed to it</b> is written: <b>“The festival of Passover you shall observe”</b> (Exodus 34:18).
<b>And Rav Sheshet says in the name of Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya:</b> Concerning <b>anyone who speaks malicious speech, and any-one who accepts malicious speech</b> as the truth, <b>and anyone who testifies a false testimony, it is fit to throw him to the dogs, as it is written: “To the dog you shall cast it”</b> (Exodus 22:30), <b>and juxtaposed to it</b> is written: <b>“You shall not accept [<i>lo tissa</i>] a false report;</b> do not join with the wicked to be a false witness” (Exodus 23:1). In addition to prohibitions against false testimony and against accepting malicious speech, Rav Sheshet <b>also reads into</b> the verse the meaning: <b>You shall not relate [<i>lo tassi</i>]</b> a false report.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And two straps</b> go up and down the doubled strap of calf hide. The Sage <b>taught: And</b> they are straps <b>of donkey</b> hide. <b>As a certain Galilean interpreted before Rav Ḥisda:</b> It is written: <b>“The ox knows its owner, and the donkey its master’s trough; but Israel does not know,</b> My people does not consider” (Isaiah 1:3). <b>The Holy One, Blessed be He, says: Let the one who recognizes its master’s trough,</b> an ox and donkey, <b>come and exact retribution,</b> through lashes with a strap of ox and donkey hide, <b>from one who does not recognize his Master’s trough</b> and performs transgressions.
The mishna teaches: The length of <b>its handle</b> is <b>one handbreadth,</b> and the width of the straps is one handbreadth, and the strap must be long enough so that its end reaches the top of his abdomen. <b>Abaye said: Conclude from it</b> that <b>for each and every one, we craft</b> the strap <b>according to</b> the size of <b>their back. Rava said to him: If so, there will be numerous straps</b> in court <b>for them. Rather, Rava said: It has loops; when</b> the attendant <b>wants, he ties</b> the loops to shorten the strap, and <b>when</b> the attendant <b>wants, he loosens</b> the loops to lengthen the strap. The length of the strap can be adjusted to correspond to the height of the person being flogged.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>One flogs him</b> with one-third of the lashes from the front of him and two one-third portions from behind him. The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? <b>Rav Kahana said:</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as the verse states: “And the judge shall cause him to lie down, and strike him before him in accordance with his wickedness, by number”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), indicating that the attendant strikes him in accordance with <b>one</b> portion of <b>wickedness from the front of him,</b> and <b>two</b> portions of <b>wickedness from behind him.</b>
The mishna teaches: <b>And he does not flog him</b> when the one receiving lashes is standing, nor when he is sitting; rather, he flogs him when he is hunched, as it is stated: “And the judge shall cause him to lie down.” <b>Rav Ḥisda says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: From where</b> is it derived with regard <b>to the strap that it is doubled?</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as it is stated: And he shall cause him to lie down [<i>vehippilo</i>],</b> which is interpreted based on the similar Aramaic root <i>ayin</i>, <i>peh</i>, <i>peh</i>, meaning double. The Gemara asks: <b>But doesn’t he require</b> that verse <b>for</b> the fundamental <i>halakha</i> <b>itself,</b> as the mishna teaches: He flogs him when he is hunched. The Gemara answers: <b>If so, let the verse write: Shall bend him. What</b> is the meaning of: <b>“Shall cause him to lie down”? Conclude two</b> conclusions <b>from it:</b> The <i>halakha</i> that the person being flogged must be hunched, and the allusion to the doubling of the strap.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And the</b> attendant <b>flogging</b> the one receiving lashes <b>flogs</b> him <b>with</b> one <b>hand</b> with all his strength. <b>The Sages taught:</b> For the administration of lashes, the court <b>appoints only attendants</b> who are <b>lacking in strength and</b> are <b>exceedingly knowledgeable</b> in Torah. <b>Rabbi Yehuda says:</b> The court may appoint <b>even</b> those who are <b>lacking in knowledge and</b> are <b>exceedingly strong.</b>
<b>Rava said: It is reasonable</b> to rule <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda, as it is written:</b> “Forty he shall flog him; <b>he shall not exceed, lest he continue</b> to beat him” (Deuteronomy 25:3). He explains: <b>Granted, if you say</b> that even people <b>lacking in</b> halakhic <b>knowledge</b> are appointed, <b>that is</b> why <b>it is necessary to warn</b> him not to add lashes. <b>But if you say</b> only people who are <b>exceedingly knowledgeable</b> are appointed, <b>does</b> the Torah <b>need to warn</b> the attendant? Apparently, even a person lacking in knowledge can be appointed as an attendant. <b>And</b> according to <b>the Rabbis,</b> that is no proof, as there is an expression that <b>one implores only one who is</b> already <b>implored.</b> In other words, only one who is already cognizant of a <i>halakha</i> can be effectively warned to observe it.
It is <b>taught: When</b> the attendant <b>raises</b> the strap to administer the lashes, <b>he raises</b> it <b>with both hands, and when he flogs</b> the one receiving lashes, <b>he flogs with one hand, so that</b> the lashes <b>will come from him</b> in a deliberate manner.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And the</b> court <b>crier recites</b> the verse beginning: “If you do not observe to perform,” as well as other verses. <b>The Sages taught: The most prominent of the judges recites</b> the verses, <b>the second</b> most prominent judge <b>counts</b> the lashes, <b>and the third</b> most prominent <b>says</b> to the attendant: <b>Strike him. When the lashes are numerous,</b> the one reciting the verses <b>extends</b> his recitation; <b>when the lashes are few,</b> he <b>curtails</b> his recitation by reciting it faster. In both cases, he does so to coordinate the recitation with the duration of the lashes. The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in the mishna: And then <b>he returns to the beginning of the</b> first <b>verse,</b> indicating that one could read the passage several times? The Gemara answers: The <b>mitzva is to precisely</b> coordinate recitation of the verses with the flogging, <b>and if he did not precisely</b> coordinate between them, and he completed the recitation of the verses before completing the lashes, <b>he returns to the beginning of the</b> first <b>verse.</b>
The Gemara cites another <i>baraita</i> with regard to the number of lashes. <b>The Sages taught:</b> From the verse: “He shall not exceed, lest he continue to beat him beyond these, <b>a great flogging”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:3), <b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> a prohibition with regard to <b>a great flogging; from where</b> do I derive that even <b>a minimal</b> excessive <b>flogging</b> is prohibited? I derive it from <b>the verse</b> that <b>states: “He shall not exceed,”</b> at all. The Gemara asks: If so, <b>why</b> must <b>the verse state: “A great flogging”?</b> This <b>teaches that the initial</b> lashes must be administered <b>as a great flogging,</b> with all of the attendant’s strength.
§ The mishna teaches: If the one being flogged <b>sullies himself,</b> with excrement or urine, he is exempt from further lashes. Rabbi Yehuda says: The man is exempted with excrement, and the woman is exempted even with urine. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: For <b>both a man and a woman,</b> they are exempted if they sully themselves <b>with excrement, but not</b> if they do so <b>with urine;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yehuda says: The man with excrement, and the woman</b> even <b>with urine. And the Rabbis say: Both a man and a woman</b> are exempt from further lashes <b>whether</b> they sullied themselves <b>with excrement or with urine.</b>
The Gemara asks with regard to the opinion attributed to Rabbi Yehuda: <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: Both a man and a woman</b> are exempted <b>with excrement,</b> indicating that Rabbi Yehuda holds that a woman is not exempted with urine. <b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said:</b> There is no contradiction; that <i>baraita</i> is merely stating that according to Rabbi Yehuda <b>both are equal with regard to excrement.</b> That does not mean that Rabbi Yehuda holds that a woman is not exempted with urine.
<b>Shmuel says:</b> If <b>they bound him</b> to be flogged <b>and he fled from</b> the <b>court,</b> he is <b>exempt. The Gemara raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: If he <b>was debased</b> with excrement, <b>whether during</b> the <b>first</b> lash <b>or during</b> the <b>second</b> lash, the court <b>exempts him.</b> But in a case where the <b>strap was severed</b> during the course of the flogging, if this occurred <b>during</b> the <b>second</b> lash <b>they exempt him,</b> but if it happened <b>during</b> the <b>first</b> lash, <b>they do not exempt him. Why</b> is he not exempted during the first lash? <b>Let</b> his status <b>be like one who fled</b> from the court before the flogging began, in which case he is exempt. The Gemara answers: <b>There,</b> in that case, <b>he fled</b> from the court and he is not compelled to return; <b>here, he did not flee,</b> and therefore he is not exempted without being flogged.
<b>The Sages taught:</b> If <b>they assessed</b> concerning <b>him that when he is flogged he</b> will be <b>debased</b> with excrement, <b>they exempt him,</b> as the court does not administer a punishment that will lead to debasing the one being flogged beyond the shame generated by the lashes themselves. But if they assessed concerning him <b>that</b> it is only <b>when he will leave the court</b> that <b>he</b> will be <b>debased</b> with excrement, <b>they flog him. Moreover, even if he was debased initially,</b> before any lashes were administered, <b>they</b> nevertheless <b>flog him, as it is stated: “And strike him…and</b> your brother <b>shall be debased”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2–3), indicating that the reference is to one debased as a result of the lashes, <b>and not</b> to <b>one who was already debased in court</b> prior to being flogged.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>All those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i> who were flogged are exempted from their</b> punishment of <b><i>karet</i>, as it is stated: “And your brother shall be debased before your eyes”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:3), indicating: <b>Once he is flogged he is as your brother,</b> as his sin has been atoned and he is no longer excised from the Jewish people; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel says: And if</b> for <b>one who performs one transgression his soul is taken for it,</b> as one’s soul can be uprooted from the world for one transgression, for <b>one who performs a single mitzva,</b> it is <b>all the more so</b> the case <b>that his soul will be given to him,</b> as the reward for performing mitzvot is greater than the punishment for performing transgressions. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: It is derived from its</b> own <b>place</b> in the Torah, <b>as it is stated</b> at the conclusion of the passage discussing intercourse with forbidden relatives, which is punishable with <i>karet</i>: <b>“And the souls that perform</b> them <b>shall be excised”</b> (Leviticus 18:29), <b>and it states</b> toward the beginning of that chapter:

Daf 23b

<b>“That a person shall perform and live by them”</b> (Leviticus 18:5). It is inferred <b>that</b> with regard to <b>one who sits and did not perform a transgression,</b> God <b>gives him a reward like</b> that received by one who <b>performs a mitzva.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon bar Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that as the verse <b>states: “Only be steadfast to not eat the blood, as the blood is the soul”</b> (Deuteronomy 12:23), it can be derived <i>a fortiori</i>: <b>And if</b> with regard to <b>the blood, which a person’s soul loathes, one who abstains from its</b> consumption <b>receives a reward</b> for that action, as it is written in a subsequent verse: “You shall not eat it, so that it shall be good for you and for your children after you” (Deuteronomy 12:25); then concerning <b>robbery and</b> intercourse with <b>forbidden relatives, which a person’s soul desires and covets, one who abstains from their</b> performance and overcomes his inclination, <b>all the more so that he and his descendants and the descendants of his descendants until the end of all generations will merit</b> a reward.
<b>Rabbi Ḥananya ben Akashya says: The Holy One, Blessed be He, sought to confer merit upon the Jewish people; therefore, He increased for them Torah and mitzvot,</b> as each mitzva increases merit, <b>as it is stated: “It pleased the Lord for the sake of His righteousness to make the Torah great and glorious”</b> (Isaiah 42:21). God sought to make the Torah great and glorious by means of the proliferation of mitzvot.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel’s colleagues are in disagreement with him</b> and hold that lashes do not exempt the sinner from <i>karet</i>. <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava said</b> that this is so, as <b>they say</b> in <b>the school of Rav</b> that <b>we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Megilla</i> 7b): <b>The difference between Shabbat and Yom Kippur</b> with regard to the labor prohibited on those days <b>is only that</b> in <b>this</b> case, Shabbat, <b>its intentional</b> desecration is punishable <b>by human hands,</b> as he is stoned by a court based on the testimony of witnesses who forewarned the transgressor, <b>and</b> in <b>that</b> case, Yom Kippur, <b>its intentional</b> desecration is punishable at the hand of God, <b>with <i>karet</i>. And if</b> the statement of Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel <b>is so,</b> in both <b>this</b> case, Shabbat, <b>and that</b> case, Yom Kippur, the punishment would be <b>by human hands.</b> Apparently, the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna, the Rabbis, disagrees with Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak says:</b> There is no proof from here that Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel’s colleagues disagree with him, as in accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna taught? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, who says: There are no lashes in</b> cases of <b>those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i>. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says:</b> All <b>those liable</b> to receive <b><i>karet</i></b> in cases of forbidden relations <b>were included in</b> the principle: “For whoever shall commit any of these abominations, even the people who commit them shall be cut off from among their people” (Leviticus 18:29). <b>And why was <i>karet</i></b> in the case of relations with <b>one’s sister excluded</b> from this verse and mentioned independently (Leviticus 20:17)? It is <b>to sentence</b> one who transgresses a prohibition punishable with <i>karet</i> <b>to</b> be punished <b>with <i>karet</i></b> alone, <b>and not with lashes.</b> Other Sages disagree with Rabbi Yitzḥak (see 13b).
<b>Rav Ashi said: Even</b> if <b>you say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Yitzḥak and hold that there are lashes even in cases where there is liability for <i>karet</i>, there is no proof that Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel’s colleagues disagree with him. The mishna can be understood as follows: In <b>this</b> case, Shabbat, the <b>primary</b> punishment for <b>its intentional</b> desecration is <b>by human hands, and</b> in <b>that</b> case, Yom Kippur, the <b>primary</b> punishment for <b>its intentional</b> desecration is <i>karet</i>, which is a punishment <b>at the hand of Heaven.</b> If he was flogged, he is exempt from <i>karet</i>.
<b>Rav Adda bar Ahava says</b> that <b>Rav says: The <i>halakha</i></b> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Ḥananya ben Gamliel,</b> who ruled that lashes exempt the sinner from <i>karet</i>. <b>Rav Yosef said: Who ascended on high and came and said</b> to you that one who is flogged is exempted from <i>karet</i>? That is not dependent upon the decision of an earthly court. <b>Abaye said to</b> Rav Yosef: <b>But</b> according to your reasoning, then with regard to <b>that which Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi says:</b> There are <b>three matters</b> that the <b>earthly court implemented and the heavenly court agreed with them,</b> the same question applies: <b>Who ascended on high and came and said</b> to him that this is so? <b>Rather,</b> in arriving at Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi’s conclusion <b>we</b> homiletically <b>interpret verses. Here too,</b> with regard to lashes and <i>karet</i>, <b>we</b> homiletically <b>interpret verses.</b>
§ With regard to <b>the</b> matter <b>itself, Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi says:</b> There are <b>three matters</b> that the <b>earthly court implemented and the heavenly court agreed with them, and these are they: Reading the Scroll</b> of Esther on Purim, <b>and greeting</b> another <b>with the name of God, and bringing</b> the first <b>tithe</b> to the Temple treasury in Jerusalem. From where is it derived that the heavenly court agreed with them?
<b>Reading the Scroll</b> of Esther is derived from a verse, <b>as it is written: “The Jews confirmed, and they took upon themselves”</b> (Esther 9:27). The verse could have simply said: They took upon themselves. From the formulation of the verse it is interpreted: <b>They confirmed above</b> in Heaven that <b>which they took upon themselves below</b> on earth.
<b>And greeting</b> another with the name of God is derived from a verse, <b>as it is written: “And presently Boaz came from Bethlehem and said to the harvesters: The Lord is with you, and they said to him: May the Lord bless you”</b> (Ruth 2:4). <b>And it states:</b> “And the angel of the Lord appeared to him and said to him: <b>The Lord is with you, mighty man of valor”</b> (Judges 6:12). The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the reason that the Gemara cites the additional source about Gideon, introduced with the phrase: <b>And it states?</b> Why was the proof from Boaz’s statement to the harvesters insufficient? The Gemara explains: <b>And if you would say: It is Boaz who did</b> so <b>on his own, and from Heaven they did not agree with him; come</b> and <b>hear</b> proof, <b>and it says: “The Lord is with you, mighty man of valor.”</b> The angel greeted Gideon with the name of God, indicating that there is agreement in Heaven that this is an acceptable form of greeting.
From where is it derived that the heavenly court agreed to the <b>bringing</b> of the first <b>tithe</b> to the Temple treasury in Jerusalem? It is derived from a verse, <b>as it is written: “Bring you the whole tithe into the storehouse, that there may be food in My house, and try Me now with this, says the Lord of hosts, if I will not open for you the windows of heaven and pour you out a blessing, that there shall be more than sufficiency [<i>ad beli dai</i>]”</b> (Malachi 3:10). This indicates that the heavenly court agreed that the first tithe should be brought to the Temple treasury. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> is the meaning of <b>“<i>ad beli dai</i>”? Rami bar Rav says:</b> It means that the abundance will be so great <b>that your lips will be worn out [<i>yivlu</i>], from saying enough [<i>dai</i>].</b>
